This appraisal report is based on the findings of a mission, which visited
Mexico in June-July 1974 composed of A. Schumacher, C. Carnemark, M. Del Buono,
J.L. Forcina, J.P. Jallade, U. Kuffner, P. McCarthy, B. Mitchell, and P. Scherer
of the Bank, and E. Cifuentes, C. Chisholm, R. Anson and E. Shearer (Consultants).
Messrs. A. Schumacher, B. Thoolen, C. Koch-Weser and M. Del Buono of the Bank
and C. Chisholm and C. Pineo (Consultants) visited Mexico in October and
December 1974, and March 1975 on follow-up missions.

MEXICO
INTEGRATED RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT - PIDER
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
1.        The proposed project is part of a national program intended
to raise the productive capacity of the rural poor.   From 1950 to 1970
Mexico achieved an average 5% annual growth of agricultural production -
the highest in Latin America.   The major contributions to this growth
came from the development of irrigated agriculture and larger farms in
the favorable rainfall areas.   Agricultural investment and services were
largely concentrated on these regions.   As a result, a considerable
number of small farmers, beneficiaries of earlier land reform programs,
still live in rural areas at subsistence income levels - estimated below
US$100 per capita annually. In the past the potential of these small
farmers to develop their holdings was hampered by lack of access to
credit, inputs, extension, all-weather roads and good marketing facilities.
Their situation was further aggravated by the absence of education, health
and other amenities, such as safe supplies of drinking water.
2.        To redress this situation, the Government has organized a program
to alleviate rural poverty. In 1972, the Ministry of the Presidency
developed federal and state administrative machinery PIDER 1/ to integrate
and expand rural development activities among all government agencies
operating in rural areas.   Criteria were established for the selection of
micro-regions and development of micro-region plans encompassing the poorest
rural areas throughout the country 2/.   During 1973 nnd 1974 sueh develop-
ment plans were prepared for 45 micro-regions.
3.        In 1973 and 1974, the Government invested US$133 million in the
initial stages of the development of these 45 micro-regions.   Another
US$450 million would be required for their completion during the 1975-1978
period.   In addition, the Government expects to program US$650 million
for the development of an additional 55 micro-regions.   Some five million
poor rural people would benefit when the 100 micro-region program is
complete.   While the Government would be able to finance a significant
portion of this large program, external finance would be required to carry
out the full program.
1/  Investment Program for Rural Development.
2/ Micro-regions comprise an average of 50,000 poor persons (per capita
incomes averaging below US$100) located in two to seven contiguous
rural municipalities within a state.   They are selected using criteria
that balance poverty levels with potential for income increasing
productive activities (Annex 2).



- L1 -
4.        The Project would help finance programmed expenditures in 30
micro-regions. Each micro-region plan has been closely tailored by PIDER
to address the development bottlenecks paxticular to that region. The
Project would support the completion of reprogrammed investment plans in
15 micro-regions initiated in 1973 and assist development of another
15 micro-regions which were programmed more recently. Total project cost
for these 30 micro-regions is estimated at US$295 million. A loan of
US$110 million would cover 37% of total project cost and would finance
US$47 million of estimated foreign exchange costs and US$63 million of
local currency costs. The Inter-American Development Bank (IDB) would
provide parallel financing for about another 15 micro-regions. The major
part (66%) of the Project would be channeled into productive investments
in livestock, irrigation, fruit production, soil and water conservation,
and  associated  farm  development  credit. To support  this
\ investment, the loan would provide for the development of feeder roads,
markets, extension, electrification,- agricultural research and farmer train-
ing (22%). Investment in social infrastructure (12% of Project) would
include health, primary education and drinking water facilities, and provide
materials for self-help projects. The evaluation component of the Project
would include support for project evaluation through a new Center for
Research in Rural Development (CIDER).
5.        The Project would provide on-farm development credit which would
be channeled through the existing agricultural credit system. Medium- and
long-term development loans to small farmers would carry the terms and
rates of interest agreed for other agricultural credit projects supported
by the IBRD (currently 7.6% interest). Short-term production credit would
carry an interest rate of 12%. The Guarantee Loan Fund of the Bank of
Mexico would operate the on-lending mechanisms through public and private
participating banks in accordance with procedures established under the
agricultural credit loans supported by the IBRD.
6.        Each micro-region plan would be submitted to IBRD for approval
prior to disbursement of loan proceeds. Fifty percent (15 micro-regions)
of the Project has been appraised, with the remaining 15 micro-region
programs to be submitted before June 30, 1975 for detailed individual
reviews prior to disbursement authorization. Disbursement would be against
actual expenditure on eligible items. International competitive bidding
would be carried out on single contracts over US$250,000. Local competitive
bidding, negotiated contracts and force account would be applied for
individual works below this amount in accordance with established government
procedures.



- iii -
7.        The Project would be carried out by some 14 federal and state
agencies.   At the federal level, a Coordinating Committee (serviced by
a 45-person Technical Secretariat) of participating agencies meets monthly
under the chairmanship of the Presidency to set policy, approve micro-
region plans and resolve major execution problems.   A similar Committee
(also serviced by technical staff) is formed within each state having one
or more ongoing micro-region development programs.   Coordination at the
local level is focused on the mayors of municipalities and community
leaders.
8.        The Project would benefit areas with a total rural population of
about 1.5 million.   Beneficiaries targeted for Project support would have
family incomes ranging from US$250 to US$750 placing them in the lower 50%
of incomes in the rural sector and the lowest 20% of the total population.
PIDER selection criteria, when applied to their full 100 micro-region develop-
ment program, would include areas containing about 40% (5 million) of the  '
poorest 12 million persons living in the rural areas. In this Project,
about 120,000 farmers (750,000 people) would be expected to achieve a near
doubling of real income over a period of eight years if all goes as planned.
For those farmers benefiting from the 30,000 ha of irrigation, it is
expected that incomes will triple from present levels to full development.
Furthermore, some 350,000 people would have access to improved rural infra-
structure.   Under the Project,about 84,000 man-years of temporary labor
and 30,000 man-years of permanent labor would be created.   Main annual
production increases resulting from Project investment would be in maize
(200,000 tons), beans (40,000 tons), beef (25,000 tons), honey (200 tons)
and fruit and vegetables (10,000 tons). About half of the increment of
maize and bean production would be consumed by the producers, the remainder
being marketed for internal consumption in Mexico. Typical ejido and village
development models in representative micro-regions show economic rates of
return on productive investments averaging 16%, with a variation from 11% to
23%.   Higher economic returns may be achieved in regions of more substantial
productive possibilities especially where based on irrigation.   Financial
rates of return on directly productive activities average 22%, varying from 15%
to 37%.   Social infrastructure such as health centers, schools and drinking
water would improve basic literacy and health standards, especially in
relation to water borne bacteria.   Beneficiaries would contribute 20% of
installation costs of electrification and 15% to drinking water supplies,
and would meet all operation and maintenance costs.  Contributions in cash,
labor or kind would be made toward the costs of health and education facilities.
9.        The Project has risks.   First, while on average the estimated
rates of return at micro-region level embody expectations about final out-
comes that are reasonable and realistic, nevertheless the calibre of local
leadership and staff involved under the program will bear importantly on
these outcomes.   It would be unrealistic to suppose that staff, leadership



- iv -
and management quality will be uniform.   In some regions results may
be less than expected, while in others better than anticipated results
should be achieved.   Second, while two-thirds of the micro-regions would
be in the higher rainfall areas (over 750 mm annually), the remaining
third would be located in areas with less rain.   In these latter areas,
the introduction of irrigation, pasture improvement and water conservation
works would be emphasized to reduce farmer risks.   However, there is some
uncertainty as to farmer responses to new technical packages for improving
rainfed maize and bean production in these less favored areas,
Improved field demonstration closely tied with deepened extension efforts
under the Project would be emphasized to minimize this uncertainty.
The Government has shown a high degree of flexibility in the execution of
the program and the recently established research center (CIDER) will
undertake a substantial and continuing evaluation effort as a basis for
timely adaptation of recommended and tested practices to small farmers'
needs.   Also, the Project would emphasize self-help activities, the
creation of community-based users' associations and the development of
local leadership capabilities in order to improve the probability that
the mix of Project investments and services would eventually lead tc a
self-sustaining development momentum at the local level.
10.       In conclusion, the PIDER project represents a major commitment
by Mexico to raise levels of living among a relatively large number of
rural poor.   The PIDER program now has a two and one-half year history.
Major new thrusts in the program towards increasing small farmer productivity
have been initiated, with the result that PIDER has taken the lead in generat-
ing major improvements in the key agencies dealing with the rural poor,
especially the Secretariat of Agriculture and Livestock.   PIDER, as well
as the other river basin and functional programs directed towards the
rural poor, continue to receive strong political commitment.



I1EXICC
INTEGRATED RUlRAL DEVELOPMENT PROjECT - PIDER
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01        The Government of Mexico is placing high priority on raising
the productive capacity of its 12 million rural poor (per capita incomes
of less than US$100), who make up over 50% cf the rural population and
20% of the total population.   Incomes of the rural population are much
lower than those of the urban sector.   The Government is committed to
narrowing this gap.
1.02        Particular emphasis is being placed on developing regions
containing substantial numbers of poorer farmers, regions identified as
having good potential for raising farmer incomes.   To undertake this task,
Mexico has expanded its existing functional rural development activities
such as credit, feeder roads and small-scale irrigation and, at the same
time, has devised a new micro-region program designed to integrate and
focus agency activities on carefully targeted areas of rural poverty.
This latter program, called PIDER (Investment Program for Rural Development),
was created in 1972 to facilitate this coordinated strategy in 100 micro-
regions. Forty-five of these micro-regions, already identified, are now
in operation.
1.03        The Government has requested IBRD and IDB financial assistance
to help implement and expand this PIDER program.   In 1973 and 1,074, project
preparation was assisted by an extensive series of visits from Bank staff
and consultants.   During the project preparation and appraisal process,
emphasis was placed on productive measures, particularly irrigation; credit
provision and improved extension services were strengthened; emphasis was
also placed on micro-region selection criteria, the program's administrative
machinery and the mechanism for monitoring and evaluation.   Appraisal was
undertaken in June/July 1974.
1.04        Fourteen IBRD projects totalling US$551 million have been
previously approved for agricultural and rural development projects in Mexico.
Recently a. US$50 million loan (1053-ME) was approved for a rural development
proJect in the Papaloapan River Basin. The bulk of these loans have been for
the development of irrigation (72%) and the provision of agricultural credit
(28%). JPerformance under these projects has been generally satisfactory.
II. BACKGROUND
A, Rural Development
2.01        Modern rural development in Mexico dates to the land reform
begun in 1917.   This reform was started with the
redistribution of land rights.   However, major efforts at supporting



- 2 -
this redistribution were not made until the Cardenas period (1935-1941)
when an extensive land redistribution program was carried out and agricul-
tural input and credit needs of ejidatarios 1/ began to be recognized. The
Bank Ejidal was created during this period.   Governments following
Cardenas pursued a different strategy, concentrating on industrialization
and development of large-scale farming under irrigation.   This policy
resulted in one of the highest rates of sustained growth of agricultural
production in Latin America (an average of 5% per year from 1950 to 1970),
but income inequalities appear to have widened as the economy was unable to
provide employment for the poorer segments of the rural population, espe-
cially those living in rainfed agricultural areas.
2.02        Rural Population.  The rural population (about 20 million) of
Mexico comprises three main groups: ejidatarios, private farmers and the
landless. There are about 1.5 million ejidatario families farming 22,500
ejidos, about 1.2 million privately owned farms and 1.2 million landless
families. The population has grown so rapidly in the face of limited land
resources that fully one-half of the agricultural labor force is now landless,
many of them sons of peasants with rights in ejidos too small to provide a
living.
2.03        Income.  Since 1960, Mexico-s overall economic performance has
been matched by few developing countries.  GDP has grown at an average annual
rate of about 7.0% in real terms. However, the benefits of this growth have
been unequally distributed. In mid-1973 the average family income was esti-
mated at US$2,700.  However, some 2 million families (over 50% of the rural
population and 20% of the total population) had average incomes of less than
US$800 per family. The Government estimates that an income equivalent to
US$960 provides the minimum acceptable living standard for Mexican families
in the rural areas.
2.04        Employment.  All employment estimates are tentative.  In the
non-irrigated rural areas, 18% of the labor force cannot find employment at
prevailing rural wages and 44% cannot find more than three months employment.
Irrigated agriculture is considered 2./ the most effective permanent employment-
generating instrument in agriculture, with 1 ha of irrigated agriculture
providing about 8 man-months of employment compared to less than 1 man-month
per ha on a non-irrigated farm taken on an annual basis.
1/  The term "ejido" is derived from the Spanish equivalent of the village
"common". In present Mexican law, the ejido is basically a group of
families with joint - and thus inalienable - tenure rights to land.
The head of such a family is called an ejidatario.
2/ Based on Presidency estimates in the Central Plateau area.



2.05      Rural Infrastructure.  These conditions are further exacerbated
by the lack of infrastructure and basic services. In the poorer rural
areas, there are only 55 medical practitioners per 100,000 of population
(77 is the national average); 24% of houses have access to pure drinking
water (61% nationally); and only 55% of children in the 6-14 years age
group are enrolled at school (76% nationally).
B. Rural Development Strategies in Mexico
2.06      Thus the Mexican Government has organized itself through both
spatial (river basin and micro-region) and functional (credit, extension,
irrigation, feeder road) programs for a major effort to address rural
poverty in Mexico over the next ten years. These programs are specifically
addressing the rural families with incomes less than the equivalent of
US$800 per year. Over this period, river basin programs (Papaloapan,
Balsas and Tehuantepec), intensified functional efforts in credit, extension
and especially irrigation and the expanded PIDER micro-region effort assessed
in this report should make a substantial impact on Mexico's rural poor if the
current political commitment is maintained.
Functional Programs
2.07      Until 1970 development efforts in the poorer rural areas were
focused along main roads and regional river basin development programs
(Comision del Balsas, Comision del Papaloapan, Comisi6n Valle del Fuerte,
etc.), most of which were starved of resources after large dams and other
infrastr-acture had been constructed.
2.08      In 1970, however, the Government began in earnest to implement a
policy shift by allocating substantial resources to a number of schemes
directed at raising incomes and increasing basic services in the poorer rural
areas of Mexico. This was brought about by expanding two existing rural
development strategies and devising a third. First, river basin development
programs are being resuscitated (Papaloapan Project 1053-ME) with special
efforts concentrated on poorer groups located within the basin by providing
packages of low-cost productive and social investments. Second, rural
development capabilities are being strengthened in agencies, such as the
Ministry for Hydraulic Resources (SRH), official credit agencies, the Ministry
of Public Works (SOP), the Federal Electricity Commission (CFE), the Ministry
of Agrarian Reform (SRA), and CONASUPO, the Government marketing agency.
Separate rural development departments were created in many of these agencies.
A related commodity extension program known as "Plan Puebla" is also being
expanded by the Ministry of Agriculture (SAG). Plan Puebla was initiated in
1967 by the International Center for Maize and Wheat Seed Improvement (CIMMYT)
to develop an efficient approach for promoting rapid increases in rainfed
maize production on the small holdings of Mexican farmers. Third, a new
program for integrated rural development (PIDER) was initiated.



PIDER
2.09        ObIectives and Strategy.   In 1972 the Government initated a
comprehensive nationwide program of rural development (PIDER) directed toward
the development of regions of endemic rural poverty.   The program's primary
objective is to provide investments and services in selected rural areas, in
order to:
a. raise rural living standards by introducing directly
productive activities;
b. increase levels of permanent and temporary employment; and
c. strengthen supporting productive activities and improve basic
social infrastructure.
To achieve these objectives, the Government's strategy is to focus additional
investments in micro-regions by improving the administrative operations of
its existing planning and execution system through the establishment of PIDER
coordinating machinery in the Ministry of the Presidency.   PIDER's strategy
is threefold;
a. increase and focus investments and services of existing agencies
on selected poor rural micro-regions with productive potential;
b. decentralize planning and especially execution to state and
local levels; and
c. encourage village and ejido level participation in the
planning and execution process.
At present, only small amounts of the regular agency budgets are being spent on
these micro-regions.   The basic unit for purposes of investment programming in
these selected poor areas is a defined micro-region comprising from two to seven
contiguous municipalities within one state (see Annex 2).   The average micro-
region contains about 50,000 persons living in three municipalities.
2.10        Characteristics.  PIDER has three characteristics that distinguish
it from ongoing rural development programs. First, PIDER is directed to regions
which are among the most depressed economically in the country. It is an attempt
to redistribute income and productive capacity to communities which have been
bypassed by other programs. Second, budget control is exercised through the
PIDER office in the Ministry of the Presidency, which has,the power to assign
all funds allocated for the PIDER program. This is a departure from the usual
situation in which each agency is independently funded and operates according
to its own assessment of priorities. Financing of each micro-region is through
funds allocated to each agency on the basis of an integrated micro-region
investment plan. Agencies can only withdraw funds for purposes approved in
the plan, and, third, it is an attempt to decentralize both planning and program
execution. This decentralization is directed at the involvement of beneficiaries
in the planning process, and includes the strengthening of the planning and
decision-making process at state and village levels.



-5-
2.11      Progress.  Thus far, 45 micro-regions are in operation.  As of
December 197T, US$133 million has been invested in the initial micro-regions.
Ovrer the period 1975-77, a further US$450 million has been programmed to
complete their development. In addition, Government expects to program an
additional US$650 million to develop a further 55 micro-regions, resulting
in an estimated total expenditure of US$1.2 billion. Some 5 million poor
rural people are likely to benefit when the 100 micro-region program is
complete, some 25% of the rural population.
C. Supporting Services in the Rhral Areas
2.12      In the past, the services supporting the development of rural areas
had been developed in a parallel rather than coordinated manner. These were
also largely directed to stimulating agricultural production primarily on
larger farms. With the advent of a more balanced policy since 1970, many of
those agencies have created special rural development departments charged with
devising programs and techniques for servicing the needs of the rural poor
(Annex 3). Among those active in this field is the Ministry for Hydraulic
Resources, which has established a special department for the planning and
execution of small-scale irrigation schemes at village and ejido level.
Throughout Mexico, and with financial assistance from IDB, this department
has created some 200,000 ha of irrigation for about 30,000 small farmers since
1965. Agricultural credit is available from both official and private insti-
tutions. In 1972, the Bank of Mexico altered its procedures in order to
intensify its rediscounting operations for development loans made by the
banking system (especially Banco Ejidal and Banco Agricola) to ejidos and
small farmer groups. Government's Banco Ejidal focuses particularly on
ejidos, while Banco Agricola services the requirements of small private
farmers. Banco Nacional Agropecuario (BANAGRO), while lending mainly to
medium and larger farmers, has also developed capability in its lending
operations to smaller farmers.
2.13      A new land reform law has added an important development role to the
Ministry of Agrarian Reform (SRA) which has created two new institutes: SOFE,
a department promoting ejido organization and development, and INACAP, a
national institute for campesino training. Special SOFE 13-person multi-
disciplinary teams are organized to work with ejidos and villages to sensitize
them to available development opportunities and to reorganize ejido and village
committees, so as to assure fuller local "bottom up" participation in their
jointly worked out development program.
2.14      The primary responsibility for expanding agrioultural extension and
research efforts in the poorer rural areas of Mexico lies with the Ministry
of Agriculture. It has been charged with the expansion of the "Plan Puebla"
extension system and has responsibility for administering the livestock
improvement program and expanding soil and water conservation efforts. SRH
and the credit agencies also have important extension and field demonstration
roles to support their irrigation and credit investments.



- 6 -
2.15        The number of rural based agricultural technicians employed in
federal agencies and banks has risen markedly since 1967 and by 1974 totaled
some 6,000.     Moreover, 700-900 agriculturalists and several thousand
middle-level technicians are graduating each year from Mexican colleges
and universities. These technicians are slowly introducing new technologies
into small farmer systems in rainfed areas. In view of the long
lead-time required for field trials and farm demonstrations to produce
results (even under such relatively favorable ecological and social circum-
stances as prevailed in Plan Puebla areas), extension workers may have to
operate for some time with uncertainty regarding knowledge of the various
agricultural practices for drier rainfed areas. Nevertheless, many specific
recommendations already exist as applicable to rainfed farming technology,
such as the Plan Puebla-developed maize (and bean) technology for sub-humid
areas, improved special water conservation measures and new storage techniques
of maize silage for livestock feeding in semi-arid areas.
2.16        Minimum support prices, administered by CONASUPO, are offered
for a number of food crops including maize, wheat, sorghum, beans and oil-
seeds. CONASUPOts crop purchasing system generally functions adequately.
Milk processing and marketing cooperatives purchase the bulk of dairy
production, both from larger and smaller farmers.
2.17      Various other agencies are involved in providing productive
support and social infrastructure in rural areas. Among the more important
are the Ministry of Public Works in charge of a countrywide labor-intensive
feeder road progran, the Ministry of Health (SSA) charged with providing
health services to rural areas and together with SRH, rural water supplies,
and the Federal Electricity Commission which has substantially expanded its
efforts in rural electrificati6n (Annex 3).
2.18        These improvements in supporting agency capabilities for dealing
with the rural poor are important to achieve the PIDER program objectives.
III. THE PROJECT
A. Objectives and Strategy
3.01        In line with PIDER's overall objectives (para  2.09), individual
micro-regions to be developed were selected using criteria based on population
density, productive potential, poverty levels, and lack of basic infrastructure,
such as roads, markets, and drinking water (Annex 2). Each micro-region plan
was prepared on the basis of field research on existing economic activities
and the potential for further development.  The directly productive and
productive support investments required to exploit this potential were iden-
tified and subsequently evaluated. Social infrastructure investments were



-7-
included where required to reinforce the development of the micro-region's
longer-term productive potential and to increase the potential for initiating
a self-sustaining rural development process.   All these investments
were programmed for each micro-region over a four-year period on the basis
of priority and each community's absorptive capacity.
3.02        For the purpose of appraisal, the country was divided into six
ecological zones (arid, semi-arid, transitional, temperate, dry tropics and
wet tropics - see Map 11418). The bulk of Mexico's rural poor live in these
zones. Micro-regions were appraised in the field which were broadly repre-
sentative of conditions prevailing in these zones. (See Annex 2.) In total,
30 detailed micro-region investment plans would be included in the Project
of which 15 have been already submitted and reviewed. Another 15 would be
submitted before June 30, 1975. Of the 15 submitted, 12 have been reviewed
in the field, six in detail (Annex 1). Annex 8, Table 1 summarizes data on
the 15 submitted micro-regions.
B. Brief Description
3.03        The Project would assist the implementation of these investment plans
in 30 micro-regions with a rural population of 1.5 million. For the 15 micro-
regions which have been reviewed, the last two years of the investment plan
would be financed.  For another 15 micro-regions, which would be submitted for
IBRD approval before June 30, 1975, the last three years of the plan would be
financed. Estimated costs for the latter 15 were based. on projections from
experience made from assessing the first 15. The Project would also improve
and consolidate planning coordination and implementation by existing government
agencies involved in rural development, and support an intensive evaluation
effort. The Project is designed to support:
a. Directly Productive Investments (66%) through the provision
of development credit for beef, dairy cattle and other
livestock purchases, rainfed grain farming, irrigated crop
and fruit tree production and beekeeping. Finance would
also be provided for improved livestock production (land
clearing, fencing and stock handling yards), irrigation
development and rehabilitation and soil and water
conservation.
b. Productive Support Investments (22%) through the provision
of finance for the development of improved extension services,
market and store construction, electrification and improved
training (mainly at farmer level involving training in-farm
management and decision-making). The Project would also
assist in speeding up clearance of land titles to newly-
established ejidos, and support technical services including
agricultural research at federal, state and village levels.
c. Social Infrastructure (12%) by the provision of improved
drinking water supplies, materials for self-help village
improvement projects, construction of primary schools and
by the development of limited rural health facilities.



d. Evaluation by supporting the activities of a new
Center for Research in Rural Development which inter alia
would carry out Project-related evaluation and applied
research.
3.04        The table on the following page illustrates the type and distribu-
tion of investments estimated under the Project.
C. Detailed Features
3.05        Micro-Region Selection Criteria.  To meet PIDER requirements for
development, micro-regions must be poor, have productive potential, and lack
basic infrastructure required to exploit the agricultural potential. During
appraisal,criteria were developed for the selection of micro-regions (Annex 2).
These criteria were based on a number of minimum target thresholds which each
micro-region has to meet as part of the selection and approval process. These
threshold criteria involve population density, poverty, underemployment and
productive potential.
3.o6        This approval process is based on the investment program balance,
an assessment of the micro-region organization and staffing and a threshold
economic rate of return of a minimum of 10%. While each of the 15 submitted
micro-regions have more than 75% of their overall investment, including
credit, programmed for productive investments, assurances were obtained
during negotiations that any micro-region to be submitted having less than
70% productive investments including credit would not be eligible for support
under the Bank loan. In appraising the remaining 15 micro-regions the Bank
would apply a three-tier economic rate of return assessment test. Micro-regions
showing a 15% or higher rate of return on productive investments would initially
be approved for reimbursement, with selected field checks. Those micro-regions
submitted carrying rates of return between 10% and 15% would be subject to a
detailed field appraisal by IBRD staff. After careful checking of the basic
assumptions underlying the return calculations, those actually falling below
10% would not be accepted for IBRD support under the Project. Assurances
on this minimum rate of return test (10%) were obtained during negotiations.
This appraisal would be made by IBRD staff prior to recommending acceptance
of the remaining micro-region investment plans for disbursement purposes.
and assurances to this effect were obtained at negotiations.
3.07        Village/Ejido Investment Programming.  During the initial village-
level investment programming, a joint state- and federal-level team visits each
village or ejido in a selected micro-region. Prior to its visit, a detailed
baseline diagnosis is completed at the state level from various censuses and
agency files (SRA, SRH, etc.).    This diagnosis includes basic data on the
village or ejido. Included in this basic diagnosis are population and income
data, cropping patterns, availability of existing resources (groundwater, stream
flows, soil and rainfall patterns), and basic infrastructure and services
(schools, roads, etc.) and a summary of all requests for improvements made
by the village to state- and federal-level authorities. During its

10 -
visit, the programming team checks and revises these data with the
villagers, explains the PIDER objectives and strategies as they might apply
to their village, and obtains from villagers their development priorities.
The mix of the villager-desired investment program is then assessed (on
the basis of priority needs for income and employment growth and improved
provision of an essential level of basic services) against available tech-
nical information (existence of groundwater, soils, etc.) and finally
developed into a multi-sectoral village-level investment program. These
village or ejido investment programs are then consolidated into micro-region
development programs. These approved programs are subject to revision on
an annual basis in the light of the "feed forward" experiences generated
through the intensive PIDER monitoring process. Where this annual reprogram-
ming results in major changes to an approved micro-region investment program
(a 30% or more increase or decrease in any of the social components),
IBERD approval for the reprogrammed plan would be required. Assurances on
such approvals were obtained during negotiations.
3.08        During appraisal, a more systematic village/ejido level
investment programming methodology (described below) was devised. PIDER
then agreed to reprogram its existing micro-regions using this improved
methodology. This appraisal report is based on 15 of these reprogrammed
micro-regions.
3.09        Essentially, this new methodology makes explicit the incremental
income and employment generating aspects of the ejido development program.
It also enables PIDER programming staff to test the internal consistency of
the ejido investment package. The investments programmed in the representative
ejido and village plans were systematically evaluated using financial rate
of return analyses and included estimates that temporary labor required for
development works related to the development of that ejido could be met from
the off-season under-employed labor from that ejido. This analysis of a pro-
posed investment package for a "representative" ejido thus provides an
analytical measure of the investment program as a whole for the micro-region,
consolidated from village and ejido based programs.
3.10        This methodology can then be used to assess: (i) the
projected yields which underlie the technical feasibility of obtaining increased
incomes; (ii) the annual temporary and permanent labor requirements of the
proposed investment package measured against seasonally unemployed ejido labor;
and, (iii) the availability of funds from within the ejido to meet fixed levels
of cash contributions to social infrastructure such as water and electricity
and to service increased operating loan amortization charges from incremental
income flows. In addition, this ejido budgeting methodology enables the
various agencies interfacing with the ejido members to time better their
investment, service and credit provision activities. Finally, the methodology
requiresthe calculation of financial and economic rates of return.



- 11 -
3.11        As mentioned, PIDER prepares an investment program for each
village or ejido receiving assistance under a micro-region program. In
addition, PIDER has now prepared detailed financial budgets in its reprogrammed
micro-region plans for actual villages or ejidos in each micro-region.
These detailed financial budgets are being prepared after selection
by PIDER staff as being an indicative sample of the likely cash
flow and benefit streams to be obtained from the investment packages proposed
for the majority of ejidos to be developed in that micro-region. To conso-
lidate and expand this budgeting methodology, assurances were obtained during
negotiations that ejido or village budgets would be prepared for representative
villages or ejidos participating in the Project no later than one year following
the signing of the loan. Copies of these budgets would be available at ejido/
village, state and PIDER (federal) levels.
Directly Productive Activities
3.12        Irrigation (17%).  Development of small-scale irrigation is expected
to comprise 17% of investments under the loan. The Project would develop
30,000 ha of irrigated land at an average cost of US$1,200 per ha (Annex 3a).
Construction would be carried out mainly by SRH, using a specialized department
called Irrigation Works for Rural Development. A small proportion of work
would be undertaken by SAG.
3.13        Prior to development, technical and socio-economic feasibility
studies would be carried out for each proposed irrigation project in accordance
with existing SRH guidelines, which are acceptable. SAG irrigation projects
supported under the Project would follow similar procedures. Assurances that
these procedures would continue to be followed both by SRH and by SAG were
obtained at negotiations.
3.14        Investment in the Project's irrigation component would be limited
to-US$4,000 per family for projects up to 25 ha (small works). For these
smaller works, the total cost of a single project would not exceed US$32,000
and would benefit no less than eight families. For larger projects (26 to
2,500 ha) the investment costs per family v/ would not exceed US$24,000 and
no family with a pre-project net income of greater than US$2,500 would be
eligible beneficiaries. Assurances were obtained at negotiations on these
limits. Costs of operation and maintenance would be the responsibility of
SRH or SAG and would be recovered from the beneficiaries. On irrigation
projects the level of recovery (over 0 & M costs) of investment costs would
be determined by economic studies carried out by SRH taking into account
the ability of water users to pay and the need to maintain an incentive for
them to make best use of the land and water available to them. This is in
accordance with the requirements of the 1972 National Water Law (Article 79).
3.15        Farm Development Credit (29.6%).  Considerable progress under the
Fourth Agricultural Credit loan has been made in developing criteria and
institutional capabilities for lending to low-income farmers. By early 1975,
the US$25 million allocated under this loan was fully committed. Both
1/ The average expenditure per family benefitting from new irrigation is
expected to be about the equivalent of US$ 5000. Average investment per
family for irrigation rehabilitation is expected to be about US$500.



- 12 -
public (Banco Agropecuario, Banco Ejidal and Banco Agr'cola) and private
banks had made loans to small farmers under the technical and financial
criteria established by the FONDO of the Bank of Mexico.
3.16        In 1974, substantial effort was made by PIDER and FONDO staff
to assess the credit (seasonal and longer-term) needs of small farmers in the
micro-regions. FONIDO and Banco Ejidal representatives joined the PIDER
Coordinating Committee in 1974 to ensure closer integration of credit promo-
tion and availability with the programmed physical infrastructure.
3.17        The Project would promote: (i) the access by small farmers to new
or expanded micro-region-level credit facilities; and (ii) the efficient use
of seasonal and longer-term development credit by these farmers, especially
those organized in groups. The incorporation of a credit component would
ensure the timely availability of credit to maximize the efficient use of
productive infrastructure. Seasonal production credit at an interest rate
currently at 12% would be provided under the Project from existing credit
lines, but would not be reimbursable from the proposed loan. The bulk of
the medium- and long-term credit would be for supporting credit needs resulting
from irrigation investments and for cattle purchases to stock newly cleared
lands. Development credit would be made available on the same terms as are
applied nationally in Mexico (currently 7.6% interest and 3- to 12-year terms).
The same arrangements set under the 910-ME low-income producers credit
component would be followed under this Project, and assurances that these
conditions (or those subsequently agreed by the Bank in connection with
future loans for national credit programs) would be followed under this
Project were obtained during negotiations.
3.18        Livestock Development (10%).  The Project would support livestock
development on some 800 ejidos. Beef cattle development would be the major
investment involving land clearing, pasture establishment, perimeter fencing
and the provision of limited cattle watering and handling facilities. Under
the ongoing program, there has been a tendency in some micro-regions for
over-design of livestock facilities by the SAG livestock extension division.
To rectify this, assurances were obtained that SAG would implement revised
plans for low-cost livestock facilities.
3.19        Soil and Water Conservation (4.9%).  The Project would support the
construction of soil and water conservation works by labor-intensive methods.
SAG estimates that 47% (93 million ha) of Mexico's land area has been destroyed
(14%) or damaged (33%) by soil erosion. Under the Project, some 91,000 ha
would involve the construction of contour ridges, terrac'es and checkdams for
gully protection. Contour ridging for water conservation in the drier areas
such as southern Nuevo Leon costs an average of US$45/ha, while terraces for
both water and soil control in Zacapoaxtla cost an average of US$180/ha.
Programs would be implemented on both arable and grazing land. The program
would be carried out by the Soil and Water Conservation Directorate of SAG



- 13 -
(Annex 3b). No cash or labor contributions for conservation works are required
under the PIDER/SAG conservation works. However, de facto contributions result
from the payment procedure. In most cases, only part of the payment is in cash
while the remainder is paid as subsistence food with a value equivalent to less
than the regional daily minimum salary. Assurances were obtained at negotiations
that benefiting ejidos and villages would provide for maintenance ol works under
SAG supervision. Furthermore, assurances were obtained that labor-intensive
techniques of construction would continue to be emphasized, and that prior to
construction detailed conservation plans on a watershed basis would be prepared
for each village or ejido area.
3.20        Fruit Production (4.9%).  The Project would support the development
of orchard infrastructure and the initial planting of fruit trees on some 9,500 ha
on ejidos and in villages in the micro-regions. CONAFRUT (the national commis-
sion for fruit production) is an autonomous agency operating under SAG charged
with developing fruit production in rural Mexico (see Annex 3m). CONAFRUT's
organization and technical capabilities have been substantially improved since
1971. Under the Project, CONAFRUT's micro-region activities relating to farmer
demonstration, nursery development, orchard preparation, plant distribution
and technical extension would be supported. The types of fruit production
supported under the Project vary according to ecological areas, ranging
from apples in Nuevo Leon to mangoes and avocadoes in Yucatan.
3.21        Other Productive Investments (1%).   The Project would also
support limited investments in the project micro-regions in beekeeping
(US$1 million), forestry (US$1.8 million) and fishery development (US$0.12
million). Beekeeping is a traditional productive activity carried on mainly
by small farmers in many parts of Mexico, especially in the Yucatan area.
Under the project, provision for hive improvement and expansion plus support-
ing gathering equipment and fencing would be provided. It is a particularly
labor-intensive activity. Reforestation and afforestation investments on
selected ejidos would also be provided for, especially where those ejidos
have already established small sawmills. In two micro-regions, Sinaloa and
Baja California, supporting fishery infrastructure in the form of cold storage
facilities would be assisted.   SAG would be responsible for supervising
construction and ongoing extension for beekeeping and forestation and for
supervising maintenance of fishery cold stores.
Productive Support Activities
3.22        Feeder Roads (6%).   The Project would provide for the construction
of about 1,900 km of feeder roads. Over the past few years SOP has developed
considerable technical and organizational capabilities in the building of low-
cost feeder roads throughout various ecological regions of Mexico (Annex 3c).
Prior to construction, SOP require that committees be organized at ejido or
village level, in order that contracts for construction labor may be made.
Construction is usually carried out during the post-harvest season in order
to minimize conflict with the labor needs of agriculture. Labor-intensive



- 14 -
methods of construction would continue to be used. Currently, labor accounts
for about 70% of estimated construction costs of US$6,800 per km.  During
negotiations, assurances were obtained that economic and efficient labor-
intensive techniques of construction would continue to be emphasized and
construction would be scheduled in periods of seasonal slack in the agricul-
tural production cycle. Assurances were also obtained that SOP would
provide for continued maintenance of these roads.
3.23        Rural Marketing (0.7%).  In recent years CONASUPO has developed
an improved capability for expanding and servicing its network of rural stores
and warehouses (Annex 3d).  Under the Project some 60 small stores ("tiendas")
and 5 warehouses ("bodegas") for the purchase and storage of farm produce
would be constructed. Existing village structures would be converted into
stores at an average cost of US$2,700 each. Initial stock for such stores
would require an additional investment of US$2,500 per store. Stock would
include simple productive inputs such as garden seeds, hoes, pesticide and,
fertilizer as well as supplies of basic food requirements. CONASUPO, after
detailed review of earlier problems, has taken a number of steps to alter
and improve its warehouse program. New rural markets cost, with start-up
working capital, an average of US$184,000 with storage capacity of 900 tons
of grain. CONASUPO, in association with Banco Agropecuario, has devised an
innovative program of rural consumer credit (Annex 3d), linked to its rural
store and warehouse network. This has proved successful when tried on an
experimental scale. This credit program would be expanded slightly in the
Project areas on a closely monitored basis. Since the amounts involved are
small (US$170,000), IBERD financial support would not be required. During
negotiations, assurances were obtained that CO14ASUPO would: (i) select sites
for store and warehouse development in areas selected for productive poten-
tial; and (ii) give priority to villages that provide the basic store
structure free.
3.24        Farmer Organization and Training (2.8%).  The Project would provide
support to SRA for the expansion of staff and services within Project areas.
A new land reform law added a development role to SRA activities and led
to the creation of SOFE and INACAP. 1/ The Project would support the estab-
lishment of SOFE teams in each Project micro-region (Annex 3e). Each team
would cost about US$100,000 annually. Each SOFE team member would service
about eight ejidos or village groups containing some 5,000 persons in total.
These teams would make campesinos aware of the possibilities and need for
"bottom up" participation in development, orientate them to development
opportunities and reorganize ejido committees, so as to ensure full partici-
pation in a jointly agreed development program. Close liaison would be main-
tained with investment agency staff, especially SAG extension field teams,
in order to ensure that farmers are aware of government development services
and their obligations once a micro-region development program is underway.
1/ SOFE is SRA's Department of Organization and Ejido Development. INACAP is
the National Institute for Farmer Training, now being assisted under a
UNDP grant.



- 15 -
During negotiations, assurances were obtained that: (i) each team would be
given a minimum of one month's training prior to entering the designated
micro-region; and (ii) non-ejido small farmers in Project micro-regions would
not be discriminated against by these teams.
3.25        Extension and Field Demonstration (7.1%).  A major effort would be
made as part of the Project to carry out micro-region field demonstrations of
recommended technical practices and to improve the effectiveness of the extension
services operating in the micro-regions (Annex 3f). For the two-thirds of the
micro-regions with adequate rainfall (better than 750 mm) and for the irrigated
portions of drier regions, the recommended technical packages for improved crop
and livestock production are relatively well known. The principal effort in
these cases would focus on dissemination of results by improved extension
techniques. For those micro-regions with less than 750 mm rainfall, technical
packages for improving rainfed crop production are not as well developed.
Under the Project, intensified micro-region-level adaptive testing and farm-
level demonstration efforts would be carried out to broaden further the low risk
packages available to farmers in these areas. PIDER has made a major effort to
strengthen and revamp the SAG extension services operating in the micro-regions
by organizing them into multi-disciplinary brigades averaging 22 persons per
micro-region (a ratio of 1 to 400 families or four ejidos), by expanding
their training, particularly in Plan Puebla techniques,L/ field demonstration
and by introducing lower-level agricultural technicians into the brigade
system. Most of the micro-regions will be operating with these SAG-supported
extension brigades. However, PIDER is also supporting some alternative types
of extension efforts in a few micro-regions. These efforts are designed to
address specific ecological and/or cultural conditions prevalent in these
particular micro-regions. For example, one is Plan Zacapoaxtla, based directly
on Plan Puebla experiences, where a group of 12 technicians supported by local
farmer demonstrators is focusing mainly on a specific commodity/crop-oriented
extension effort rather than on a multi-crop approach. Another is the
High Chiapas effort, designed specifically for isolated Indian regions, where
4 to 6 professionals select and train local "promotores" who return to their
villages to work on improved extension practices. These "promotores" are
paid wages equivalent to about half the level of SAG lower-level technicians
who are high school-level agricultural graduates. Finally, intensified
technical assistance through the credit system, especially from Banco Ejidal
and Banco Agropecuario is encouraged by PIDER. Annex 3f provides further
details on these various approaches.
3.26        Quantitatively, the micro-regions are reasonably well serviced,
roughly in a one-worker to 400-farmer ratio. The major problem is to ensure
that th^.e technical packages being recommended are soundly based and are low
risk to participating farmers in the drier areas. To minimize this risk,
assurances were obtained that SAG would execute a plan of action to be agreed
1/ These extension teams will be trained in 1-1/2 months courses by supervisors
from CENEIEA National Demonstration and Agricultural Extension Center.



- 16 -
and approved as a condition of loan effectiveness, for the qualitative
improvement of SAG's micro-region-level extension brigades. This plan
of action would give particular attention to their need for intensified
training, the development of farmer-level crop trial techniques and the
use of local farmers as extension and demonstration agents.
3.27       Rural Electrification (5.7%).  The Project would bring electricity
to some 170,000 rural people living in 300 villages spread throughout some
of the poorest areas of Mexico at a total cost of US$12.1 million (Annex 3i).
50% of the demand is estimated to be associated with directly productive
investments and the rest for improving the living standards of the population.
The average cost of bringing electricity to villages is estimated at US$70
per beneficiary. CFE builds, operates and maintains electricity generation
and distribution facilities throughout Mexico and is now implementing a
national six-year (1971-76) plan for rural electrification, which is now
more than 50% completed. During negotiations, assurances were obtained from
Government that installation costs would not exceed US$150 per person, that
villagers would pay 20% of installation costs, cover the full cost of opera-
tion and maintenance and repay investment costs over 25 years. CFE's rate
structure as now applied in the rural areas is adequate for this purpose.
Social Infrastructure
3.28        Village/Ejido-Level Educational Facilities (2.7%).  The Project
would help improve education opportunities by renovating, constructing and
furnishing 1,150 primary level classrooms accommodating some 60,000 students.
New construction of each classroom would cost about US$5,500 inclusive of
furniture (Annex 3g). This program would account for nearly 30% of the current
building program of CAPFCE (the school construction agency) directed at rural
communities of 300-2,500 people. CAPFCE has established a sound system of
rural school siting and construction, choosing low cost building designs
and methods and coordinating closely with the Ministry of Education to ensure
staffing arrangements.  Siting and construction would continue to be closely
coordinated with the Ministry of Education to ensure adequate provision of
teaching materials and staff. In carrying out the program, CAPFCE requires
benefiting ejidos and villages to provide a free site. CAPFCE also promotes
local contribution of building materials and teacher housing. During
negotiations, assurances were obtained that these facilities would be adequately
staffed and maintained by the Government.
3.29       Rural Health (1.6%).  The Project would provide for the renovation,
construction and equipping of 220 rural health posts and 70 rural health
centers (Annex 3h). Rural health posts, staffed by community health workers,
would be established in communities of 500 to 2,500 persons, and their new
construction would cost approximately US$4,000 each, including simple furniture
and equipment. Rural health centers costing US$50,000 each, when newly
constructed and equipped, would be established in larger rural towns with a
population of about 8,500 people (1,500 families). These centers have three



- 17 -
beds and are staffed by two assistants and a physician.  The Ministry of
Health (SSA) would be responsible for constructing health facilities under
the Project. During negotiations, assurances were obtained from Government
that beneficiaries would contribute 10% in cash, labor or materials to health
post construction costs and pay for non-staff operating costs and that
health posts and centers would be adequately staffed and maintained.  In
a few micro-regions, a more comprehensive low-cost rural health delivery
system would be developed. Aiming at a fuller coverage of rural population,
it would also provide for training of about 150 community health workers and
30 auxiliary nurses and the development of effective operation and management
capacity. An evaluation component would produce criteria for the widened
implementation of the National Health Plan.
3.30        Rural Water Supply (5.9%).  The Project would provide rural water
systems for approximately 250,000 persons ataan average cost of US$50 per
person (Annex 3j). Both SSA and SRH provide rural water systems, with SSA
concentrating on smaller villages and SRH on larger towns. Both agencies are
also responsible for operation and maintenance. During negotiations assurances
were obtained that a minimum contribution of 15% to investment costs would be
made by villagers, that full payment for operation and maintenance costs would
be obtained, that the strengthening of state-level maintenance operations would
be continued, and finally that there be a limit, except with IBRD approval, on
Government's share of system costs of US$100 per person benefited.
3.31        Materials for Self-Help Projects (1.6%). The Project would provide
support for self-help activities at ejido and village level. Materials costing
no more than US$40 per capita would be made available to ejidatarios and
villagers who would develop projects mainly on a group basis. Group projects
would be encouraged. All labor would be provided by beneficiaries. Self-
help projects would include a variety of activities such as workshops, vegetable
gardens, fencing for children's playgrounds, and community centers. During
negotiations, assurances were obtained from Government that the cost of materials
provided would not exceed the equivalent of US$40 per capita in each micro-
region.
D. Cost Estimates
3.32      The total cost of the Project is estimated at US$295 million, includ-
ing provision for_necessary incremental production credit;_US$50 million, or 17%,
represents foreign exchange costs of the Project. Base cost estimates for
civil works, equipment and labor are calculated on a-two-year experience with
investments under the PIDER program compounded from October 1974 prices (Annex 4).
Physical contingencies of 10% are included for construction, and equipment and
technical services components. Price contingencies of 31.5% are estimated,
based upon expected price increases of: 15% to 10% per annum for labor; 17%
to 10% per annum for civil works and equipment needs; and 5% to 10% per annum
for cattle, all compounded over the four-year disbursement period.

3.34        Incremental production credit required for the Project, amounting
to US$18 million, would be financed by the Government. The remaining project
cost of US$277 million would be financed by a proposed Bank loan of US$110
million which would cover foreign exchange costs of US$47 million (16% of
these remaining project costs) plus US$63 million of local currency costs
(21%). Government, including the Bank of Mexico, would contribute US$142
million (6I%).  Beneficiaries would contribute US$7 million (2%) to the costs
of water, electrification, development credit and health. In the case of
selt-help projects, materials would be provided by the Project, and labor
would be provided by the householder or village. Assurances on this cost
financing were obtained during negotiations.

3.35      The loan would be made to Nacional Financiera, S.A. (NAFIN), a
Government borrowing entity for loans from the Bank.
F. Disbursements
3.36      The Bank would reimburse Mexico 40% of the investment expenditures
for civil works, equipment, vehicles and technical services of agencies
participating in the program, against appropriate documentation, and 56%
of rediscounts of the development credit component administered by FOND0,
against certificates of expenditure, the documentation for which would not
be submitted for review but would be retained within Mexico and held
available for inspection by the IBRD during the course of supervision missions.
The statements of expenditure, certified by the Ministry of Finance, would
reflect the expenditures made by each agency in each approved micro-region.
Any undisbursed loan funds remaining after Project completion would be
cancelled. Since the Project would include 15 micro-regions already under-
way and 15 recently programmed micro-regions, a disbursement period of four
years is contemplated (Annex 5). Regional Government banks, selected on
a state basis, act as the financing agents of PIDER. The selected banks
receive, at the beginning of each fiscal year, comprehensive investment
plans for the approved micro-regions in their command areas, on the basis
of which they will disburse against vouchers from the executing agencies.
The participating banks will, in turn, be reimbursed by the Ministry of
Finance.
G. Procurement
3.37      The Project would include a diversity of relatively small construc-
tion jobs in many locations throughout the country involving at least 14
government agencies. Thus, only a small part of the Project would be
suitable for international competitive bidding.
3.38      For civil works and building construction, and for procurement
of machinery, equipment, materials, and supplies, the following procurement
procedures would be followed:
a. For single contracts exceeding US$250,000 in value,
international competitive bidding following Bank
Guidelines for Procurement would be required. The
normal preference for domestic suppliers of 15% would
be permitted in the evaluation of bids for manufactured
items. Each agency would endeavor, where feasible,-to
group contracts together in order to attract more
competition in bidding.



- 21 -
b.  Contracts less than this arcunt such as for
construction of classrooms, health posts, feeder
roads, markets, stores and other buildings which are
al" small, of varied design and geographically
scattered, wculd not be of sufficient size to attract
international bidders and would be constructed by a
combination of local competitive bidding, negotiated
contracts and force account in accordance with
Government's regular procedures. For such contracts
pre-established urit prices approved by each agency
would be acceptable. These agencies would then
undertake the work themselves (force account) or
select the private contractor best qualified to do
the job. Not more than the equivalent of US$60
million (67% of a total loan exclusive of credit)
would be let under local bidding. negotiated
contracts or by agency force account.
During negotiations, assurances vere obtairned from Government on these procure-
ment procedures.
H. Accounts, Monitoring and Auditing
3.39         In addition tc the regular annual budgetary allocation each agency
participating in the PIDER program receives a separate allocation at the begin-
ning of the fiscal year out of PIDER's budget to carry out the agreed program
of rural development in preselected regions throughout the country.  Separate
accounts for the PIDER program are kept by the Presidency, PIDER staff, the
Ministry of Finance and each participating agency to ensure there is no overlap
with the normal budgetary program. In addition, the Ministry of Finance would keep
separate accounts to cover the external financing of the program by the Bank
and IDB. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that these separate
accounts would continue to be maintained.
3.40        To ensure that expenditures are actuaily carried out as
programmed and approved, separate supervision and authorization/control
divisions are established within PIDER. The supervision division is divided
into seven zones, the authorization/control group into an authorization and
a control and statistics section. The supervision division staff spend
considerable time in the field, especially -n check-ing expenditure against
estimates. The system of progress monitoring is curre-it_y being reviewed to
establishl a close link wicth the project evaluation studies carried out in-
dependently by CIDER (para. 4.06). Given diversity of the investments and
micro-regions being supported, a substanlial supervision effort by IBRD staff
has been programmed.
3.41        Each agency has its own auditin& section where all vouchers
and documents are checked and approved before payments are made. The accounts
of each agency are subject to independent auditing by the Ministry of Finance,
the President's office and the Ministry for National Patrimony.



- 22 -
IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
4.01        Principal responsibility for the Project is vested with the
Ministry of the Presidency. This Ministry, with final authority on the
public investment budget, started coordinating the activities of public
agencies 1/ in the rural sector on a pilot basis in 1969. While public
agencies traditionally selected individual projects within their overall
budget and operated independently with little or no coordination, their
activities within the selected micro-regions are now synchronized within
the framework of PIDER. At the same time the main participating agencies
have created special departments for rural development in close liaison
with the Presidency. In total, these agencies have about 2,700 professionals
working full-time on the execution of the PIDER programs in the selected
micro-regions. This PIDER program is organized at three levels.
A. Federal Level
4.02        The highest policy-making body of PIDER is a coordination com-
mittee chaired by the director of public investment of Presidencia and
comprising senior officials of the participating executing agencies (see
Annex 6, Chart 1). The coordinating committee is supported by a permanent
w,orking group of agency representatives at the technical level. The
permanent working group develops planning methodology and concepts, screens
the micro-region investment plans for consistency and handles operational
issues. The working group in turn is supported by a special staff of 45
technicians within the Presidency which is responsible for preparation of
individual investment plans and routine management operations and investment
monitoring tasks.
B. State Level
4.03        Program execution in the micro-regions is coordinated by state
committees consisting of the state delegates of the participating agencies
under the chairmanship of the respective Governors. Management responsibi-
lity is delegated to an executive secretary who serves simultaneously as the
state PIDER director. The secretary is assisted by a technical secretariat
of Presidency staff at the state level and delegated liaison officers from
the executing agencies. The technical secretariat is primarily responsible
for day-t6-day coordination of operations within the different micro-regions
and for maintaining close contact with local authorities.
2   Federal institutions participating at the federal and state levels
include: Ministries of Agriculture, Finance, Public Works, Water
Resources and Health and Assistance; Agrarian Reform, Federal Electric
Commission, National Corporation for Basic Marketing, Committee for
Administration of the Federal School Construction Program, National
Arid Zones Commission, National Indigenous Institute, National
Institute for Rural Community Development and Low Cost Housing, and
the Ejido Bank.



- 23 -
C. Village/Ejido Level
4.o4        Local participation in the planning and execution of PIDER has
made rapid progress. This has been achieved through a variety of schemes,
depending on the political, social and administrative characteristics of the
micro-regions. As a rule, SRA field personnel take the lead in organizing
communities prior to the start of PIDER to ensure their full involvement.
In some cases, individual programs, e.g., feeder roads of the Ministry of Public
Works, small irrigation works of the Ministry for Hydraulic Resources, have
created village communities which can serve as a viable base for PIDER. Local
authorities, e.g., municipal chiefs, ejido presidents, teachers, are given
responsibilities according to the specific local circumstances, for organiza-
tion and supervision to promote their active involvement and to constitute an
independent information link to the state-level technical secretariat.
4.05        This three-tier organization provides the overall structure t6
carry out the various functions under the project. Experience to date
indicates that the system described works satisfactorily and without undue
inter-agency friction. Responsibilities are adequately delineated between
agencies. The appointment by PIDER of resident State and -- in some cases --
micro-region staff provides a direct coordinating link to the PIDER central
tier. In addition, such local-level staff provides PIDER and state-level
staff with a much more realistic assessment of ejido and village development
preferences. While the authority for selection of micro-regions and the approval
of the respective investment plans will remain at the central level, the
responsibility for plan preparation and coordination of the different
agencies is being taken over increasingly at the state level.  The indi-
vidual project works are executed by the appropriate public agency, as
determined by the central coordinating comnittee and concurred by the
state committee. Operation and maintenance, other than for larger works,
will be the responsibility of the benefiting communities. Project
implementation will be supervised at all three levels. While locally-
based PIDER staff would monitor continuously the progress of the Project,
the state committees would be informed independently by selected local
authorities. Federal level staff will, in addition, supervise progress
in the individual micro-regions on a routine basis,
4.o6        Evaluation.  A Center for Research in Rural Development (CIDER)
has been organized within the Presidency but independent from PIDER to
undertake evaluation and applied research tasks, to guide overall training
efforts and to diffuse information. For the purposes of evaluation, two
programs are being instituted.  The first is ex post,,  as the Center will
contract with Mexican and external research groups to do basic micro-region
evaluation studies. The second program is formative. CIDER will commit
"action teams" to field work in the micro-regions to evaluate project progress
for the purposes of information "feed forward" into micro-region reProgramming
exercises. For the training function, the Center will design various training
programs for the 2,700 staff of 14 agencies who are involved in executing



- 24 -
the PIDER program. These programs would be mainly micro-region-based and
focused on improving the local-level interaction between farmers and
government agency staff. Information from the ex poste and formative
evaluation will be introduced into this training program and diffused
through newsletters and other publications. During negotiations, agree-
ment was obtained that Government would undertake detailed evaluation of
the Project.
V. PRODUCTION, MARKETS, FARMER BENEFITS AND FINANCIAL RESULTS
A. Production, Yields and Marketable Surplus
5.01        The Project would support agricultural improvements relating
to both crop and livestock production of small farmers in the selected micro-
regions, including maize, beans, beef, milk, vegetables, fruits and honey.
Projected crop yields for the principal crops at full development compared
with yields under traditional agriculture without the Project are based on
experience in the Puebla region and on field trials and experience under similar
ecological conditions elsewhere in Mexico. Yield increases from existing
low levels would be facilitated under the Project by the improved training
provided for farmer-contact extension workers, more intensive trials and
demonstration of recommended practices on farmers' plots, and by provision
of credit and input delivery systems, seeds, fertilizers, etc., at the
village or ejido level. In addition, the development of irrigation facili-
ties on the semi-arid lands, where feasible, would permit major improvements
in crop yields relative to those achieved under rainfed conditions. Projected
yield improvements resulting from the Project for major crops in the categories
of irrigated, 1,200 mm rainfall and 800 mm rainfall are illustrated below:

5.02        Incomes among the 120,000 farm producers benefiting directly
under the project average around US$440 per family. At this low level of
income, production for household subsistence accounts for roughly 70% of the
output on the farm. As farm output and income rise as a result of the Project
so marketed surplus should increase considerably. Nevertheless, it is antici-
pated that a substantial fraction of incremental production would be absorbed
as additional direct consumption by the farm household. Base-year production,
incremental production resulting from the project, and the projected increase
in marketed output of major cropsare shown in the table.

B. Markets and Prices
5.03        Most of the maize, beef, potatoes, and beans produced by the Project
would be consumed locally, either for subsistence or in the local towns and
villages. Larger neighboring towns, currently largely supplied from outside
the micro-region, also provide a ready market for extra beef, vegetable and
fruit production. Institutional arrangements and facilities relating to
marketing would also be strengthened and improved under the Project through
the provision of produce stores and warehouses. The marketing agency would
also undertake to purchase surplus maize and beans at official support prices
should this be necessary, thus providing a price floor. Incremental project
output would contribute 2% and 4% respectively to Mexico's production of maize
and beans.
C. Producer Income and Benefits
5.04        Starting from an average of US$440 per farm, the effect of the
Project should be to raise incomes over a development period of about eight
years to US$700 per farm on rainfed areas and to US$1,100 for families with
access to new irrigation works. There is likely to be considerable variation
in the increase attained, however, related mainly to the varying productive
potential of the micro-regions in respect of agriculture, particularly in



- 26 -
regard to opportunities for irrigation. Thus, financial returns are estimated
to vary from a low 15% in Baja California micro-region to as high as 37% in
San Luis Potosi, with the average estimated at 22%. For a typical ejido-based
farmer participating in the Project with a small (2 ha) amount of new irriga-
tion, his income increment of US$660 would be derived from the following
categories:
