The importance of irrigation is illustrated by the fact that a mix of on-farm
development activities without supplementary irrigation is estimated to raise
families' incomes to US$700, as opposed to US$1,100 under irrigation. This
reinforces the PIDER investment emphasis given to small-scale irrigation under
the program. Presidency empirical data indicate that irrigation on small farms
may increase their net incomes 5 times and labor requirements from one
man-month per ha per annum on rainfed plots to nearly 9 man-months per ha
per annum on irrigated plots.
D. Cost Recovery and Fiscal Impact
5.05        Government would recover a total amount of US$150 million of ori-
ginal investments, or 51% of Project costs, leaving an initial fiscal impact of
US$144 million, excluding interest. The costs of maintaining and operating



- 27 -
directly productive infrastructure would be borne fully by beneficiaries.
They would also be charged with the full operating costs for marketing,
electricity, drinking water and self-help projects, and would contribute
to health and education. At full development, the additional fiscal burden
for Government would be US$6.7 million per annum, a small part of which (10%)
would be offset through additional tax revenues as a result of incremental
production and income from the Project. For the direct beneficiaries,
average per capita expenditures would be some US$10.
5.o6        Specific investments  under the Project would be recovered from
beneficiaries as follows (see also Annex 9):
a. Directly Productive Investment. While aiming at maximum
recovery of original investment costs especially in
irrigation, the responsible agencies - SAG and SRH - would
determine repayment capacity project by project. Since
the Project's target group is the rural poor, who will
need part of the income increase to meet their subsistence
needs, ho% of directly productive investments would
be recovered.
b. Credit. Government would follow established repayment and
interest rate policies, leading to full recovery.
c. Productive Support Investments. No direct recovery is
expected for feeder roads, extension and farm organization.
CFE user charges would enable full investment recovery for
electrification.
d. Social Infrastructure. Apart from community participation
in the construction of health, water supply, education and
self-help projects, no investment recovery is expected for
these components.
E. Ecological and External Effects
5.07        A portion of the Project (11%) is directed at improving range,
land and water management. Particular attention is being given to soil protec-
tion works in order to address an erosion problem of increasing severity. The
cattle component in the semi-arid and arid areas would contribute to some
temporary destocking of overgrazed pastures leading to improved range manage-
ment. The fertilizer and insecticides to be used in the Project would have
only a small effect on the local ecology given normal precaution in their
use. Soil and water conservation works would be directed at reducing soil
erosion, a significant problem in a number of the rural areas of Mexico.
Some of the materials for the self-help component would be used for the
construction of sanitation facilities for villages.



- 28 -
VI. ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
A. Economic Rate of Return
6.o0        Economic rates of return under the Project have been calculated
on the basis of typical ejido or village models for representative micro-
regions. The average economic rate of return is 16% varying by micro-region
from 11% to 23%. The calculations include on the cost side the indirectly
productive investments in infrastructure and services as well as the directly
productive on-farm investments. Thus, the costs of electrification, exten-
sion, farmer organization, feeder roads, and markets have been charged
against the benefit stream. While, however, the estimated production increases
are unlikely to be fully achieved without the inclusion of these items in the
total investment package, the calculation does not capture all the benefits
arising from the availability of these facilities, since their use is not
confined to Project investments or activities. For this reason the rate of
return estimates may be somewhat conservative.
6.02        Costs associated with the provision of improved public amenities
and social services - for health, education, water supply and electricity -
have not been charged against project benefits. This is mainly because it
was not possible to construct plausible estimates of productive benefits due
to provision of these services nor the monetary equivalent of the direct user
benefits associated with the consumption of improved services. Thus, short-
and long-term benefits to production arising from the improvement in health
conditions and the diffusion of functional literacy (important though these are
thought to be) have not been allowed for in the estimates of productivity
increase among the small farmers. The importance of these services as a
stimulus to co-operative arrangements and community associations - for example,
the Water Users Committees and the village associations for the construction of
feeder roads and soil conservation works - merits special mention, however.
Experience and confidence gained in these types of association would spill
over to improve and enhance the prospects for successful co-operative efforts
among producers. The encouragement and support given to such associations is
one of the important and innovative features of the PIDER program.
6.03        These social infrastructure investments have been planned: (i) so
as to provide each community with a minimum package of social services required
to avoid constraints to its economic and social development; and (ii)to raise
on average access to water, health and electrification from current levels
of about 45% to 60%, the latter about the overall averag6 for rural Mexico.
These social services have the immediate benefits of making producers more
productive (health, drinking water) and of enhancing community development (self-
help materials). But their most substantial, though unquantifiable, benefit
is that these social services reinforce the basis for sustained development in
the long run (education, electricity), laid by the project's productive invest-
ments.



- 29 -
604.        In calculating the economic cost of labor, shadow prices varying by
micro-region from 70% of the minimum daily wage down to 40% were used.  The
differences reflect mainly the relative uniformity of officially-determined
wage minima vis-a-vis variations in the minimum supply price of labor, the
latter being influenced by severity of rural underemployment. Since, however,
labor costs account for only 20% of total investment costs, alternative assump-
tions regarding these shadow wage rates would have limited impact on the
calculated rates of return. From the sensitivity analysis, in Sinaloa, for
example, the effect of a general 20% increase in investment costs would be to
reduce the rate of return from 16% to 12%; alternatively, the effect of a 20%
increase in operating costs would be to reduce the return to 13%. Details on
methodology and assumptions used in calculating the economic rates of return
are provided in Annex 8, together with results of the sensitivity analysis
applied.
B. Project Risks
6.05        The Project has risks.  While two-thirds of the micro-regions
would be in the higher rainfall areas (over 700 mm annually), the remaining
third would be located in areas with less rain. In these latter areas, the
introduction of irrigation, pasture improvement and water conservation works
would be emphasized to reduce farmer risks. There is some uncertainty with
regard to farmer response to new technical packages being field tested under
the Project for improving rainfed maize and bean production in these less favored
areas. Improved field demonstration closely tied with deepened extension efforts
under the Project would be emphasized under the Project to minimize this
uncertainty. Secondly, while on average the estimated rates of return at
micro-region level embody expectations about final outcomes that are reasonable
and realistic, nevertheless, the calibre of local leadership and staff involved
under the program will bear importantly on these outcomes. It would be
unrealistic to suppose that staff and leadership quality will be uniform.
So in some regions expectations may be disappointing, while in others
better than anticipated results should be achieved.  Again, however,
the PIDER coordinating mechanism has demonstrated a capacity to respond quickly
and flexibly to problems of execution, and the recently-established research
center (CIDER) will undertake major evaluation efforts as a basis for ongoing
adaptation of the program in the light of changing needs and circumstances.
Moreover, the Project would emphasize the activities of SRA-supported self-
help activities, and creation of community-based user's associations and
the development of local leadership capabilities in order to improve the
probability that the Project investments and services would eventually lead
to a self-sustaining development momentum at the local level.
6.06        These risks seem worth taking in the light of the anticipated bene-
fits under the Project that would accrue to low income people. To s-ummarize
these, farm incomes of about 120,000 farmers - accounting for more than a
660,000 total population with their family members - would approximately double
as a result of the Project. For those farmers benefiting from the
30,000 ha of irrigation, their incames may triple from current levels to full
development.  In addition, some 30,000 permanent jobs would be created,
mainly due to the expansion of small-scale irrigation systems.



'- Jsc -
Some 84,000 man-years of work would result from the construction activities
under the Project, a portion of which would benefit landless groups living in
the micro-regions. The Project would provide access to basic public services
in the 30 selected micro-regions. Some 170,000 people would directly benefit
from improved drinking water and from rural electrification. Furthermore,
some 60,000 children would get access to improved primary education and 750,000
persons to basic health services. The materials for self-help projects would
be made available to about 85,000   people in the selected micro-regions.
While for cost reasons it is not possible to fully service the micro-region
population with all public services, full health coverage would be provided in
three micro-regions on a pilot basis as part of the first-phase implementation
of the new national health plan.
6.07        Benefits from the Integrated Approach.  Finally, the realization
of a decentralized, integrated approack to development, on such a large scale,
is itself an important innovation in dealing with the problem of mass rural
poverty. Before PIDER, no mechanism for local-level coordination existed,
and agencies operated largely in isolation from each other. Prior to PIDER,
a number of government agencies had on-going development programs in the rural
areas, such as SRH, CFE and SOP. Each agency had developed its own criteria
for selecting target regions. As a result, there was little integration
among the efforts of various programs in rural roads, electrification, credit,
irrigation, extension and agrarian reform. Under PIDER, however, there is a
much closer liaison in both planning and especially execution among these
agencies in  order to fulfill the aim of PIDER of institutional coordination
of strengthened programs directed at sustaining a rural development process
for large numbers of rural poor. For example, SRA and extension services
are operating together in a micro-region, CFE and SRH coordinate the timing
of irrigation and electrification so that pumps are not installed and have to
wait for wiring and SOP and SAG are beginning to coordinate their labor-intensive
feeder road and soil conservation works so as not to be competing for labor
at the same time. CONASUPO is better able to schedule the routes of mobile
stores so as to coincide with SAG and SOP paydays in the micro-regions.
Finally, without the PIDER integrated approach, the efficiency of services,
such as extension, health, etc., would be significantly reduced.
6.08        Thus, the PIDER micro-region planning and execution process filled
a missing link - that of focusing overall agency direction and organization
around a realistic micro-region program of action. The preparation and sub-
sequent execution of such plans thius serve a catalytic function - causing
existing and new agency programs to-gel on an integrated basis. While these
qualitative benefits of PIDER are impossible to quantify ex ante, they are
some of the most important benefits to be expected from the Project. CIDER
and the Bank will be giving close attention to these aspects in its evalua-
tion work.



- 31 -
VII.  RECOMMENDATIONS
7.01        During negotiations, assurances were obtained from Government
that:
a. the criteria for directly productive activities outlined
from paras 3.12 to 3.21 would be followed;
b. productive support criteria described in paras 3.22 to
3.26 would be followed;
c. criteria for social infrastructure laid out in paras 3.28
to 3.31 would be followed;
d. the cost sharing given in para 3.33 would be followed;
e. the procurement procedures outlined in para 3.37 would
be followed;
f. PIDER and executing agencies would maintain separate
Project Accounts (para 3.39); and
g. an evaluation of Project progress would be carried out
(para 4.06).
7.02        With the indicated assurances, the proposed Project constitutes
a suitable basis for a Bank loan of US$110 million equivalent, for a term
of 25 years, including five years of grace.

MEXICO
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT - PIDER
SUMMARY APPRAISAL OF ZACAPOAXTLA (PUEBLA) MICRO-REGION
I. Background
The Zacapoaxtla micro-region is located in the northern, mountainous,
part of the State of Puebla. The sub-tropical climate is humid with rainfall
between 1,000 and 1,600 mm. About 74,000 people   live in seven"counties"
covering 596 square kilometers. The population density - 123 persons/km2 - is
among the highest in rural Mexico. The micro-region comprises 97 villages,
of which 90 have between 100 and 2,5DO inhabitants.
The main economic activity of Zacapoaxtla is rainfed agriculture.
The rainfall and soil conditions are adequate enough to grow such crops as
maize, beans, fruit and coffee. Only 2,000 ha, out of the 2h,000 ha avail-
able for agriculture and forestry are organized in ejidos. The remaining
22,000 ha are divided into 12,500 small landholdings averaging 1.8 ha.
Production techniques are extremely primitive. Slopes of 20 to 30% as well
as population density prevent the use of energy input other than human labor
and draft animals.
The economy is basically subsistence agriculture. Outside the
cities of Cuetzalan and Zacapoaxtla, which serve as market centers for the
entire area, exchange activities are restricted to a few items such as
soap, sugar, candles, salt and maize. Communications - especially from one
valley to another - are difficult if not impossible. Rural poverty is
apparent throughout the region. Underemployment is chronic and only
partially alleviated by migration out of the region between April and
October. The daily wage rate for unskilled labor during the harvest period
varies between US$0.50 and US$1.20.
About half of the population living in this micro-region have
received no schooling whatsoever and can be considered as illiterate.
There are only two high schools and only 31 complete primary schools, most
of which are located in the municipios of Zacapoaxtla and Cuetzalan. There
is no transportation from the rural areas to the schools located in those
two cities. No technical training in agricultural or handicraft skills is
available in the micro-region.
Communications problems are compounded by the existence of an
important group - 28% of total population - who speak only an indigenous
language - nahuatl or totonaco. The INI (Instituto Nacional Indigenista)



AINNEX 1 a
Page 2
is running a crash program of Spanish courses designed to prepare indigenous
children to enter primary school. The program has so far been held back by
the lack of bilingual teachers. The language problem goes certainly a long
way to explain the low efficiency of primary education in the micro-region.
The health conditions prevailing in Zacapoaxtla are below the
average of other rural areas in the country. The infant mortality rate is
close to 45 per thousand. There are only three health centers in the micro-
region and a fourth one is being built. None of them have means of trans-
portation. All other communities lack even the most elementary forms of
health care facilities.
II. Resources
In the Zacapoaxtla micro-region, neither the climate nor the soil
conditions are bottlenecks to an increase in agricultural output. The
ability of the region to take advantage of its potentialities rests on
relatively simple technological improvements such as planting corn and beans
at the appropriate time of the year, adopting better plan density and
fertilization levels and, more generally, turning to labor intensive cultiva-
tion as exemplified in the Plan Puebla experiments.
Farmers responsiveness to technological improvements can be expected
to be high since the initial levels of corn yields are half those of the Plan
Puebla area and population consciousness of its needs and pressure for a
better standard of living are high. Experiments carried out in 1973 by Plan
Puebla-Zacapoaxtla show preliminary results as good as, or better, than those
achieved by Plan Puebla. But Zacapoaxtla requires some additional techno-
logical ingredients, especially as regards to soil conservation.
In addition to traditional crops, new crops could be developed
without calling in for costly investments or production techniques. A good
example is the cultivation of potatoes which has recently been introduced
as a commercial crop in the southern half of the micro-region which has an
appropriate climate. Corn usually follows potatoes on the same land as a
second crop. This type of land use may actually double farmers' cash income.
The region has also potentialities for fruit-tree production which
could be developed into a commercial, labor-intensive activity. Research on
modern fruit tree production techniques other than traditional farm methods
could be carried out to develop several new fruit tree species (besides
avocados), that are not presently cultivated as commercial plantations.
Commercial fruit tree plantations would also contribute to better soil
conservation and may provide economical justification for additional invest-
ments as well as technical assistance.



ANNEX 1a
Page 3
III. Micro-Region Development Strategy
The overall development objectives of the Zacapoaxtla micro-region
are similar to those of other micro-regions eligible for PIDER investments.
They consist of increasing employment, production and income levels of the
populations concerned as cost-effectively as possible. This micro-region,
however, has a number of distinctive characteristics which can be summed up
as follows:
a. Extreme economic and cultural backwardness coexists
with the presence of a mostly indigenous population.
b. The medium-term future lies in intensification of
rainfed agricultural activities as few other
resources exist.
c. The region has two "exports"1: coffee and labor.
d. The infrastructure level is below any reasonable
standards.
These features call for additional cautiousness in the provision
of investments. The difficulties to ensure local coordination and efficient
use of investments will be greater in Zacapoaxtla than elsewhere. Technical
assistance thus receives a major focus in the regionts development program.
The development strategy tailored to meet the needs of the
Zacapoaxtla micro-region thus gives a high priority to (i) increasing
production through better cultivation techniques of current crops and
introducing new marketable crops such as potatoes and fruits; (ii) bridging
the infrastructure gap, starting with feeder roads in order to connect all
communities with the market towns of Zacapoaxtla and Cuetzalan; and
(iii) providing the kind of technical assistance required to coordinate
investments, ensure the propagation of technical innovations and enlist
communitiest participation.
Agricultural Intensification. A major thrust of the strategy is
intensification of rainfed crop production through an adaption of Plan
Puebla experience to the conditions of the Zacapoaxtla region. Plan
Zacapoaxtla, a program of technical assistance to farmers, was designed to
achieve these objectives. The main thrust of this Plan is (i) to carry out
research programs on the crops of the micro-region (corn, bean, potatoes)
as well as on fruit production, (ii) to implement technological innovations
and (iii) to promote the use of credit to finance the inputs required by
farmers willing to improve their technology. This Plan, which has replaced
SAG's traditional extension services in the area, will also act as a
coordinator of the investments made by this agency in the field of soil
conservation, supplementary irrigation, fruit production and livestock
improvement centers. The ultimate goal oL Plan Zacapoaxtla, which will
benefit heavily from the experience gained under Plan Puebla, is to increase
yields in the following manner:

in order to enable them to apply for financial assistance from agricultural

among cooperating farmers' own plots to test fertilization levels, plant
density and varieties of each crop.

In terms of extension, the goal of Plan Zacapoaxtla is to reach
25% of the corn-growing area in four years.  In the light of the experience
gained under Plan Puebla, where it took six years to reach 15% of the
population - or to cover 20,000 ha - this objective may appear as overly
ambitious.  However, the target population - 12,000 producers and 10,200 ha
of corn - is smaller in this case. Furthermore, Plan Zacapoaxtla is
expected to draw heavily from the experience accumulated under Plan Puebla.
IV. Micro-Region Investment Program
Owing to the aforementioned characteristics of the Zacapoaxtla
micro-region the investment program designed to implement the development
strategy is balanced between the three categories.

Page 5

Directly Productive /      32.1    15.2   19.0   24.8  51.5  614.2
Production Support         31.1    25.0   39.5   40.9   17.8   23.1
Social Infrastructure      33.6    59.8   40.7   30.4   1565   5.5
Project Administration      3.1      -      o.8    3.9    5.2    7.1
2/ Credit would be additional
The rough topography and the low educational level of the micro-
region call for additional investment in feeder roads and agricultural
extension, thus boosting the share of productive support investment in total
investment. Additional investments are also required to finance
supporting social infrastructure, In the area of productive investment, the
climatic conditions render the financing of sophisticated irrigation schemes
unnecessary, but strong population pressure coupled with scarcity of crop-
land require many micro-investments, especially soil conservation works
designed to facilitate the shift towards more intensive use of land.
The relative shares of directly productive, productive support and
social infrastructure in the five-year investment program designed for the
Zacapoaxt'a micro-region vary over the time period of the development plan.
The first two years of implementation of the program show that it has been
easier to deliver productive support and social infrastructure investment
than directly productive investment. This phasing of investment is justified
in the view that the latter type of investment is unlikely to yield benefits
unless physical communications and educational levels are first improved
through roads, education and extension. As a consequence, the share of
directly productive investments will increase in the coming years as other
types of investments are gradually phased out.
Municipalities not only have a role in identifying vi'llage needs
but can also serve as catalysts to foster farmers' organizations. In the
past, municipalities helped to set up water and soil conservation organiza-
tions as well as credit associations. They are instrumental in defining
local contributions to PIDER investments and act as intermediaries to collect
such contributions. For instance, soil conservation terraces built in 1974
incorporated a local contribution in labor from the beneficiaries equivalent
to about 10% of their costs. This labor contribution from beneficiaries
could be larger if municipalities were more adequately briefed by representa-
tives of federal agencies about the notential benefits to be derived from
such investments as well as about the overall strategy envisaged for the
micro-region. In this area of fruit production, for instance, the fruit
tree saplings distributed in 1973 were planted after the primitive terrace
construction were prepared by the peasants themselves, and cared for with
very little or no technical assistance.



ANNEX la
Page 6
V. Local Level Organization and Participation
For example, at the local level, municipal presidents are
indispensable channels of information for federal agencies. In one area
at least, the municipio of Xochiapulco, their knowledge of the problems of
the village proved to be a valuable ingredient to the planning process.
The president of this village, a former school teacher, showed substantial
interest in the program and complained about some of its deficiencies. He
was obviously a respected leader and had a highly analytical approach to
local problems of production, marketing and supplies of agricultural inputs
(especially fertilizers) and consumer's goods. This example may suggest
that true community leaders can very well share some of the investment
decisions and responsibilities provided that representatives of all public
agencies are capable and, indeed, willing to incorporate their views into
the decision-making process.
VI. Benefits
There is little doubt that the Zacapoaxtla micro-region is one of
the poorest of all micro-regions eligible for financing under the PIDER
program. The economic strategy outlined above to help the country to make
the transition from a subsistence, self-consumption economy to a market-
oriented economy relies on rainfed, labor-intensive agriculture. In this
connection, although the potentialities of the micro-region look promising,
their implementation implies drastic shifts in land use and production
techniques. Whether or not such shifts will take place in due time depends,
to a large extent, on the amount and quality of technical assistance
provided to farmers. This is why the ability of the technical assistance
team appointed under Plan Zacapoaxtla to reach small farmers will be a
decisive factor in the success or failure of the whole investment program.
It is also clear that the proposed investment program of US$8.58
million during the 1973-77 period is not intended to support an inward-looking
type of development. This micro-region is simply too poor to live in economic
autarky and no amount of investment can actually relax such a basic constraint
as the deteriorating population/land ratio. The micro-region needs the out-
side world not only to market its coffee and fruit production but as an outlet
for its surplus labor. In this respect, the sole ambition of the proposed
investment package is to stop the continuous deterioration of the employment
picture.
T22loyment. The investment Drogram described above is expected to
have a sizeable impact on the temporary employment situation of the micro-region.
It is clear, however, that neither the temporary employment arlsing fr r, the
irnvestment program nor the permanent additional employment resulting from
shlifts towards more efficient land use and more intensive agriculture will
be sufficient to solve the very severe employment problem of this micro-
region. By and large, it is estimated that the total employment generated
cy the program will just offset the increasing labor ar.ailability due to
p L1alationi rpressure.



ANNEX 1a
Page 7
All in all, residual unemployment per family will decrease from
1 .05 man-years before the launching of the program to 0.96 man-years when
the program is completed.
As far as feasibility is concerned, past performance demonstrates
that all the public agencies concerned have the capability to deliver the
investment required by the program in due time. Clearly, some agencies are
better organized than others   SSA, for instance, needs to clarify its
policies in the health sector. NIT has yet to show that it can run the
physical facilities built by other agencies in an efficient manner. Although
initial difficulties caused delays to start work in some areas, the mission
believes that, by and large, the overall ability to implement planned invest-
merits can be rated as satisfactory.
't was noted that this ability is clearly above the average as far
as feeder roads and social infrastructure investment are concerned. In the
coming years, the effort of the coordination should focus more decisively on
directly productive investment. To deliver such investment in a coordinated
manner will be a difficult task owing to the backwardness of the micro-
region. This is, nevertheless, the inescapable and crucial test that PIDER
has now to face in the Zacapoaxtla micro-region.

RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SUMMARY APPRAISAL OF SUR DE NUEVO LEON MICRO-REGION
Background
Duality Between State and Micro-Region. The Sur de Nuevo Leon micro-region
(Surnel) is located in the southern part of the state of Nuevo Leon. Its
population and economic activity is concentrated in Monterrey, the state
capital. Nuevo Leon has a total populafion of 1,695,000 inhabitants, of
which 78% live in the Monterrey metropolitan area. Furthermore, 82% of the
state labor force is employed in the industrial and service sectors, which
together contribute substantially to the state average family income of
US$1,815 per year.
In contrast, the micro-region is characterized by a subsistence agriculture
economy which comprises 80% of the economically active population and 7% of the
state's total population. Surnel's backwardness and its duality with Monterrey
are reflected in an equivalent 60% family unemployment rate and average family
income of US$825 per year.
Micro Region Selection Criteria. Though Nuevo Leon has two other areas that
are potential candidates for the PIDER program, Surnel is clearly the state's
poorest micro-region.
Surnel's Resource Base. Surnel's relative,y low family income levels are
characteristic of its five muncipalitiesl and its 113,000 inhabitants. Their
poverty is largely explained by an under-utilized resource base. Specifically,
the area's development bottlenecks have consisted primarily of a shortage of
water (average rainfall of 500 mm per year), of soils that are typical of an
arid area, and of the absence of basic physical infrastructure, productive
inputs and adequate technical assistance. However, Surnel's development resource
potential is comprised of 103,000 ha of cultivable area (for corn, beans and
fruit production) of whibh 10% represents an irrigated area. Both rainfed
and irrigated agriculture production have yet to be fully exploited by the
area's 14,500 ejidatarios and 3,000 small private farmers. Furthermore, Surnel's
500,000 ha of grazing land and iO2.000 head of eattle offer an already existing base
for an improved cattle and pasture management program that would make possible
1/ A municipality is equ_3vaIi-j- J1o a county that has dist'nct geo-political
divisiors .wi T:hL a state



Arimex 0
Page 2
a significant increase in the marketable quantity of livestock prodcLltiun.
Objectives and Strategy. The PIDER project is a direct response to lessening
the dualities between Surnel and the rest of the state and to Surnel's
underutilized productive potential.  Specificel;-, the miero-region progrr
objectivres are to:
(a) increase levels of income and employment;
(b)  improve social infrastructure and services; and
(c)  promote state and local participation in development decision-
making.
The strategy devised to achieve these goals is through:
-   (a)  a "big-push" and "growth pole" approach to providing the directly
productive physical infrastructure and conmplementary productive
support investments in order to imore productively exploit
Surnel's existent and potential resource base.
(b)  the creation of temporary employment opportunities during the
gestation period of the investments;
(c) the provision of social infrastructure investments that
would lessen the gaps between the state and Surnel;
(d) establishment of an organizational base (e.g., coordination
of agencies, of ongoing technological research results with
a competent extension service, and the promotion of local
participation) to efficiently oversee PIDER's allocations.
Investment Program. Based on a sound development strategy and a micro-regional
resource profile, the State Coordinating Group has carefully formulated an
integrated investment program. It calls for the investment of M$152.2 million
(US$12.2) over the period 1973-19760 Of this total: (i) 47% will be invested
in directly productive infrastructure whereby livestock and irrigated related
projects comprise 86% of this amount. The remainder of this category will be
allocated for soil and water conservation works, fruit-tree production, and
rural industries. (ii) 28% will be appropriated for productive support, items,
roughly distributed even among feeder roads, agricultural research and extension
service, and farmer organization. (iii) 22% is earmarked for social infra-
structure, with a particular emphasis on providing drinking water facilities.
Through a favorably "equity-efficiency" investment and scheduling balance,
the project:
(a) will have widespread impact bydirectJuybenfiting 67% of
Surnel's population (US$145 per beneficiary);
(b) for the most part, has the type and extent of investments that
are designated in areas and communities according to the suit-
ability of their resource potential;

Prge 3
(c) provides assurance that the public works labor require-
menrts do not conflict with labor avilability.
In addition, ongoing applied research is developing technological packages that
are appropriate to Surnel's various eological areas.
Organization and Management. On the basis of mission field visits and
subsequent evaluation, the outstanding feature of the Surnel project is the
State Coordinating Techmical Group. The resourcefulness of its staff and
the decentralization of funds have given this group the power and influence
to command the respect and cooperation of the 13 federal and state partici-
pating agencies. Furthermore, the Group's philosophical approach (i.e.
encouraging self-determination of beneficiaries throagh local participation in
project planning and execution) is largely responsible for the target population's
enthusiastic support for the Project. Their favorable attitude towards self-
development has been confirmed by an attitudinal study undertaken by the
Group's sociologist.
Benefits and Justification. The ejido model developed to be representative
of SurnelI has served as the basis for projecting the project's mnicro regional
benefits and costs.  Under varying cost assurmptions, the financial rate of
return ranges from 12.15% to 7.95%, while the economic rate of return spans
from 13.40 to 10.10.

1EXICO
RWUi.LI DEVELOPITNT PROJECT
COSALA-EIJOTA, 3S-ALOA MICOR-fEGI'NT
SUMMARY
Introduction
1.        The Cosala-Elota micro-region covers three counties having a
total area of 5,6h0 km2. The micro-region straddles two topographical
regions:  the coastal plain and the western slopes of the Sierra Madre
Occidental. The county of Cosala is wholly in the mountainous area, Elota
is entirely on the coastal plain and San Ignacio is about equally divided
between coastal plain and mountainous terrain.
2.        The micro-region has a populption of 55,000 people who are among
the poorest inhabitants of the prosperous agricultural state of Sinaloa.
Average family income is about US$500 (as compared to US$800 for ejido
families in irrigated districts and over US$1,000 in urban areas); 52% of
all families have annual incomes below US$479 and only 10% have incomes
exceeding US$960. The absence of irrigation (and of the services associated
with it) is the major cause for the relative poverty of the micro-region.
Low income levels in turn prevent the populatiorn from improving its living
condition through the self-financing of economic and social infrastructure.
The Micro-Region
3.        The micro-region is located half-way between Culiacan and Mazatlan,
from the Pacific to the border of the state of Durango. It has a significant
productive potential which could be realised through a more rational use of
the areas major resources; the labor force and the land. The micro-region
has an economically active population of about 15,000 of which about 12,000
are in agriculture. This labor force is fully employed for only about 100
days at the peak of the agricultural cycle 1/. Since on-farm employment is
often insufficient to yield a reasonable income, many ejidatarios from the
highlands migrate seasonally in search of work in the irrigated valleys and
plains. Some off-farm employment is available in the coastal part of the
micro-region where small areas totalling 3,000-4,000 ha are irrigated.
PIDER's justification for investing in the micro-region is based on the fact
that ecological conditions are favorable enough so that even without wide-
spread irrigation, higher incmes are possible. Except for a narrow coastal
strip which gets little rain (300 mm) but is suitable for irrigation, rainfall
in the micro-region averages 700 mm per year and may reach 1,200 mm in certain
areas.
1/ Underemployment on a year round basis averages 40-45% of the available
labor force: it varies from 20-25% in the coastal areas where the small
irrigated area is concentrated to 60% in Cosala which is entirely in
the highlands.



ANNEX _C
Page 2
4.        The other major factor of production which is also underemployed
is land.  Almost 300,000 ha of land, roughly 40 ha per family, are available
for development (of these, 40,000 ha could be used for agriculture and the
rest is suitable for orchards and/or grazing). Development of this land
could result in a ten-fold increase in exploited land per family (up from
the present 4-5 ha). Herd management and pasture improvement would result
in a large increase in the livestock population and the off-take rate and
would assist in erosion control.
5.        The Cosala-Elota micro-region is broadly representative
of three more micro-regions (within Sinaloa) at various stages of
implementation. Computations based on a representative integrated ejido
development model yielded a financial rate of return of 20.6 and an.economic
rate of return of 14.75% 2/.  In order that this potential be realized,
the basic infrastructure must be made available but access to ancillary
services such as credit, extension and distribution of modern farm inputs
is just as crucial.
Development Objectives and Strategy
6.        PIDER has four main goals in this micro-region: (i) increase incomes;
(ii) raise the level of on- and off-farm employment; (iii) improve the quality
of rural life; and (iv) diminish the importance of rural-urban migration 2/.
A three-pronged basic strategy was devised to reach these goals. First, to
promote a gradual increase in the amount of land under crops, improved pastures
and orchards, with irrigation whenever feasible. Second, to increase produc-
tivity (in crops and animal production) in presently exploited areas and in
newly incorporated ones through agricultural extension and training, credit,
marketing services and the use of modern farm inputs. Third, to create
temporary employment of two to three years' duration (and to promote savings
out of these earnings) in order to bridge the gap between the initiation of
productive projects and the time they start yielding their fruits. This
temporary employment will diminish seasonal migration and create useful
infrastructure (such as roads, soil and water conservation countours, etc.,
in addition to directly productive infrastructure).
7.        Among the six micro-regions appraised in June 1974, Cosala-Elota is
the one with the highest existing income levels. This fact may explain why
the population tended to insist on social infrastructure rather more strongly
than was the case elsewhere. In part for this reason, the first two years
of PIDER were relatively weighed in favor of productive support and social
infrastructure, while initiation of projects with longer gestation periods
(e.g., irrigation) were also started.
1/ A statistical profile of the micro-region is included, together with a
summary of the PIDER investment plan.
2/ Permanent out migration from the micro-region usually starts as seasonal
migration; many families just never return to their (highland) villages.



ANNEX 1C
Page 3
8.        In the mission's view, the strategy is adequate and reflects
grosso modo, the possibilities of the micro-region.
PIDER Investment Plan and Expected Results
9.        The strategy described above is presently being implemented
through the micro-region investment plan. It calls for the investment of
US$10.6 million over the period 1973-77. Of this total, 52% will be
invested in directly productive infrastracture such as livestock, irrigated
crops and fruit trees, and beekeeping, 28% will be for productive support
facilities such as roads and marketing outlets, and 19% will be for social
infrastructure such as schools, drinking water and village electrification.
Program coordination will require 1% of the total. The fulfillment of the
directly productive share of the investment plan will require an additional
US$5.5 million in long-term development credit over the period 1973-79 and
US$12.2 million in incremental short-term production credit over the period
1973-80. This investment program will directly increase the incomes of
approximately 2,800 families living in 30 ejidos (out of the 90-95 ejidos
existing in the micro-region). The total number of people directly benefited
(including about 1,000 non-ejido smallholders) will be 14-15,000 and about
1,000 new permanent jobs will be created at full development (in 7-8 years)
excluding temporary emplo-yment during the investment period and recurring
part-time labor needs frequently encountered in agro-pastoral activities.
Organization and Management
10.       In order to implement the Plan, PIDER developed an organizational
structure consisting of federal and state officials and local leaders (of
municipios and ejidos).  Its role is to implement the Plan, supervise, co-
ordinate and monitor its execution and train and motivate peasants and their
leaders to participate. The Vocal Ejecutivo is the chief executive officer
of the Comite Estatal (State Coordinating Committee) under the ex-officio
chairmanship of the Governor. The Vocal is assisted by a Grupo Tecnico
(Technical Support Groap) hired, trained and paid by PIDER. The Technical
Support Group is appointed to conduct the day-to-day tasks of supervision
and coordination in conjunction with Dependencia employees and local leaders.
Local leadership was fairly heavily involved in the wnole process, an ex-
ceptional state of affairs. It was the mission's impression that the State
Committee was functioning well and that adequate supervision and coordination
was taking place. The interest and the involvement of the local population 1/,
together with the excellent quality of state level leadership, are significant
factors making for a high likelihood of successful Plan,implementation.
a
1/ The fact that the population is relatively well educated and motivated
compared to other micro-regions makes the economic loss from its low
employment all the more significant.

RURAL DEVELOPIENT PROJECT
SIUR DE YUCATAN MfNICR0-REGI0N
SUMMY
Introduction
1.        The Sur de Yucatan micro-region has 25,000 inhabitants, most;y
Maya speaking, (see Map IBRD, 1418),  spread over an area of 4,730 km  of
which only a small fraction is ceiared and under crops. Slash and burn
shifting agriculture is practised using planting sticks, the same techniques
employed by their ancestors 1,000 years ago--and with just about the same
results, namelr, 600-700 kg of maize per hectare.  Family incemes average
only US$385 per year with only 10% of the families having annual incomes
exceeding US$500 in the micro-region.
The Micro-Region
2.        The micro-region is located at the southwestern edge of the state
of Yucatan. It lies outside the economic center of the state which spans
the Merida-Ticul-Valladolid triangle dedicated almost exclusively to the
cultivation of henequen. The micro-region has good rainfall (1,000-1,200 mm
*of which 65% falls in the June-October rain season), a relatively high
water table (that would require careful management to prevent the upward
rise of the underlying salt water table), and large quantities of potentially
productive land. Only 6-8% of the land owned by ejidos and smallholders is
cleared. The rest is under medium to heavy forest. It is ironical that
the lands now under cultivation are not the best lands in the area, due to
access problems. The better lands are located farther to the south (20 km)
of the Merida-Chetumal highway along which most of the micro-regional
population lives. Improved accessibility to the southern part of the micro-
region could result in a tenfold increase in cleared land per family (up
from the present 1.7 ha/family) either under crops, fruit trees or grazing.
3.        The economically active population of the micro-region numbers
about 6,600 people, of which 80% are engaged in subsistence agriculture even
though 45% of the population is considered urban (the largest towns in the
micro-region being Peto, 8,000 inhabitants and Tzucacab,,4,000). This means
that "?urban"t agglomerations are merely clusters of housing for peasants
working fields nearby. Eighty percent of the families of the area have
incomes below TTS$479 per year. Almost 60% of the adult population is
illiterate, only 48% of school-age children are enrolled and most.adults



ANNEX 1D
Page 2
speak only the Maya language. A long-range plan to assist the socio-
economic development of the area underlies the work that the National
Institute for Indigenous Affairs (INI) has been carrying out in the past
10 years. INI was the only government agency active in the area until
PIDER was launched in mid-1973 1/. This case illustrates the past neglect
of areas with development potential.
4.        As suggested by the small amount of land in productive use, the
majority of the population is almost entirely idle during seven months of
the year. The absence of employment opportunities at reasonable distances
from the micro-region means that very little off-farm work can be found and
this explains the very low income levels of 80% of the population.
5.        Local forests (which until recently were worthless) are likely to
acquire an econaoic value as land clearing progresses and hundreds of kilometers
of fences are being built.
6.        The micro-region is both a poor area and one with a significant
potential for sustained economic growth. The Sur de Yucatan micro-region
composite index of micro-region selection. The Sur de Yucatan micro-region
is broadly representative of four other micro-regions now operating in the
Peninsula (2 in Campeche, 1 in Quirntana Roo and an additional one, Litoral
Oeste, in Yucatan state). Computations based on a representative ejido
development model indicate that a good potential for development exists:
the model yielded a financial rate of return of 18.8% and an economic rate
of 18.85%.
Development Objectives and Strategy
7.        Since the micro-regioni is inhabited by Maya communities whose
poverty stems in good measure from isolation and cultural-historical causes,
(e.g., three centuries of virtual enslavement under colonial rule and a nearby
genocidal war waged against them by the 19th century landed elites), the
almost entirely economic goals of PTDER have had to be complemented with
some of the social goals implicit in TNI's mandate.  INI takes the view that
economic and socio-cultural progress can only be introduced simultaneously.
This is why INT (which has been compared to a "mini-PIDER") conducts both
economic and socio-cultural programs.  INI provides credit and technical
assistance to the farmers for orchards, livestock or beekeeping, as well
as teaching children to read and write simultaneously in the Maya and Spanish
languages. For this reason, the training aspects are emphasized by PIDER
(about 50% of all productive support investment is for training and a sig-
nificant share of it will be channeled through INI technical assistance),
in addition to the normal economic goals. The economic goals of PIDEP. are
to significantly increase the amount of land under production, thereby
increasing local employment and doubling family income within seven or
eight years.
1/ In 1969/70, SRH conducted a study for the diversification of Yucatan
agriculture and it identified the southern portions of Tzucacab and Peto
as suitable for large-scale irrigated and rainfed agriculture, orchards
and grazing. These lands are included in the micro-region.



ANNEX 1 D
Page 3
8.        The strategy adopted to reach the above goals could be
summarized as follows:
(a)  Since the pcpuilation's incomie level is so low, it
was estimated that its interest and participation
could best be ensured by a mix of investments heavy
with productive projects in the first years of the
Program.  That was started off by TINI-led beekeepirg
and fruit tree projects and followed closely by SAG-
built livestock units. All three programs have been
successful and initial fears and mistrust hpve been
allayed in most communities.
(b)  At the same time as incomes are being increased by
the introduction of new activities (especially grazing
and fruit production), technical assistance and training
will be supplied.
(c)  Only after the bases for self-sustained growth have been
established will social infrastructure receive some
em,phasis.
Investment Program and Expected Results
9.        In order to carry out the above strategy, an investment plan was
designed and is presently being implemented. It calls for the investment
of US$7.9 million during 1973-77. Of this total, 55% will be for directly
productive and 33% will be for productive support investments,  Nine percent
will be for social infrastructure and 3% for progran coordination. As devised
in the strategy, productive and productive support investments will amount to
89% and 84% of the total in Years 1 and 2, while social investment will be
only 7% in Year 1 and will be increased to 14i and 11% respectively in Years 2
and 3. The emphasis of this investment plan is clearly laid on the directly
productive investments.
1C.       The productive investment will require US$3.43 million in medium-
and long-term development credits and US$1.65 million in incremental short-
term operating credits between 1975 and 1980/81.
11.       The project will directly increase the incomes of about 2,000 families
livino in 25 ejidos. It will directly benefit about 12,000 people and create,
at full development, about 700 full-time permanent jobs in addition to tempo-
rarn employment during the investment period and part-time labor requirements
of the productive activities.
Organization and Management
12.       To carry out the investmient program the follo-wing administrative
structure was formed: a federal agency at the state level, the Conite
Promotor del DesarroLlo Economico y Social de Yucatan (COPRODEY), coordinates
and supervises all federal investments in the state 1/. 'he Deputy Director
of COPRODEY is the acting Vocal Ejecutivo and therefore the Technical
1/  Indications are that this organizational structure will be adopted by
-   most other states eventually.

Director of PIDER for the state. As in other states, he is assisted by
a Technical Support Group to oversee the day-to-day operations of PIDER.
Some personality conflicts have arisen occasionally between the acting
Vocal and some of the federal agencies, but assistance from PIDER head-
quarters effectively circumscribed any possible ill effects and the invest-
menrt programn suffered no harm. A new PIDER Director is to be appointed
soon and the problem will be resolved entirely.
13.       The low socio-economic level of the population makes ejido level
participation difficult, but the evidence is that efforts are being made to
remedy this situation. Encouraging signs are clear--as successful programs
(beekeeping and livestock units) are being imitated everywhere 1/.
14.       The problem of local participation admits of no ove-right solution:
the local and ejido leaders will have tc be taught to participate. Since
many local leaders of necessity have been involved in PIDER, the investment
program will graduate a crop of relatively experienced and motivated leaders
whose example Tqill be followed by others.
An actual PIDEPR prepared analysis of Ejido Sabecani/ was submitted
in late 1974 and is summarized below:
1.   Physical Characteristics
The ejido is located in the southern zone of the State of Yucatan.
Its area is 2,375 ha., of which 2,109 ha. are forest or mountain,
16.5 ha. are urban, and 250 ha. of rainfed arable land.
In the forest/mountain zone two projects are planned: a 300 ha.
livestock unit and a 40 ha. irrigation unit for annual crops (Soy,
Beans, Jamapa).
The climate is sub-humid tropical (Koepper classification A.W.G.)
with rain from June to October. The average temperature is 26.20C.
The average yearly rainfall is 1,053mm.
The soil is predominantly AK'ALCHE, with an average depth of 1 meter.
The soils have a high clay content (coluvial).  The color varies from
dark grey to black.
1/ Some ejidos turned down SAG offers for a 300 ha livestock unit 18 months
ago. The only conditions were: (a) collective exploitation and (2)
assumption of a bank loan for livestock. Several ejidos are now
negotiating with the banks for loans for the entire cost of the unit,
frcm land clearing to the purchase of animals--on a collective base
as that is a condition for bank credit.



AtNEX JD
Page 5
2.   Human Resources
The population of the ejido is 185, composed of 41 families. The
whole of the population works in agriculture. The income levels are
low with average yearly expenses being about US$385.
3.   Infrastructure Works
The ejido is reached from the Merida-Chetumal highway by a track
to the Tzucacab village. 5.8 km. of this were done by hand. There is
a pump for taking water from a well. One teacher provides the first
three years of primary education. There is a basketball court, a house
for the Comisario Bjidal, and a health post is under construction.
There is no electricity.
.   Production Characteristics
Corn is grown on the arable land (600 kg/ha. yield). Beans (frijol)
are grown on one ha. (25 kg/ha. yield).  Pumpklns we grown on It ha. (25
sacos/ha.).
Agriculture is rainfed, and is done through the rudimentary,
traditional method of ( slash and burn). Neither improved seeds,
fertilizers, nor fungicides are used.
5.   PIDER and the Sabecan Ejido
In 1973 PIDER began a socioeconomic development plan in a large
rural area in the southern part of Yucatan. The objectives were to
increase family incomes, increase production, and lead to an improved
social welfare.
A 5.6 million peso (.45 million dollars) investment program over
4 years was created, of which 81% goes to productive investments and
19% to supportor social infrastructure.  The specific investment types
are shown in the attached table.
Over the four year investment program the unskilled labor require-
ments generated are calculated to be 29,498 man-days. (24 man years per year).
a)   Livestock Development
300 ha. of the mountain/forest zone will be developed. Guinea
grass will be planted on 260 ha. of rainfed land. African Star Grass
will be planted on the remaining 40 ha. These will be irrigated by
means of a 50 meter deep well to be dug. The herd will be beg-un in
year 4 with the acquisition of 43 heiffers and 43 steers. The herd
will stabilize at year 10. At first, the herd will be in a fattening
period, later to be converted to a weaning one, with a yearly sale
of 107 male calves and 39 female ones. Once stabilized the rainfed
grasses give one A.U. per ha. and the irrigated grasses 6 A.U. per ha.



ANMEX 1D
Page 6
b)   Bee Unit
A bee unit will be established with the purchase of hives and
the construction of fencing and other supporting materials.
The bees would produce honey, wax and create a nucleus for new
bee hives, whether for this ejido or others.
Extension
c)        fIte 'technical group required for covering the technical assistance
requirement of 5 ejidos in the same zone, consists of an agrarian
engineer, a vetinarian, two agricultural technicians and the necessary
vehicles. The cost per ha. is US$13.
d)   Road
To create access to the track to Tzucacab it is necessary to
complete, in years 1 and 2, a road constructed with labor-intensive
methods at a cost of US$4O,ooo.
e)   Electrification
Electrification is programmed for year 3 to meet the necessities
of the ejido and for the irrigation pumps. The cost is
approximately Us$4O,000.
f)   House for Comisario Ejidal
A house for the ejido representative is to be built in
year 1 with a cost of US$1,250 only in materials.
g)   Basketball Court
A basketball court was built with an investment of
US$730 in materials only.
h)   Health Post
A health post is under construction at a cost of U $3,000.
It is assumed that a doctor will visit once a week, but at the
present time, people have to travel 22 kms to obtain health
care.
6.   Financial Projection
The following conclusions were reached through analyzing the
financial projections. The initial ejido participation in the works
financed by PIDER is 20% of the total cost. The productive investments
are amortized over a period of 20 years with 5 years of grace. In
the development loan, the ejido will provide 10% repid over a 15 year
period, with 2 years of grace. The financial rate of return of the
investments (PIDER and development loans) is 16%.

MEXICO
RTJRAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
EL LLANO (AGUASCALIEWTES) MiICRO-REGION SUMMARY
Background
Tocated almost at the geograohical center of Mexico,
Aguascalientes is one of the four smallest states in the country. The entire
state's 5,590 square kilometers is in the semi-arid ecological zone; average
annual rainfall in the state's nine municipalities ranges from 400 mm to nearly
600 mm.
The state population in 1970 was 338,000, of which 200,000 live in the
state capital of Aguascalientes, the state's only city. This urban-centered
population also accounts for 55.5% of the state's economically active population
that is engaged in the industrial and service sectors. Clothesmaking, embroidery
products, and vineyards are Aguascalientes' primary activities, whose workers
comprise the major portion of the 50%0 of the state's working population that earn
incomes more than US $500 per year.
The Micro-Rezion: In contrast, the El Llano micro-region has four
municipalities and 75,000 rural inhabitants. Over 75% of this area's economically
active population is involved in agricultural-related activities. This orientation
may also account for El Llano's relatively low standard of living, whereby 77%
of the working population earn less than US $500 per year.
Selection of the Micro-Region:  The four municipalities couprising the
micor-region known as El Llano -- Aguascalientes, Asientos, Cosio, and Tepazala --
are located in the eastern part of the state. From the point of view of a
sizeable and relatively homogeneous target area and group, the EL Llano micro-
region area is the state's most appropriate delineated area that is consistent
with PIDER selection criteria.
El Llano's Resource Base: The micro-region's relative poverty is explained
primarily by underutilized natural and human resources. Only 122,000 ha., or
22% of the micro-region's total land surface is under cultivation -- primarily
by corn, beans, alfalfa, and fruit-trees (i.e. grapes). Though the northern areas
are classified as "semi-desert" with little possibility for increasing the amount
of arable land and crop yields, the central and southern portions of the micro-
region offer substantial potential for increasing the crop area and yields.
In particular, El Llano's already existing 19,000 ha. of irrigated area
(surface and underground), or 8% of total land surface, is located primarily in
this sub-region. However, it is reported to be inefficiently utilized through
excess application of water, both per irrigation and with respect to the number of
irrigations used on certain crops. Neverthless, though non-traditional technolocy
is used by a limited number of ejidatarios, who have obtained reported corn yields
of 2.5 to 3 tons per ha, there seems to exist a comparatively favorable base for
an increased irrigated area and for the further promotion of yield increasing
technology.



ANNEX le
Page 2
Another important mismanaged resource is El Llano's 170,000 head of
bo-ine cattle, of which 40% represent dairy cattle. There are cattle on
almost all of the ejidos, but only 43% of the ejidatarios own cattle, and on
only about one-half the ejidos does livestock represent an important part
of the economy.  However, serious overgrazing, insufficient supplemented feeding,
and inadequate technical service constrains the area's livestock activity.
EL Llano's second underutilized factor of production is its labor force.
Approximately 80% are principally subsistence farmers, with an average of 17 ha
per farmer. In terms of employment levels, this population is productively
active only for an annual average of 120 days per farmer, with the remainder
of the year remaining almost entirely idle. However, given El Llano's relatively
abundant off-farm employment opportunities and its good road network, the labor
resources can be more fully utilized through appropriate labor mobilization
measures. These could be coordinated through DAAC personnel. The micro-region
is endowed with a relatively literate population (85% of the population more
than 10 years of age) and a high primary school enrollment ratio (75% of school
are children). Furthermore, an agricultural secondary school in the El Llano
sub-region promises to be of substantial benefit to a wide area.
Finally, the micro-region has an ample road network averaging nearly
100 meters per square km. that can potentially accelerate the more rational
use of El Llano's natural and human resource base.
PIDER Ob.jectives and Strategy
The overall PIDER objectives in EL Llano are to:
a.  increase permanent employment in agriculture and industry;
b.  raise labor productivity and hence income levels of its rural
population;
c.  make technicians, and subsequently the farmers, aware of the need
to embark on a novel and more flexible approach to development,
based on a deeper and better coordinated problem analysis.
Despite the micro-region's small overall size, the strategy to accomplish
the above objectives is tailored to the specific characteristics and problems of
each of the three sub-regions:
a.  Sub-region 1 (Pabellon irrigation district):  The plan in this area
is to introduce more yield increasing technology and water saving
practices, and to create more agricultural employment opportunities,
among both subsistence farmers and entrepreneurial producers.
Specifically, this high resource potential area is suitable for
greater shifts to labor-intensive, high value crops, such as peaches
and grapes, peppers, potatoes and garlic, as well as alfalfa for



ANNEX Ile
Page 3
expanding the already important dairy industry. Furthermore, it
is appropriate to increase industrial and handicraft employment
in agro-industries and clothesmaking and embroidery, for which
the latter already has a sizeable pool of skills, to be augmented
by special training programs.
b.  Sub-region 2 (El Llano):  Efforts in this area are concentrated on
raising rainfel crop and livestock yields to provide something more
than subsistence levels to those farmers who will not benefit from
irrigation to be achieved through (i) further water exploration and
well drilling, (ii) soil and water conservation works and small
earth dams to store rainwater for cattle and irrigation, and (iii)
constructing the missing social infrastructure through providing
primarily drinking water and electricity.
c. Sub-region 3 (Tepazala, Cosio and Asientos): A similar strategy
applies here as in sub-region 2, but where the potential results
are constrained by lesser knowledge regarding underground water and
by poorer soils and climate. Some hope is expressed for the
development of mineral resources for the benefit of the ejidos on
which they are located.
In general, the above situation is suitable to achieving the PIDER
objectives through a careful consideration of the micro-regions's varied needs
and potential. However, two important strategy questions remain to be resolved:
(i) the equity aspects of the irrigation development; and (ii) the relative
long-run economies of dairying as compared with tree fruit and grape production.
PIDER Investment Program and Expected Results
The strategy described above is presently being implemented through the
PIDER investment plan. It calls for the investment of US $6.1 million over the
period 1973-1977. Of this total:
a.  55% will be invested in directly productive infrastructure whereby
irrigation and livestock-related projects comprise 85% of this
amount. The remainder of this category will be allocated for soil
and water conservation works and rural industries.
b. 22% will be allocated for productive support items, primarily for
agricultural extension and demonstration.
c.  31% of the investment allotment is earmarked for social infrastructure,
distributed evenly among electrification, drinking water, and
materials for self-help projects.
d. Program coordination will require 2% of the total.
Overall, this investment program is consistent with its stated strategy
and has a favorable spatial distribution within El Llano and a logical phasing
of infrastructural works.  The key component missing during the June 1974
appraisal was a strong input from DAAC, which is concerned with promoting farmer
management and decision-making.



AMNEX 1e
Page 4
The ejido model developed to be representative of El Llano has as
the basis for projecting the projectts micro-regional benefits and costs.
Under varying cost assumDtions, the financial rate of return ranges from
24.4% to 21.2%, while the economic rate of return spans from 24.0% to 20%.

Furthermore, this investment program will directly benefit 10,700

grapes and corn.
Organization and Management
Program coordination is contracted with CONAIA. The positions of
State Committee Executive Secretary and PIDER Program Coordinator are
combined in one person, an agronomist by formal training. His staff consists
of two assistants, one of whom is an economist and the other a former rural
school teacher.
A State Committee meeting on June 15, to which the mission was
invited, demonstrated the statets interest in the PIDER program. However,
the mission assessed two shortcomings of the meeting: on the one hand,
the membership of all involved agencies is too large for meaningful discus-
sions of issues and, on the other hand, many of the operational matters raised
by agency representatives were of little general interest and should have
resolved ad hoc directly with the coordinator.
Regarding local participation, the June 1974 mission did not
observe evidence that either the municipalities or ejidos were involved to
any significant extent in the planning and implementation of the investment
program.



1ANFNEX le
Page 5
As presently structured and managed, the mission feels that the
long-term impact of PIDER as such in the state will in fact be measured
principally in terms of its financial reinforcement of regular agency budgets.
However, a new governor is to be elected in December 1974 and the official
candidate, a former Oficial Mayor of DAAC, is expected to make some funda-
mental changes. If these changes include appointment of a more capable
coordinator, creation of a small policy and strategy committee, assigning
the proper role to DAAC and obtaining more dynamic leadership from - or
eliminating - CONAZA, the situation for realizing PIDER objectives could be
improved tangibly in El Llano.
