MEXICO
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
NCRTH-EAST GUANAJUATO MICRO-REGION SUMMARY
Background
Guanajuato is an inland state in central Mexico, surrounded
(clockwise, from the north) by the states of Zacatecas, San Luis Potosi,
Queretaro, Michoacan and Jalisco. Its total area of 30,600 sq km comprises
about 2.3 million inhabitants, an average of 74 per sq km. Only 48% of
the state's population is still rural and 48% of the EAP depends on agri-
culture and forestry.
The northern part of the state contrasts sharply with the relative
prosperity and urbanization of its central and southern portions. Forty-three
percent of the state's area--comprising 15% of the population with an average
density of only 26 per sq km--is classified as "Arid Zone" by CONAZA.
Selection Criteria
The seven northeastern municipios comprise the 4,680 sq km
iTuanaiuato micro-region (see Map IBRDI lV& ).  It is essentially a very
dry mountain region where only half the municipal seats are connected by
paved roads. Most of the smaller localities lack access roads as well as
most basic social infrastructure.  It is one of the poorest and most abandoned
regions in this part of Mexico.
Since there are no major towns in the area, the population density
is only Po per sq km. Eighty-two percent of the population is rural and 69%
of the economically active population is engaged in agriculture and forestry.
Only 4% of the agricultural land is irrigated and nearly all of this is
located in the western edge of the region along the north-south Lerma valley.
Little more than one-third of the school age children are attending school,
as compared with nearly one-half for the state as a whole. Less than one-
third of the homes (including the towns) have water supply, against 56% for
the state, and only 13% are electrified, compared with 52% for the state.
Nearly three-fourths of the working population in 1970 declared incomes of
less than US$480 per year; this percentage is only 58 for the state as a whole.
Resources
From an agricultural point of view, this is probably one of the most
resource-poor micro-regions in the countrY in relation to the existing population.
With the exception of limited flat areas in the three western municipios, the
land is hilly to mountainous. Rainfall averagv,es 600-700 mm per year, concentrated



ANNEX 1(f)
Page 2
during the summer months and highly unreliable from year to year. The
area is subject to frequent drought conditions. As a result of population
pressure, both deforestation and over-grazing have been intense.
The eastern part of the region is said to be quite wealthy in
various minerals, including lead, mercury and silver. There exist many
small mines, some abandoned since World War II and others still operated
desultorily and primitively by the local population.  With the recent
increases in world metal prices, it may be possible to convert this potential
asset into a substantial source of income for this population, especially if
a more adequate road network is built.
Forest resources, actual or potential, may be significant in the
eastern part of the region but no surveys exist. FONAFE had accrued funds
from ejido forest exploitation fees in this area of more than M$60,000 in
1973, indicating a certain volume of activitv, but PIDER investigators found
that the ejidos did not even have receipts for these fees. The disorganization
in this sector is also reflected in DAAC's ejido census (see (c) below. In
other areas, there is evidence of serious forest over-exploitation by private
concessionaires. Added to the grave over-grazing, this is accelerating soil
erosion and the increasing desertization of the region.
Micro-Region Development Strategy
The micro-region plan stresses the "precariousness" of the region's
natural resources (rainfall and mountainous and very sloping land). It also
underlines the fact that the region had been virtually ignored by public
services and programs, and the interaction of the resource and infrastructure
underdevelopment. What the plan fails to emphasize, though there are occasional
reference in the text, is the almost complete lack of information regarding
some of the key resources of this area--underground water, forestry and mining--
and the lack of research-based data, both physical and economic, concerning
the potential for rainfed crop and livestock production. Hence, the strategy
proposed is heavily dependent on the key feasibility studies.
The lack of roads--both main and access--has impeded economic
development and social investments and services. Without roads there will
not even be a resource inventory nor economic research.
Similarly, the introduction of potable water supplies in the main
localities--especially the nearly 40 ejidos that are still devoid of such
service--are justifiable, not only for humane and health reasons but also
because well drillings for drinking -water (and for cattle) could be combined
with a systematic SRH underground water survey. Their cost could thus, in
social accounting terms, be partially written off against "production support"
research. In view of the fact that about one-half the school age children
still receive no education, substantial investment in school rooms is also
justified.



ANNEX 1(f)
Page 3
The overall objectives and timing of a development strategy
for this region provide: (a) a minimum package of basic investments in
economic and social infrastructure that would tend to make further invest-
ments viable to the extent that opportunities arise; kb) support key
activities such as underground water, minerals and forest surveys; and
(c) field-level research on pasture improvement, carrying capacity and
economically justified soil conservation, reforestation and yield increas-
ing rainfed agriculture technology. As was pointed out earlier, some of
this research-can be combined with productive investments on a pilot
basis. Meanwhile, DAAC should continue--and intensify--its efforts to
solve remaining tenure problems and promote organization and training in
the ejidos to enable them to be aware of their resources and capabilities
and participate in solving some of their problems.
Investment Program
Directly productive investments account for 61% of the cumulative
total all for agricultural activities. No investments are projected for
forestry or for mining, industry or handicrafts, This is believed to reflect
the scanty resource knowledge rather than the lack of any real potential out
of agriculture. On the other hand, more than one-half of this is for surface
water projects.
A soil conservation project that was visited in the field appeared
to be technically well executed on gently sloping land. However, the contour
ridges had been washed out in several places during the previous rainy season.
This indicates that; (a) greater protection may be necessary in the upper water-
shed; and (b) the community has not yet taken responsibility for maintaining
the work's.  Several of the "irrigation units" in the western, Lerma-valley part
are being planned,and some implemented, for grape production, as the area has
a well-developed technology for this crop.
Production support accounts for 22% of the cumulative investment
program. Well over one-half of this is earmarked for access roads, the major
bottleneck in the region. The remainder is assigned to agricultural to agri-
cultural extension and campesino organization and training. Agricultural
research facilities for the area are presently limited to the nearby livestock
experiment station. The recent equipping of a soils laboratory, with PIDER
funds, in the State Headquarters of SAG is a promising development; there had
been no such facility in the state. Some experiental parcels had been estab-
lished by extensionists in the micro-region, but no conclusive results were
as yet available. Meanwhile, 40h40-0 fertilizer applications for corn were
being recommended, though not based as yet on any locally derived field tests.
Plans are advanced for the construction of micro-region agricultural
research sub-center in San Luis de La Paz with federal funds. Because of its
location, and because it will be combined with an agricultural secondary school,
such an institution may have a major impact on farm technology in the area,
over the longer term.



ANNEX 1(f)
Page 4
Program Management
The program in the state of Guanajuato is contracted by the
Presidency with the agency CONAZA. There is one state level coordinator
(who is also executive secretary of the State Coordinating Committee) for
both micro-regions. He is a vigorous and at the same time modest and
patient lawyer who has close ties to, and the ccnfidence of, the Governor.
A native of the state, he was, for many years before returning to Guanajuato,
a labor union attorney in Mexico City. The state coordinator is responsible
simultaneously to CONAZA, the Governor and the Presidency. He appears to
know the state and the micro-region extremely well and has a grasp both of
the overall objectives and problems and of the operational and technical
details. He has a small office at some distance from the state government
building. He is assisted by a staff of four or five relatively junior
field persons of varying backgrounds, whom he uses not as subject matter
specialists but as general field evaluators and trouble shooters.

MEXICO
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
MICRQ-REGION SELECTION
1.        Micro-regions are composed of two or more neighboring municipa-
lities within one state. The basic requirements for a micro-region to be
selected for development under the PIDER program are that it be poor, have
productive potential, and lack basic infrastructure to exploit this potential.
2.        Initially, PIDER staff applied the following criteria in selecting
and delineating micro-regions:
(a) total population of about 100,000;
(b) total area of about 7,000 sq. km.;
(c) a maximum of 75 localities between 300 and 3,000 persons;
(d) existence of one or more growth poles;
(e) a high proportion of the economically active population with
an income of less than US $1,000 p.a.;
(f) a high index of underemployment;
(g) adequate development potential; and
(h) inadequate social infrastructure.
3.        Micro-Region Selection Criteria.  During appraisal a more explicit
approach was developed to specifically apply priorities in the selection
of micro-regions. (Table 1) This approach is based on a number
of minimum target  thresholds which each micro-region has to meet as
part of the selection and approval process. These threshold criteria
involve population density, poverty, underemployment and productive potential.
The 15 micro-regions appraised thus far meet these criteria. The remaining
15 micro-regions would first be selected on the same criteria and subse-
quently be appraised individually.
4.        Appraisal Criteria.  The appraisal would consist of an evaluation
of the investment program balance, an assessment of the micro-region
org-anization and staffing and a threshold economic rate of return of a
minimum of 10%. While each of the 15 submitted micro-regions have more
than 75% of their overall investment, including credit, programmed for
productive investments, assurances would be sought during negotiations
that any micro-region to be submitted having less than 70% productive



AN NEX 2
-2-
investments including credit would not be eligible for support under the
Bank loan. In appraising the remaining 15 micro-regions, the Bank would
apply a three-tier economic rate of return assessment test. Micro-regions
showing a 15% or higher rate of return on productive investments would
initially be approved for reimbursement, with selected field checks
carried out where necessary. Those micro-regions submitted carrying rates
of return between 10% and 15% would be subject to a detailed field appraisal
by IBRD staff. Those falling below 10% would not be accepted for IBRD support
under the Project. Assurances on this minimum rate of return test (10%) were
obtained during negotiations. This appraisal would be made by IBRD staff
prior to recommending acceptance of the remaining micro-region investment
plans for disbursement purposes, and assurances to this effect were
obtained at negotiations.
5.        Village/Ejido Investment Programming.  During the initial village
level investment programming, a joint 10 to 15 person state and federal
levelteam visits each village or ejido in a selected micro-region. Prior
to their visit, a detailed baseline diagnosis is completed at the state
level from various censuses and agency files (DAAC, SRH, etc.).  This
diagnosis includes basic data on the village or ejido. Included in this
basic diagnosis are population ana income data, cropping patterns,
availability of existing resources (groundwater, stream flows, soil and
rainfall patterns), and basic infrastructure and services (schools, roads,
etc.) and a summary of all requests for improvements made by the village
to state and federal level authorities. During their visit, the programming
team checks and revises these data with the villagers, explains the PIDER
objectives and strategies as they might apply to their village, and obtains
from villagers their development priorities. The mix of the villager-
desired investment program is then assessed (on the basis of priority needs
for income and employment growth and improved provision of an essential
level of basic services ) against available technical information (existence
of groundwater, soils, etc.) and finally developed into a multi-sectoral
village level investment program. (Annex 8, Table 1 contains a typical
village level investment program. These village or ejido investment
programs are then consolidated into-micro-region development programs.
6.        During appraisal, a more systematic village/ejido level
investment programming methodology (described below) was devised.
PIDER then agreed to reprogram its existing micro-regions using this
improved methodology. This appraisal report is based on 15 of these
reprogrammed micro-regions, containing this improved methodology.
7.        Essentially, this new methodology makes explicit the incremental
income and employment generating aspects of the ejido development program
It also enables PIDER programming staff to test the internal consistency
of the ejido investment package. The investments programmed in the repre-
sentative ejido and village plans were systematically evaluated using a
economic rate of return test (above 10%) and estimates that temporary
labor required for development works related to the development of that
ejido could be met from the off-season under-employed labor from that ejido.



ANNEX2
This analysis of a proposed investment package for a "representative" ejido
thus provides an analytical measure that the investment program as a whole
for the micro-region, consolidated from village and ejido based programs,
meets adequate financial and labor availability tests.
8.        This methodology (see Annex 8) can then be used to assess:  (i)
the projected yields which underlie the technical feasibiiity of obtaining
increased incomes; (ii) the annual temporary and permanent labor requirements
of the proposed investment package measured against seasonally unemployed
ejido labor; and, (iii) the availability of funds from within the ejido to
meet fixed levels of cash contributions to social infrastructure such as
water and electricity and to service increased operating loan amortization
charges from incremental income flows. In addition, this ejido budgeting
methodology enables the various agencies interfacing with the e.ido members
to better time their investment, service and credit porivision activities.
Finally, the methodology requires the calculation of financial and economic
rates of return.
9.        As mentioned, PIDER prepares an investment program for each
village or ejido receiving assistance under a micro-region program. In
addition, PIDhR has now prepared financial detailed budgets in its repro-
grammed micro-region plans for two actual villages or ejidos in each
micro-region. The ejidos for which these detailed financial budgets were
prepared were selected by PIDER staff as being an indicative sample of
the likely cash flow and benefit streams to be obtained from the investment
packages proposed for the majority of ejidos to be developed in that
micro-region. To consolidate and expand this budgeting methodology,
assurances would be obtained during negotiations that ejido or village
budgets would be prepared for each village or ejido participating in
the project no later than one year following the signing of the loan.
Copies of these budgets would be available at ejido/village, state and
PIDER (federal).
10.       EJido Selection & Responsiveness Criteria.    In relation to these
criteria, it is important to know the relative readiness of ejidos or
communities for responding positively to PIDER action. An assessment of
potential responsiveness indicates the pace at which communities can be
brought into the program over time, leading to a more realistic set of
expectations for the micro-region program. Optimally, criteria should
be developed to rate communities and place them within five or six categories,
ranging from very responsive (ready-now) to totally uninterested (may not be
ready within even a long term, fifteen or twenty year plan). Such criteria
should ideally further define the objective related to attitudes, and the
scaling of communities would provide a framework for PIDER action. A better
definition of the problem would also help action agencies search for alterna-
tive strategies for motivating and working effectively with less interested
communities.



ANNEX 2
11.       While PIDER has not developed such a detailed typology for
ordering communities according to responsiveness, the selection of communi-
ties by PIDER staff for intensive action clearly responds primarily to
their intuitive sense of ccmmunity readiness. The current Presidencia
strategy is to work first with the most interested communities. This is the
only feasible alternative if the program is to produce positive results in
a fairly short time, demonstrating PIDER's effectiveness to both the political
leadership of Mexico and the neighboring communities.
12.       Dual Equity Considerations.  This strategy, however, also leads
to the concern for equity, since the most prepared communities tend to be
at the upper end of the development scale. An ex-ante analysis of the
place of these communities on a development scale and an assessment of the
relative responsiveness of all communities can indicate the equity costs
of the PIDER strategy and, hopefully, the minimizing of these costs. The
second level of concern for equity is within, rather than among, communities.
The detailed budget analysis within the ejido model assumes incremental net
income will be evenly distributed among ejidatarios.  Despite legal provi-
sions for equitable access to land within ejidos. there often exists a
very unequal distribution of control over land and other assets. The
inequitable distribution of control does not respond to economic efficiency
criteria, but to other factors associated with the prevailing social struc-
ture. Optimally, PIDER ejido appraisal criteria should include the identi-
fication of the factors responsible for the concentration of control over
assets in a few hands. It should also determine that (1) PIDER action will
not reinforce existing inequities, but rather that (2) PIDER action will
contribute to an improved distribution of decisionmaking and control over
assets. To an extent, such criteria are used when DAAC SOFE team members
are already well-established ant participate actively in the ejido planning
exercise.

IMEXISO0
RURAL DEVELOPIC-NT PROJECT
SMALL-SCALE IRRIGATION
I. Introduction
Small-scale irrigation is a major component of the project.  The
proposed project investment of US$35 million in irrigation represents 16%
of the total investment.  The executing federal agencies are the Secretaria
de Recursos Hidr'aulicos (SRH) and the Secretaria de Agricultura y Ganaderia
(SAG).
II. Background
On much of the land suitable for agricultural production, irriga-
tion is required to achieve sustained and significant improvement in crop
yields. Subsistence rainfed agriculture in regions of inadequate precipita-
tion (large parts of the central and northern parts of the country with less
than 700 mm per year) can be improved through the use of adapted seed varieties,
soil and water conservation and better farming methods, but only to a limited
extent.  This is largely due to the high risks entailed when investing in
inputs (seed, fertilizer, etc.) in zones of insufficient rainfall. Tradi-
tionally, the MIexican farmers have tried to minimize the risk of crop losses
by cultivating maize and more recently sorghum crops which offers at
least some return even under relatively dry weather conditions. Shifts to
higher-value crops require some supplementary irrigation in areas of less
than 700 mm rainfall.  Consequently,the Government has allocated some 60%
of total pubiic investment for agriculture for irrigation schemes.
III. SRE
SRH is empowered by the Federal vlater Law of 1972 to construct,
administer and operate irrigation, drainage and flood protection works.
While SRH previously concentrated its efforts to build large-scale irriga-
tion districts, small-scale projects gained in importance during the past
seven years. In 1967, SRH was charged with preparing a plan for bringing



A-.\  X 3a
Page 2
irrigation to the numerous sm1ll villaoes Tw-,hich had been bypassed by previous
development projects.  A "Plan Nacional de PequeHa Irrigacion" (National Plan
for Small Scale Irrigation) was designed which provided, during the first
phase (1967-1970), irrigation to 120,000 ha benefiting 40,000 families.
During the second phase (1971-1976), now called "Plan Nacional de Obras de
Riego para el Desarrollo Rural" (National Irrigation Plan for Rural Develop-
ment) 230,000 ha are planned to be irrigated benefiting some 130,000 families.

The Plan is carried out by the SRH Department for Small-Scale
Irrigation for Rural Development (ORDR)o Irrigation facilities provided
under this Plan range from small wells powered by windmills (for domestic
and livestock water supplies) to small dams, pumping plants and deep wells.
These facilities are in schemes not exceeding 2,500 ha. In implementing
the Plan, SRi has become involved in integrated rural development as well
as irrigation ranging from investigating and promoting to constructing and
operating water development projects.
The SRH Plan for small-scale irrigation (Plan Nacional de Obras
de Riego para el Desarrollo Rural), calls for a total irrigated area of
280,000 ha with an average land holding of 2.15 ha per family. SRH small-
scale irrigation projects supported by IDB in 1968-1972 provided an average
of 3.07 ha of irrigated land per family in a total area of 87,500 ha.
The size of these holdings may appear to be relatively small and
to contribute to the fragmentation of the irrigated area in 1Iexico.  hoowever,
practically all farmers o'm also rainfed crop land and, most probably, could
not cultivate a much larger irrigated area without the help of hired labor.
The present policy of providing about two to four hectares land in small-
scale irrigation districts, therefore, appears to be justified.



A'NNDEX 3a
Page 3
Although the water shortage in many regions of Mexico appears to
limit seriously further irrigation development, small-scale irrigation
projects can still be built in most regions.  ORDR has carried out numerous
feasibility studies which show the potential still to be developed.  These
studies reportedly cover an area of about 800,000 ha.
A. Criteria for SRH Supported Small-Scale Irrigation Projects Under
PIDER
Ln vie;T of the large number of applications for developing small-
scale irrigation projects received by SRH, ORDP. has developed a procedure
(which applies both generally and to PIDER) to screen and rank them according
to economic and social priorities. Requests are preliminarily screened
according to such factors as available infrastructure (roads and electricity
lines), markets, levels of education and farming capability of the farmers
within each community. In order to establish priorities for irrigation
investments, a socio-economic index was defined based on the 1970 I\National
Census.  The following data were considered:
- the percentage of the rural population, relative to
the total population;
- the percentage of the economically active population
in the agricultural sector, relative to the total of
the economically active population;
- the percentage of the economically active population
with incomes below the official minimira salary,
relative to the economically active population in the
agricultural sector;
- the reciprocal value of the average monthly income
from the agricultural sector;
- the percentage of the population which had not eaten
meat, eggs and milk during the week before the Census,
relative to the total population.
A second index was elaborated to consider the availability of
water and agricultural land. However, considering the uncertainty that water
and agricultural land would be available in one and the same project area,
this index was substituted by an index based on the number of hectares
identified as possible projects (in total 683,220 ha) in a nationwide
inventory of irrigation projects.
Finally, a combined index was calculated by simply adding the socio-
economic situation and the projects identified. This information enables
the planners to site irrigation projects in the light of poverty and produc-
tion potential.

Page 14
Applications are then classified into one of eight models ranging
from simple installations serving small areas (about 25 ha) to larger and
more elaborate schemes.
Model 1.  A simple windmill delivering about 16 m3 a
day for watering livestock and utilizing a well 25 to
100 m deep.
I4odel 2. A small dam.with maximum height of 17 m and
impounding up to 200,000 m3.  Design is according to a
standard handbook.
ibodel 3. A pump scheme larger than Model 1 (driven by
windmill or diesel motor) and provided with a small
storage tank; this scheme is suitable for irrigating a
small orchard and/or providing water for domestic and
stock use.
XModel 4. .. simple small diversion stru.cture from
streams with perennial flows.

lIodel 6. A dam with a height exceeding 17 m, capable
of storing more than 3 million m3 and of irrigating up
to a maximum of 5,000 ha. (Such a dam must be designed
individually rather than from a handbook.)
hodel 7.  Pumping station for extracting water from a

to 4,000 ha.
Model 8. A deep well on group of deep wells yielding
a minimum flowy of 20 lit/sec ivith each well capable
of serving 30 to 120 ha.
After screening the applications and selecting priority areas,
ORDR enters agreements with a local users' association which specifies wdhat
works and services ORDIR will provide and it binds the users, association to
accept conditions concerning operation and maintenance 6f the work, water
use and water charges. The agreement requires signature of the userst
association, SRH and DAAC.  The latter is required because in many cases
changes in titles for landholdings may have to be agreed-to by the users
(ejidatarios or small landow-ners).
When an agreement with a users' association is reached, SRH
proceeds with a feasibility study which include hydrology, soil surveys, an
appraisal of existing crops and cropping practices, a survey of present land
distribution between ejidos, small proprietors and other types of tenure,



ANNEX 3a
Page 5
preliminary designs and cost estimates, social and economic factors. Con-
struction of Drojects finally approved by SR} is carried out by contractors
following the usual tendering procedures of SRH with supervision of the
contractor by field staff.
After construction, project operation follows regulations established
by SREI regarding types of crops grown, the quantity of water used, the manner
in which water is shared and the maximum area of land which any one owner may
irrigate. The SRH Direcci6n General de Unidades de Riego para el Desarrollo
Rural is charged with these functions including the assignment of extension
workers to new irrigation districts to assist the users' associations in
managing their fields in the most adequate form. Each association elects its
owm chairman or president, a secretary, a treasurer and such other members
of the committee as considered necessary.  Two or more associations may be
combined to form an "irrigation district." These procedures are soecified
in the relevant articles of the Federal vlater Law (articles 73 to 83) dealing
with Unidades de Riego para el Desarrollo Rural.
B. Financial Criteria
Public investment in irrigation increased from US$62 million
during the years 1926-1947 to US$788 million in 1965-1970 (see Table 1).
During this period, the investment per  hectare  increased
from US$75 to US$2,004. The tentative SRH budget for the present 1971-1976
sexenio called for an investment of US$821 million in new irrigation projects
of which US$240 million were allocated to small-scale projects. In addition
to these investments US$330 rillion was assigned for completion of irrigation
works under construction and US$147 million for rehabilitation of irrigation
districts.  The goal for SRI small-scale irrigation investments was revised
in 1973 and set at US$340 million for 280,000 ha irrigated land (see Table 2);
US$144 million were committed by the Inter-American Development Bank (IDB)
to assist this program.
The average investment costs per hectare of small-scale irrigation
projects during the years 1968-1972 were reported by IDB as US$960 per ha
and US$2,880 per family. To eliminate expensive sub-projects and to assure
a more equitable distribution of the investment benefits, the following
limits to construction costs are to be applied under the Project.

C.  Operation and Maintenance Costs
Operation and maintenance costs always have to be paid fully by
the users of the irrigation districts as spelled out in previous loan
agreements between IDB and SRH. This condition is recommended to be
applied in all PIDER irrigation projects.
D. Investment Recovery
The 1972 Mexican Federal Water Law requires beneficiaries of
irrigation to repay capital investment from water charges at rates determined
by SRH after SRH has carried out detailed studies on the beneficiaries repay-
ment capacity.  Current practice by SPH is not to recover capital costs from
the very small "obras minimas," schemes up to 25 ha benefiting from 10 to
25 families. However, for larger schemes, 26 to 2,500 ha, SRH after various
economic and social studies. Under the Project, assurances would be obtained
that all irrigation investment would be subject to investment recovery at
rates to be determined by SRH after determination through socio-economic
studies of repayment capacity of beneficiaries. "Obras minimas" schemes
emphazising irrigation would not be excluded from being subject to capital
recovery, as a number may be over 20 ha, benefit only a few families which
would enable them to repay a substantial portion of the initial investment.
E. Organization and Management
ORDR carries out the overall planning, prepares projections at
the national level and annual programs, and ensures an integrated appoach
nationwide. It is responsible for projects between 500 and 2,500 ha_/ and
has to review and approve the smaller projects prepared at the regional
level (25 to 500 ha) and at the state level (up to 25 ha). To ensure an
integrated approach to all projects, two working committees have been
established within ORDR: (a) Comite de Esth.dios y Proyectos and (b) Comit6
de Construccion.

MEXICO
INTEGRATED RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
SOIL AND WATER CONSERVATICN
Introduction
A 1974 SAG study estimates that 47% of Mexico's land has been
adversely affected by soil erosion and that 14 million ha have been permanently
lost to agriculture. An IDB soil conservation consultant estimated that 66%
of the lands in five micro-regions be appraised were eroded, a percentage well
abow the national average of 47%.
While the Government has increased agency (SAG + SRH) budgets for
improved conservation practices, the f4nds programmed for the 1973-1976 period
(US$ 101 million) would only be sufficient to initiate a national program.
At an average cost of US$104/ha, only about 960,000 ha would be benefitiaL_
Over the longer term, a very much larger, coordinated resource management and
conservation program would be required to mitigate the damaging effects of
soil erosion on the productive capability of the land. By integrating these
soil and water practices in the coordinated PIDER micro-region effort, an
excellent opportunity exists to maximize the benefits of these works with
related efforts (extension etc.) designed to increase small farm productivity
PIDER and Soil Conservation
The PIDER program includes 5% of its total investment for improvements
in soil and water conservation in the Project micro-regions. Under PIDER, the
majority of these works are to be executed by SAG. The primary objectives
of the program are (1) to assist farmers to make, in orderly progression over
a period of years, changes in their cropping system or land use needed to
conserve, develop, protect, and utilize the soil and water resources of their
lands, and (2) to install needed conservation measures and carry out the
practices needed under such changed systems and uses. The average cost per ha
using labor intensive techniques averages US$104 per ha. These costs range
from a low of US$45 on 5% slopes in Sur de Nuevo Leon to US$240/ha on 20%
slopes under higher rainfall in the Zacapoetla micro-region. The PIDER/SAG
strategy to carry out these objectives is twofold, to use labor intensive
construction methods to provide temporary, off-season employment in the micro-
regions(85% of construction is labor) and to design the works so as to deal
with current soil erosion and to improve water retention for rainfed maize
and bean cultivation.
The Project
The Project would support the construction of soil and water conser-
vation works by labor-intensive methods. Some 91,000 ha would be protected
at an average cost of US$104 per ha. The works involve contour ridging and



ANNEX 3b
Page   2
terracing and checkdams for gully protection, to avoid water erosion and
at the same time conserve soil moisture. Programs are implemented on both
grazing and rainfed crop land but technical criteria for each differ. Hand
labor and draft animals, as well as machinery, are used, but the former is
preferred on slopes of 10% or more.
The program is the responsibility of the Soil and Water Conservation
Directorate of SAG.  Its budget has increased substantially from 1968 to 1974),
including both regular program and PIDEER. Field staff generally consists
of one "delegate" with one to three assistant engineers per state. There are
23 state "delegations" in the respective state offices of SAG.
Field visits indicate that the specific works per se are generally
well designed from a narrow technical point of view. However, projects are
generally not conceived as forming part of a general watershed management and
conservation plan. Hence, erosion in, and runoff from the upper watersheds
often defeat the conservation works in the lower areas where the crops and
livestock are produced. 1/   Since most projects are udertaken on ejido land,
which often commands entire small watersheds, a opportunity is perhaps being
missed. This may well reflect lack of training and experience among the
responsible officials in this type of approach. It may also illustrate the
lack of coordination at the state and micro-region level of the activities
of the various branches within the Ministry of Agriculture.  Close collaboration
in such projects between the Soil and Water Conservation   Directorate and the
Forestry Department is a prerequisite.
To widen the import of these works, the Project would require that
prior to construction, detailed conservation plans on a small watershed basis
would be required on an ejido or village basis.
For very low rainfall areas (250-500 mm annually, the arid lands
institute (CONAZA) is working in the micro-regions developing suitable water
retention techniques. The technical research arm of CONAZA has an experimental
farm near Matehuala, strategically located (on the border between S. LuTis
Potosi and Nuevo Leon) for a number of rmicro-regions. A number of water conser-
vation practices are being tried there, with preliminary results already
available. For example, closely spaced contour ridges on gently sloping land
have produced significant gains in pasture grass availabilities.  Also, selected
maguey cactus planted on contour ridges have been shown to be capable of providing
additional income at a low marginal cost. Some of these experiences have been
informally adopted by conservation -and extuension personnel in some areas,
especially of cactus planting and contour ridging.
1/     It is also futile to expect communities to maintain these conservation
works as long as heavy runoff from the upper watershed is not controlled.



ANNEX Lb
Page   3
Maintenance
Maintenance of SAG constructed conservation works would be
strengthened under the Project. The contract with the village and ejido
conservation association would specify that maintenance of constructed
works benefiting that ejido or village would be at the expense of the
beneficiaries under SAG supervision.
Criteria
To qualify for reimbursement under the Project, the following
criteria for soil and water conservation works would have to be met:
(1) A Soil and Water Conservation Watershed program would
be designed for the works to be undertaken on a village
or ejido level. Since most ejidos and villages cover
substantial areas of land, the development and executiorn
of such a plan should not create major difficulties as
compared to trying to develop such plans required the
coordinaticn of hundreds of private farmers in a watershed.
(2)  Works constructed under this lrogram would only be under-
taken on those ejidos or villages where the farmers have
indicated a strong willingness to work with extension teams
from SAG to take advantage of a package of productive
investments including soil conservation. Soil and water
conservation works without complimentary agricultural
improvements such as improved seeds, better spacing, use
of fertilizer etc. does not maximize the full potential of
constructed works.
Benefits
The benefits of integrating soil and water conservation works into
the coordinated program of PIDER micro-region development are threefold:
(1) economic;
(2) technical; and
(3) employment.
Economic
Although the economic benefits derived from soil and water conservation
programs in the micro-regions have not been quantified, the national problem



AITNEX 3b
Page 4
or eroision and sedimentation is critical in Mexico. The situation is
getting progressively worse and is beginning to seriously undermine the
country's agricultural productivity base. High degrees of erosion result
in lower yields, and soil erosion is negatively related to the yields of the
most important crops in any micro-region. The most evident economic effect
of erosion is that it affects gross income through declining yields. Once
the first one-third of the surface horizon is lost, yields become more
sensitive to further topsoil losses and income is affected accordingly.
SAG does not have a detailed analysis available of the economic
returns from the various types of works constructed under its direction.
For contour ridging in 600-850 mm rainfall. SAG stated that early evidence
showed a 20-30% increase in corn yields due to improved water retention,
yield increase *as achieved without fertilizer.
A more detailed measure of the benefits that may be expected from
such works is provided in a recent (May 1974) economic analysis of the
on-going Great Plains (U.S.) Conservation Program. Using present combinations
of conservation practices, which like Mexico, emphasize water retention
through contouring the study concluded that one dollar of investment generated
US$3.80 in additional agriculture.
In the Project, the costs of soil and water conservation are
included in village and ejido development programs for purposes of calculating
the economic rate of return to the productive investments in that ejido.
A (minimum rate of return of 8% for the productive component is established
(see Annex 8).
Technical
The same U.S.D.A. national study demonstrates conservation practices
initiated on 77 million acres in the Great Plains Region has resulted ir.
reducing soil losses caused by wind and water erosion by approximately 221
million tons per year. Since there are approximately 150 tons of topsoil
per acre inch, the 221 million tons per acre saved represents an equivalent
of six inches of soil from 2455,500 acres annually. The conservation methods
applied on the 77 million acres are reducing the sediment loads of area
streams and reservoirs by 10.5 million tons or 6,887 acre.feet df sediment
annually.
In Mexico, the types of erosion creating land damage are the
following:
Sheet and rill erosion: reduction in soil productivity;
Gully and streambank erosion: land loss, land depreciation and
sediment production;



ANNEX 3b
Page 5
Streambank and streambed erosion, landslides and landslips;
Streambed erosion and floodplain scour: lowering of water
tables and sediment production.
Enployment
At the same time, the provision of teimporary, off-season employment
in the micro-regions (85% of construction is labor), and the ensuing economic
benefits to the population of the areas, cannot be overemphasized. The
program will contribute to a more equitable income distribution, and provide
to a large number of farmers opportunities to contribute to productive
investments on their farms. The Project would provide about 9,500 man years
of labor in total. Given the seasonability, about 19,000 persons would receive
employment for ! year during the non-cropping (November - May) season in rain-
fed areas, some 600 people per micro-region.

RPIL DEVBL0PIST' PROJECT
FEEDER ROADS
Introduction
For the past 45 years, limited attention was given to the tertiary
road network in Mexico, the bulk of resources beinr' used for construction
and maintenance of primary and secondary roads of excellent qualitv and
standards. At the time the present federal administration took office
(1970), some 15,000 out of 20,000 localities with a population between 300
and 2,500 were not connected to the primary/secondary road network except
by low standard tracks, many of which were passable only during part of the
year or on animal back.  In the six states where ricro-regions were visited
by the appraisal mission, only 23% (882 out of 3,833) of such localities'
were accessible by regular motor vehicles on all weather roads.
The present federal administration has emphasized the importance
of feeder roads as an expression of its concern for the rural poor. Up to
300,000 km of such roads (class D and E) are to be built or improved over
the period 1970-1976. These public works will be labor intensive, providing
about 600 million man-days at minimum wages for local labor.  The Secretaria
de Obras Publicas (SOP) is currently building more than 25,000 km of such
feeder roads per year and plans to connect before 1980 most villages and
towns with a population above 250 inhabitants. In this report, only the
roads integrated in the PIDER project will be examined. These constitute
15% of the works mentioned above while the other 85K? will be planned outside
the PIDER program and budgeted directly from Hacienda to SOP (this last
category is referred to as the "normal program")O
Feeder road construct-on represents a significant change in SOP's
policy the general objective of which, up to 1968, was to provide and main-
tain road connections between the main towns (especially the state capitals),
sea ports, border crossings and lIezico City.  A main SOP objective is the
construction of a network of feeder roads to connect isolated communities to
the present highway network.  Out of its US$2.5 billion total investment plan
for the period 1973-1976, SOP has earmarked US$D1.12 billion (45%) for labor
intensive feeder roads.
SOP is a dynamic agency and has taken an early lead in the PIDEP
integrated rural development project.  The PIDER road investments will
amount to US$100 million in the 1974-1976 period (about 100 of the 3rojected
PIDER expenditures), corresponding to approximately 13,000 km of E roads.
These works will make a substantial contribution to the pro4.ect by helpDing
to open up backward rural areas, facilitating construction and maintenance
of directly productive and social investments and Droviding adeauate
accessibility for key inputs such as extension an-d  reting services.
In spite of recent improvements, the highway density in Mexico
is still only 0.038 km per km  (about 1.6 km of road per thousand
inhabitants). This lack of accessibility has adverse effects on rural develop-
ment. Infrastructure costs can reach prohibitive levels and maintenance of
directly productive and social investments is difficult when no vehicle



ANNEX 3c
Page 2
access is possible. Because of such factors, Government agencies (e.g., SRH)
tend to focus on already accessible areas, thereby accentuating local
disparities. Finally, a good feeder road support infrastructure is an
absolute prerequisite for effectiveness in extension service delivery, itself
a preliminary condition for rural development in backward areas in M4exico.
The ProJect
Some 1,900 km of Class E labor intensive feeder roads would
be constructed in the 30 micro-regions. The feeder road component would
cost about US$12.7 million, some 6% of total project costs. While
average km costs would be roughly US$6.800, there is considerable cost
variation due to wide terrain and rainfall conditions in the micro-regions.
In the drier parts of flat Mexico such as Sur de Nuevo Leon, construction
costs per km average US$6,000 compared to us*$14,000 in mountainous wet regions
of Puebla state.  An important aspect of this component is the integration
of feeder road construction into the broader productive rural development
strategy of the micro-region. weeder roads would not be constructed unless
the economic rate of return for the productive and productive support invest-
ments, including feeder roads exceeded 10%.
Design Standards
Feeder road design standards may vary somewhat from one state to
another mostly because the resident engineers and SOP centers have managed
to adapt effectively general design guidelines to local conditions. For
examDle, when drainage is a minor problem (e.g., in Southern NTuevo Leon),
birdbaths are mostly used; in other micro-regions where rainfall is
relatively important (e.g., in Zacapoaxtla) culverts are a more usual
feature.  Resident engineers use local materials as much as possible to cut
costs and this may affect construction standards: e.g., in Zacapoaxtla,
the resident engineer uses large stones instead of the usual 15 cm gravel
surface on some roads and local pine tree trunks to reinforce small crossings;
otherwise, gravel is obtained on the site or by hand crushing stones in the
area of road works.
IIost feeder roads integrated in the PIDZR. proje6t are class E.
The Nuevo Leon SOP center has built some roads that will easily be upgraded
to D status in the future; in particular, bridges are about 5 m wide (vs. 4
m in general), tlfo 1 m shoulders have been provided (other-roads in the
PIDE program do not provide any) and turnouts (about every 500 m) have been
constructed (no turnouts are generally provided on other roads)0 In general,
other construction standards are as follows:

Construction
Road works comprise clearing of the alignment, leveling, patching
with smaller aggregates of 3-5 cm, spreading of gravel, and culvert building.
Trhe roads are not surfaced or compacted.  Generally, every task is performed
by using exclusively local hand labor (SOP nrovides the workers with wheel-
barrow s, Pickaxes, shovels and other necessary implements).  Local sup lies
are made use of as much as technically feasible:  stones and gravel are
moved by workers if available close to the construction site; if necessary,
stone crushing is done by hand.  Local contractors are occasionall- hired
for hauling of gravel and for earthworks (in the cases w7here the  volumes to
be moved are very important).  SOP does not usually use its omn equipment.

1Iaintenance
The feeder roads are to be maintained by the population
in the newly connected villages under control of the village road committees.
SOP would provide a minimum of supervision and pay for the materials and
oart of the labor that will be required; M$3,000 (US$240) per kilometer has
been budgeted-for this purpose in 1975. However, these arrangements have
yet to be tested as very little feeder road maintenance works have yet been
carried out.
SOP Organization
Labor intensive feeder roads are under the responsibility of
directorate supervised by an undersecretary. The Directorate plans and
selects future investments, coordinates SOP feeder road programs with
state highway departments supervises procurement and specifies design
standards.
SOP State level offices propose feeder road programs and organize
procurement. The execution is carried out by resident engineers at the micro-
region level. They are solely responsible for supervision of feeder roads
construction in their area. They deal directly with the population that is
going to contribute to this public works program (in the form of labor, paid
at minimum wages); their task is to organize and motivate the population so
that ad hoc committees can be formed to act as contractors for village labor
procurement in feeder roads construction and maintenance works.

MEXICO
RURAL DEVELOPMET PROJECT
M4ARKETING AND THE ROLE OF CONASUPO
I. Introduction
CONASUPO has the basic jurisdiction for planning, execution and manage-
ment of public sector marketing facilities, within the PIDER program. The
contemplated CONASUPO investment in PIDER would primarily focus on construc-
tion of rural warehouses (bodegas rurales), and rural retail stores (tiendas
rurales). Of the total PIDER investment for 1973-78, the investment in
warehouses and stores in the 100 PIDER micro-regions was estimated at roughly
US$24 million, or about 2p of the total.
Units                      Investment (in US$1 ,O0)
Years   WarehousesLa    Stores/    Total        Warehouses/c   Stores/d Total

The investment requirements for marketing facilities are relatively small.
Yet, a satisfactory performance of marketing functions is a crucial
determinant of the overall success of the rural development program. This
is because size of the net disposable income of individual, PIDER beneficiaries
will, to large extent, depend on the prices they will be paying for production
inputs and consumer staples, and on prices they will be receiving for sale
of their marketable labor, crops and livestock.
II. CONASUPO
A. Objectives and Strategy
CONASUPO is the latest in a series of government regulatory and
marketing organizations beginning with the Wheat Regulatory Board in 1937.
A 1965 decree formed CONASUPO as an expanded version of the Campafnia



ANNEX 3d
Page 2
Nacional de Subsistencias Populares, S.A. which had been formed in 1961. In
1971 a significant strategy shift occurred; while CONASUPO's programs had
succeeded in increasing production and improving marketing channels, the
distribution of the benefits had been unequal. Consequently, CONASUPO
reformulated its objectives to focus more sharply on the lower income segments
of the society, especially in the rural sector. To achieve this revised
strategy, CONASUPO set forth three basic objectives:
a. regulate the basic goods market;
b. increase the income of poor farmers;
c. increase the ability of low income consumers
to acquire basic goods.
The prime target groups are producers with annual incomes below
M$12,000 (US$960).
CONASUPO has set forth the following general strategy for its rural
development efforts; enable the campesino to increase his income, retain it,
and invest it in productive means. The major components of the CONASUPO rural
development strategy are the following:
a. Sell basic consumer goods (primarily foodstuffs)
in rural areas at prices lower than those currently
prevailing.
b. Provide consumer credit to rural consumers on a
carefully controlled basis.
c.  Buy farmers' produce at guaranteed prices.
d. Provide auxiliary services needed to alter the
unfavorable structural economic relationships.
e. Help market campesinos' non-agricultural products,
e.g., handicrafts as well as commercial products
such as construction materials.
f. Involve the campesinos in decision-making and
operational aspects of the programs.
B. The Project
Under the Project some 60 small stores ("tiendas rurales") and 5
warehouses ("bodegas") for the purchase and storage of farm produce would
be constructed. This represents about 26.5% and 21.0% respectively of the
total units planned for the 100 PIDER micro-regions during the 1975-78
period.

1. Rural Stores
Most of the 60 retail stores visualized for establishment within
Project micro-regions would be in existing buildings. Adaptation of the
structures would be done at an average cost of about US$2,500 (see Table 1).
Initial stock for such stores requires an additional investment of US$2,400
per store. It would include simple productive inputs such as garden seeds,
hoes, pesticide and fertilizer as well as supplies of basic consumer require-
ments.
The PIDER stores would be incorporated into an extensive network
of about 2,700 urban and rural stores selling around M$3.7 billion (US$300
million) worth of goods annually. The basic rationale for having integrated
vertically forward into the retailing segment of the food system was to lower
prices of basic goods (and ensure supply availability) to poor income groups
via a more efficient and less exploitative distribution system. The lower
prices come directly via the merchandise offered in the stores and
indirectly via a competitive regulatory effect which would tend to keep
private sector store prices closer to CONASUPO's.
The prices in CONASUPO's urban stores are about 10% less than the
competition's. The price differentials in the rural sector are even greater.
This is not surprising. Perhaps a universal economic dictum is that "the
poor pay more." Generally, the distribution systems supplying rural areas
are fragmented, poorly organized, and inefficient. Outlets are small,
inventory management rudimentary at best, transportation costs excessive,
and quantity buying almost nonexistent. In addition to this high per unit
costs, rural stores often hold a semi-monopolistic position because of their
geographical remoteness and the generally infrequent and costly transporta-
tion services to urban centers. CONASUPO states that its surveys reveal



ANTThX 3d
Page 4
that its prices are 20-23% less than those prevailing in ouher rural stores.
In fact, in some areas campesinos reportedly refer to these lower prices as
the "normal difference".  They expect COTNASUPO's prices to be lower.  Price
verification during field visits by Bank consultants corroborated these
differences.
Because the rural sector is generally very poor, with limited
buying power, it is often assumed away as part of the countryts comercial
market. As a consequence, marketing of consumer goods to the rural sector
has generally been a forgotten dimension of rural development. Such over-
sight ignores reality. Most campesinos do enter into the goods economy to
some extent as indicated in the introductory statistics on subsistence
farmers.  Because of this, and because food expenditures probably absorb
30% of their family budget, food prices have a dramatic imnact on the
economic situation of the rural family.
As pointed out above, the fixed investment in a rural CONASUPO
store amounts to about US$2,500 and another US$2,L4OO is needed for working
capita'l. Assuming that a rural CONASUPO store has annual sales of US$5,400
and that campesino purchasing power would increase US$1,090, and the store
manager would receive commissions amo-mting to about 4'% of sales, or US$220.
If one takes off depreciation at 15 years and interest on the working capital
at 10%, the return on the US$,900 fixed investment is about 20%. This
represents a significant direct economic benefit to the campesinos. In
addition, the store may help untie the campesino from having to sell his
crop at lower prices to store owners in exchange for merchandise; he might
thereby be able to sell to CONASUPO buying centers at higher prices, thus
increasing further his income.
2. Rural Warehouses
The rural warehouse project would focus on construction  5 new
storage facilities of 500 and 1,000 tons capacity. The average cost would
be about US$184,000, including start-up working capital.
The total cost for this component is US$1.5 million, 1l  of the
total Project cost and 6% of OONASUPO's expenditure (US$24 million) under
the overall PIDER program.
The PIDER warehouses would be incorporated into CONASUPOts network
of 1,241 rural warehouse centers with a storage capacity of 1.4 million
metric tons. A 20% expansion in centers and a 575Z increase in capacity is
projected through 1976. Ancillary transport and shelling services will also
be expanded.
The rural warehouses were built as part of CONDASUPO's price support
program for basic crops, primarily maize, wheat, bean, sorghum and oil crops.
CONASUPO purchases around 20% of maize crop at guaranteed prices.  The thrust
of the program is to ensure small farmers a minimam income from their crops
and to stimulate maize production which is the dominant (30% of agricultural
output) crop and the major item in the Mexican diet (25jo of low income family's
food budget). The objective is to also regulate the prices paid to farmers



ANNaC d
Page 5
by other buyers by providing an alternative outlet at a guaranteed price.
The primary causes of past failures in price support programs have
been inadequate budgets of the grain procurement agencies and insufficient
storage space. Without the funds to buy the grains or a place to store them,
there can be no effective market intervention by the Government marketing
agency. CONASUPO has therefore moved forwTard on both of th,ese fronts, so as
to enable it compete effectively with alternative buyers.JI
In addition to the storage space and funds for buying the grain,
CONASUPO also assists in developing related activities, which aim at over-
coming problems caused by the economic interrelationships between the
campesinos and the buyers of their crops. The extension of credit either
consumer or production, can obligate the farmer to sell his crop to the
credit source at inferior prices. The lower prices compensate the credit
givers for their services but the power relationships are unequal and
therefore the possibility of exploitation inevitably emerges. In addition
to credit, buyers can offer other services such as transportation or grain
bags or processing (e.g., drying or shelling), which either tie the crop or
cause the farmer to pay higher prices for these services.
CONASUPO has recognized this multi-faceted nature of the producer
crop marketing process and, therefore, is attempting to surround the crop
buying, warehousing, and price support program with the ancillary services
needed to operate as a viable regulator and direct participant in the rural
produce marketing system. The resultant improved economic benefits to the
campesinos can significantly increase their savings and consumption capacities.
3. Rural Consumer Credit
CONASUPO's consumer credit program through 1973 was relatively
small with total credit under a coupon system amounting to US$170,000.
Another 30 cooperative stores were programmed to be added to the credit
program in 197L.  In addition, several of the Institutional Store systems
(e.g., Instituto Mexicano del Cafe, La Forestal) were developing credit
schemes for implementation in 1974. Because of the favorable experience
in the initial program (100% credit repayment), the Secretaria de Hacienda
y Credito PCblico extended to the regional banks the authority to provide
guarantees to consumer credit loans. BANAGRO (national agricultural bank)
has manifested strong support for this new credit concept and has incorporated
a rural consumer credit line into its 1974 programming.
1/  CONASUPO surveys reveal that the small farmers retain about 69' of their
crop for on-farm consumption, which 40% of farmers sell some or all of
their crop.  Approximately 17% of the corn they sell goes to CONASUP
The reason for selling to CONASUPO was stated by 79% to be the better
prices. The major reasons given for not selling to OONASUPO were distance
(30%), crop promised to others (23%), and lack of information (15,0).  Most
of the corn not sold to CONASUPO has traditionally been channeled to
various intermediaries: local merchants (20.9%), grain buyers (21.4%),
and truckers (12.6%) due to inability of farmers to arrange for direct
sales of maize to a relatively distant CONASUPO warehouse.



AhNEX 3d
Page 6
The oDerating mechanisms for this system vary depending on the
particular economic situation of the campesinos and the organizational
characteristics of the collaborating institutions. Generally, however,
CONASUPO issues coupons to the campesinos on a monthly basis up to a
predetermined credit line. The campesinos redeem the counons during the
month for merchandise at the CONASUPO rural store.  The campesinos can pay
off all or part of their credit balance at any time within the year. After
the second month. they must pay interest on the outstanding balances at the
commercial rate of around 11%. In those cases where a third party such as
BANAGRO is involved an additional dimension is added, namely, the bank
guarantees the consumer loan against nonpayment.  In other instances CONASUPO
might extend the credit to the cooperating institution which in turn would
carry out whatever credit program it deemed appropriate, but it would carry
the financial responsibility to CONASUPO for the credit.
. Benefits
CONA.SUPO's rural marketing program holds considerable potential for
generating a significant socio-economic development impact on the low income
segments of rural IMIexico, especially in the integrated approach of PIDER.
The strength of the program lies in five areas:  strategy, infrastructure,
personnel, and management risks and financial benefits to rural people.
a. Strategy
CONASUPO's development strategy is based in the realities of the
economic environment of the campesinos. It gives full recognition to the
nature and adverse effects of the economic terms of trade between the
campesino and the other participants (suppliers and buyers) in the rural
economy. Unless these structural relationships are explicitly addressed
the imnact of other development efforts could be dissipated and the economic
benefits siphoned off by others rather than reaching the target campesino
group. The constraint imposed by the economic structure are multiple and
require a multifaceted approach.
CONASUPO has designed such a multi-pronged program and it is one
that gives cognizance to the reality that the marketing of inputs (consump-
tion and production) to the rural sector is intimately interrelated with the
marketing of outputs (produce) from the rural sector. They can be isolated
in theory only, to do so in practice is to tug at only part of the web of
underdevelopment. The final aspect of the CONASUPO strategy which is
particularly appealing is that the approach is not aimed simply to income
redistribution and resultant short-run increases in standard of living as
an end in itself, rather, the goal is to increase the income of the campesino
so that it can be channeled into productive investments which can be the
basis for an ongoing sustained development. Also in this context is an
emphasis on involving the campesino in a meaningful way in the design of
their own development plans and process.



ANNOt 3d
Page 7
b. Physical Infrastructure
CONASUPO has assembled a sizable and strategically located network
of retail stores and storage facilities with ancillary services. It has
developed a rural delivery system capable of reaching deeply and broadly
into the rural sector with relative efficiency. The lower input (consumption
and production) prices possible through this system will dramatically increase
campesino purchasing power which probably will result in an increased intake
of food. This may well positively affect the nutritional status of rural
consumers; however, if additional attention is paid to increasing the
nutrient quality of the food, greater impact on nutritional status night be
possible.
c. Personnel
Although a broad survey of CONASUPO personnel was not made, a
spectrum from top managers to village store operators was interviewed. The
resultant impression was that most CONASUPO staff and line personnel are
reasonably competent and highly motivated relative to what one frequently
observes in public entities. The personnel training programs, especially
those at the lower levels of Grain Analyst and Store Manager, generally
appear to be effective. The organization does not seem to be a resting
grounds for dormant bureaucrats, although an inevitable portion of personnel
undoubtedly fall into this category. There seems to be a genuine concern
for not padding the payroll with additional, unproductive bodies.
d. Management
Although CONASUPO's management systems are neither the most
miodern nor the most efficient in Mexico, they are relatively well designed
and effective. There is a top management appreciation of modern management
techniques and many have been instituted; for example, a Programming-
Planning-Budgeting system and a computer based management information
system were being instituted in early 1974. There is an understanding of
the limitations or organizational centralization and the need for decentral-
ization in order to increase coverage and penetration in the rural sector.
Finally, there seems to be a reasonable control system.
e. Risks
The CONASUPO program, of course, also has a number of risks.
First, although it has developed a fairly broad and integrated package of
services and facilities for its rural development effort, these attach only
a certain portion of the development bottlenecks in the rural sector.
Accordingly, to realize their full potential CONASUPO's efforts must be
integrated into and coordinated with other key inputs (e.g., production
credit, penetration roads, irrigation facilities, education) aimed at the
other bottlenecks.



ANN EX 3d
Page b
Second, the CONASUPO effort is critically dependent on maintaining
a highly motivated and well trained headquarters and field staff. The
strain of rapid program expansion is great and continual attention must be
paid to the quality of training and to in-house personnel development programs.
Third, the decentralization move creates new requisites in terms of control
mechanisms and perceptions toward the delegation of responsibility; care
must be exercised to strike the right balance between authority and responsi-
bility, control and delegation. Fourth, there is an ongoing need for sys-
tematic and quantitative evaluation of program results; such evaluation in the
past has been more impressionistic than systematic, even though a spirit of
frank self-evaluation does exist. Evaluation becomes a critical feedback
mechanism for program planning in any expanding and innovative development
effort.

MEXICO
INTEGRATED RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
MINISTRY FOR AGRARIAN REFORM (SRA)
SRA. Objectives and Structure
1e          When formulating the Integrated Rural Development Program
(PIDER), the Mexican govenment was fully aware of the key importance for
the success of such a program of (a) bringing order into the chaotic legal
situation of land tenure and (b) assuring a massive input of organizational
and developmental assistance to make up for decades of neglect. A new
Agrarian Law was drafted in 1970 - the first major piece of agrarian legisla-
tion in thirty years - and was enacted by the Congress in March 1971. The
law makes no fundamental changes in agrarian policy. Rather, it attempts to
correct some of the more serious problems which have plagued this sector for
decades, puts new teeth into enforcement measures and mechanisms, provides
for a substantial reform of the administrative structure for agrarian affairs,
and assigns high priority to assistance for the social and economic develop-
ment of the country's small farmers.
2.          SRA, the  Ministry  for Agrarian  Reform, was
assigned major responsibility for the implementation of this new law. For this
purpose, SRA was completely reorganized and restaffed from top to bottom.
It has considerably improved with respect to the number and quality of its staff,
its organization and methods and the pace of work. SRA 's budget jumped from
US $ 11 million in 1971 to US $ 80 million in 1973, and is further being
strengthened with PIDER funds and by the proposed Project. The department,
whose head is a cabinet-level official directly responsible to the President,
is now divided into four functional secretariats: (a) Agrarian Affairs;
(b) Ejido Organization and Development (SOFE); (c) New Population Centers and
Colonization; and (d) Legal Counsel. Each secretariat is headed by a young
and vigorous Secretary General and comprises several functional Directorates
General. The two most important secretariats are Agrarian Affairs, responsible
for land tenure, and SOFE, charged with community development.
Land Tenure
3.          Mexico's rural population comprises three main groups: ejidatarios
(para 4 ), private farmers and the landless. There are about 1.5 million
ejiditarios with 44 million ha. of land, 1.2 million privately ownled farms
with 125 million ha., and 1.2 million landless families. The Constitution pro-
vides that individual private property is limited to a maximum of 100 ha.
of irrigated land, or 200 to 800 ha. of rainfed land, depending on the ecolo-
gical zone and the slope of the land. Thus, from the time of the 1915 Revo-
lution to 1965, some 60 million ha. had been redistributed. Yet, the 1960
census still showed considerable inequality in the distribution of the re-
maining private land, as fo11ows:

4.            Ejidos. The bulk of the redistributed land was organized in
ejidos !/. An ejido is essentially a joint and inalienable land holding arrange-
ment for a group of farmers who made a successful claim to all or part of a
former hacienda under the provisions of existing agrarian legislation and the
Constitution. Joint tenure was meant primarily to protect the ejidatarios
against renewed usurpation of their newly acquired land by former landlords or
money lenders. The ejidos were not conceived as production units. In fact, in
addition to conferring a limited collective property title to the land to the
ejido as a whole, the law also provides that the head of each family is to be
issued a certificate of usufruct right to a particular parcel of arable land
within the ejido. This right may not legally be rented or sold, nor bequeathed
without government permission. Abandonment legally signifies loss of rights.
In practice, therefore, the croplaad of the vast majority of ejidos has been
worked in individual parcels. However, grazing land, forests and other non-
arable land have not been parcelled, but are, at least formally, exploited col-
lectively. Owing to the legal provisions regarding the procedure for land
claims and ejido establishment, the communities have remained relatively small,
comprising typicaIly between 50 and 300 families of ejidatarios.
5.            There are also about 2,000 "Indigenous Communities" with a popula-
tion of some 200,000 families. While the historical basis for their land tenure
rights differs somewhat from that of the ejidos, the legal status of those
rights is the same. In the culturally isolated coamunities, vestiges of the
traditional tribal community structure are found and the land mazy, in fact,
be farmed collectively0 The communities may apply for ejido status. For the pur-
poses of this Annex, they are included in the discussion of ejidos and ejida-
tarios. A special agency, the National Indigenous Institute, has been taking
special responsibility for these commnmities in some areas (Annex 3 1).
21 The term is derived from the Spanish equivalent of the village common, based
on the concept that the land reform returned the usurped village lands to
their rightful owners - the farners, called "ejidatarios". The ejido com-
imunity is represented by an elected "comisariado" of three members: a
president, secretary and treasurer.



ANNEX  3 e
Page   3
6.           Parcel Size. The ejidos cover 25 percent of the total farmland,
43 percent of the cropland, and 42 percent of the irrigable land. TWihen all land
expropriable under present legislation is redistributed, some 53 percent of
all land will be in ejidos. Except for illegally large private holdings which
may be discovered as a result of the new political push in this direction and
limited areas of virgin land in the extreme south (where additional "colonies"
will be established), the land still available for redistribution in Mexico
under present limits on private property, would suffice to settle barely
five percent of the landless campesinos.
7.           Although the Constitution guarantees each ejidatario 10 ha. of
irrigated land, or 20 ha. of dry rainfed land, the man/land ratio was never
great enough to implement this statute except in a few areas. In 1960, the
distribution of cropland parcel sizes on ejidos (cropland comprising 23 percent
of all ejido land) was as follows:
1 ha. or less                       9.7  4
