4.1 to 10.0 ha.                    40.5 D0
more than 10 ha.                   15.3 %
Total:                             100.0 %
The mean area of cropland per legal parcel in 1960 was only 6.5 ha.,
and of irrigated land, only 0.95 ha. In most rainfed areas, the parcels have
been insufficient to provide a decent living for the average ejidatario family.
Only in some of the irrigated areas is there a theoretically adequate resource
base.
8.          The ejido situation has been aggravated over the years by the
growth of the population and the inability to find non-agricultural work. For
every ejidatario, there is at present at least one grown son without any formal
rights in the land, who must either hire himself out as a day laborer to
larger farmers, or share in the meager proceeds of the family parcel while
awaiting the passing away of his father before he can acquire land rights of
his own.
90           Legal Status. The above problems were aggravated by the organiza-
tional and legal neglect from which the ejidos had been suffering over the
past decades. From the point of view of agrarian procedures, the most glaring
neglect had been  SRA's failure to issue the equivalents of land titles to
even one-third of existing ejidos, and certificates of membership and usufruct
to the members of these and other ejidos. In fact, the exact number of ejidos
and ejidatarios was not known, despite repeated censuses. At the end of 1972,
there were 8,000 unresolved cases of collective ejido tenure certificates.
In addition, half the ejidatarios were still awaiting their provisional, indi-
vidual certificates of membership, and hardly any had received a final cer-
tificate of rights to a specific parcel. Some had been waiting for 20 years.



ANNEX 3 e
Page  4
10.         This situation has, of course, led to widespread, and at times
serious, conflict over boundaries among ejidos, between ejidos and private
owners, and especially among ejidatarios. Abuses have been rampant with
little or no corrective action being taken by any public agency. The law
specifically forbids the renting of ejido land either among ejidatarios or to
outsiders and provides that "tabandoned" parcels are to be reassigned to
landless campesinos. All ejidatarios are required to reside in the ejido and
to work on their parcels or in the-collective land of the ejido unless
there is a special dispensation, e.g. for widows and ivalids. Yet, a substan-
tial proportion of ejido parcels are rented out by ejidatarios who are either
unable or unwilling to farm thee or who have other sources of income, and
have even moved to a town.
11.         Recent Developments. The present administration is committed by
law and by public pronouncements to a massive program of cleaning up the entire
administrative and legal backlog. To this end, the Secretariat of Agrarian
Affairs has been strengthened considerably. Since the beginning of 1973, when
the Secretariat began to operate at full speed, Presidential Resolutions have
been signed for about 1,000 ejidos of the 8,000 which had not yet received
their collective charters. But 7,000 of these cases were still pending in
mid-1974 (including 3,000 New Population Centers), covering 13 million ha.,
one-third of the total number of ejidos in the country. In addition,
1,000 tenrare rights certificates were pending for Indigenous Communities.
As regards the membership and parcel tenure certificates of individual ejida-
tarios, DAAC had issued a cumulative total of 2.4 million such certificates
by mid-1974, leaving "only" 400,000 cases unresolved. In "colonies" in the
southern virgin lands, 7 million ha. were awaiting final surveying and tenure
regularization by mid-1974. At the same time, progress is finally being made in
the issuance of "certificates of inaffectability". These give private land-
owners, whose holdings do not exceed the legal limit, security against any
further expropriation under the agrarian legislation.
Ejido Organization and Development.
12.         While the Secretariat for Agrarian Reform  is beyond question
laying the legal (and psychological) foundation for a successful development
effort in the ejido sector, the most important secretariat in the DAAC in
the long run is that for Organization and Development of the Ejidos (SOFE).
This secretariat represents an entirely new activity for the DAAC. In the past,
DAAC had virtually nothing to do with developmental activities, and virtually
abdicated the important role which it should have played in supervising and
aiding the social and economic organization of the ejidos.
13.         SOFE is divided into four Directorates General which deal, respec-
tively, with organization, development, planning and training. The latter is
known as the National Institute for Farmer Training (INACAP). Aside from a
probably excessive headquarters staff of nearly 1,000, the Secretariat already
has another 1,000 people in the field in all states.  The field work of the
SEA brigades comprises three theoretically sequential tasks: (a) "sensitivi-
zation"; (b) orientation or "awareness creation"; and (c) reorganization
and development.



A1NEX 3 e
Page   5
The approach is designed to avoid a paternalistic imposition
of reorganizational initiative from above. Rather, it is designed to gradual-
ly prepare the understandably diffident campesino communities concerned for
self-determination and subsequent assumption of initiative for development.
Dependirn on prevailing attitudes and the existing organizational and human
development in the ejidos concerned, DAAC expects that the intensive, initial
work leading to a complete reorganization, including election of new officers,
takes from 6 to 12 months in each ejido. Detailed guidelines for methodology
have been prepared for the field teams, which undergo one month orientation
courses. Field manuals of specific procedures have also been prepared for use
by them and by ejido officers.
1h.         INACAPts training program, though impressive in size got off to
a false start under improperly focused leadership. Main weaknesses were the
massive but indiscriminate dissemination of literature, correspondence courses
and radio programs, and the divorce between action and training personnel at
the field level. In mid-1974, INACAP's program was completely revamped, and
a master plan for organization and training prepared. This master plan is
designed to bring all farmer organization and training activities, presently
being carried on by a multitude of agencies, under DAAC's umbrella, in
accordance with the statutory responsibility assigned to the Department, and
to put primary emphasis on agriculture. The following targets were set:

Accomplishments and Remaining Problems
15.          SRA's involvement in PIDER is substantial, both in terms of in-
puts and results. In 1974, 552 or 55 percent of DAAC's personnel were being
paid with PIDER funds and active in the micro regions. Conceptually, DAAC's
conmmuity development brigades function as the spearhead of PIDERts develop-
ment activities at the community level. Unless the communities and their
members become aware of their resources and potential, and develop their own
capabilities for problem identification and solving and for negotiating
directly with the outside world - public and private - the physical investments
being made in their welfare are likely to remain monuments to failure. This is
DAAC's ostensible philosophy, and the leaders of its Secretariat for Ejido
Organization and Development are making an earnest and dedicated effort to im-
plement this philosophy.
16.         In practice, however, there is still inadequate interaction
between SHA's organizational effort and the other investments in services,
credit and physical works provided under PIDER. SRA does not yet fully play
the role of general broker between the ejidos and the development program that
the master plan spells out for the future. Largely for this reason PIDERts
planning is still essentially done from above rather than from the grass roots,
and the local communities, by-and-large, have not yet become full participants
in the development process. To date, SRA's contribution has been conceived
as just another input, parallel to those of the other agencies, rather than
as the hub of the program that determines the viability of the rest. Moreover,
there are still instances of political resistance to  SRAts objectives at the
state level which make it virtually impossible for SRA's personnel to make
an impact on the PIDER activities.
17.         Except for the supervisors, the personnel of these SRA brigades
tend to be mostly young people fresh from the university who receive a one-
month orientation before being sent to the field. Since SOFE expanded very
rapidly in a short period while it was still exploring its new approach to
community development, it is hardly surprising that staffing constituted a
major problem. Particular difficulties were encaountered in recruiting,
training and managing the thousand new field workers, who were largely un-
prepared for and unaccustomed to, this environment. SRA 's training division
should provide further in-service training as the new program becomes fully
operational, and CIDERts evaluation effort should be instrumental to help
SRA in learning from doing.
18.         A particular area of potential conflict and lack of cooperation
exists between the DAAC brigades and the extension service teams in the micro
regions. This problem arises in part from the fact that nearly one-half of
SRAts professional and technical personnel in the micro regions are agricul-
tural technicians. These technicians are inevitably tempted to dispense agri-
cultural advice; indeed, this is a sure way to gain the confidence of an
ejido assuming that the advice is well thought out and acceptable. This is,
however, clearly unacceptable to the extension personnel, who see this as their
responsibility. Limiting technical agricultural advice and assistance to the
extension service would imply, of course, a very close working relationship
between the personnel of the two teams, as well as a thorough acquaintance of



ANNEX 3 e
Page   7
extension personnel with DAAC's aims and methods. Both agencies as well as
PIDELRTs managemwt are now aware of this problem and should make a special
effort towards an effective solution.
19.         Along with the approximately two million ejidatarios of infrasubsist-
ence  size, there are also about 900,000 private farms in this category. In the
past, however, SRA has concentrated almost entirely on the ejidos and indi-
genous communities, which are much easier to identify, approach and service.
The private smallholders are either dispersed or may live in villages with
non-poor farmers and non-agricultural population. They also tend to be more in-
dividualistic than the typical ejidatario and are thus harder to organize.
Nevertheless, SRA recognizes that the practical advantages of taking care of
the ejido needs first do not exempt the Government nor its agencies from pro-
viding at least comparable facilities for the small private famners. Thus,
SRA should make a special effort to (a) develop a theoretical approach to
organizing smallholders, among themselves and jointly with ejidatarios, and test
the model or models in one or more micro-regions, (b) exploit fully any ob-
viaUs opportunities for organizing sma lholders and servicing them on an or-
ganized basis.

MEXICO
RURAL DEV21OPMENT PROJECT
Extension and Field Demonstration
Background
About half the micro-region populations subsist in semi-arid and
arid climatic regions that together cover 77%0 of the total land area.l/ In
addition, rainfall in other areas is often poorly distributed over the years,
creating considerable uncertainties for farming systems without access to
irrigation. Of the 15 PIDER micro-regions submitted in late 1974 to the
IBRD, seven are located in areas defined as semi-arid and one in an area
defined as arid.  For the other seven micro-regions, _ucated in the better rainfall
areas (700 mI  or higher), the technology for improved rainfed crop and
livestock production is reasonably well-developed. The principal effort
in these areas is needed on wider extension of known techniques such as
pasture improvement, crop rotation, improved seeds and fertilizer applica-
tion.
Equally, the technology and extension practices for the irrigated
are as in the drier micro-regions and in the drier parts of the other seven
micro-regions is adequately covered by PLAMELPA, especially for the type
of basic crops (grain, fruit and fodder) that small farmers produce in the
early years of irrigated agriculture.  PLAMELPA is the aggressive extension
arm of SRH. However, the technology and extension Dractices for the
improved production of agriculture in areas below 700 mm is not as well
developed. Under the Project, a major effort is being made to expand more
widely existing technologies in the higher rainfall and irrigated districts
and to consolidate and expand new technologies (derived from Plan Puebla,
INTA, CONAZA, etc. research and selected national research) for improving
the production of rainfed agriculture in drier areas.
This inadequate rainfall and its uncertain distribution has a
number of implications for the design of strategies supporting technical
innovations in the drier areas. Specialists from the staff supporting the
Plan Puebla strategy argue as follows:
a.  In rainfed areas that       receive 750-9OO mm of
annual precipitation, severe drought in maize
can be expected one to two years in each 10, and
moderate drought in another 2 to 3-1/P years in 10.
This signifies intermediate or moderate risk for
1/ Of Mexico's total land area, about 50% is arid (500 mm or less), and 10`
is semi-arid (500-750 mm). Another 15% of land gets 750-900 mm, but these
areas are also dry for `-6 months of the year, and there are great fluc-
tuations around the average from year to year.  Over 17% of Mexico's
population live in the arid regions (500 mm or less).



AN1TEX 3f
Page 2
maize producers.   Depending on initial yield levels,
average maize yields can be increased tw.ro to fourfold
through the use of existing technology packages.
b.  In areas receiving 500-750 mm of annual precipitation
the expected frequency of severe drought in malze is
2-4 years in each 10, and of moderate drought nearly
flour additional years in each 10.   Farmers assume a
high level of risk.  Present indications are that
average maize yields i-n these regions can be increased
only moder.-tely,   perhaps by no more than 50-75,5.
c. Where rainfall is less than 500 mm per year, maize can
be expected to suffer moderate to severe drought damage
every year.   The level of risk is too high to permit the
use of costly inputs like fertilizer.
T'he t=;ical subsistence farmer in these drier areas has barely enough
land to cover his famaily's basic food requirements of maize and beans during an
average year.   In a good year, he may have a little surplus to sell, and in a
bad year he may have to borrow money to buy part of the famil-V food needs.
Traditionally, maize and beans have been the crops produced almost exclusively
by subsistence farmers in these areas.   It is futile to speculate on whether
ecology determined dietary habits or vice versa:   at the time these patterns
became established, there were no "cropping alternatives".   Cattle raising is
an important ancillary activity but it must be practiced on a very extensive
scale in view of the poor (and already seriously overgrazed) pasture.   It also
does not provide much employment.   Since non-irrigated agriculture until very
recently hardly participated in government sponsored programs of modernization,
93% of Mexican farmers in 1960 were classified as subsistence or below sub-
sistence level.
Maize and beans:  the main staples.    '.-aize occupies about 8 million
ha, i.e., about half of the crop land in the country.   Maize occupies over 65%
of the crop land of the tropical gulf, Pacific south and central regions;  these
three regions contain over 70% of Mexico's maize land.   It is estimated that
one-third of the harvested maize area is economically marginal to maize growing,
mainly because of water deficiency.
Beans are second only to maize in the Mexican diet.  1More than one
million hectares of beans are harvested each year;  about half of this area is
grown interplanted with maize by subsistence farmers.   The main producing areas
are the north central states and the higher central plateau.   The main problems
related to bean production are diseases, poor yielding characteristics of native
strains and rreat heterogeneity of qualit-y factors.
During the 19L0's, maize output and yield grew fastest in the central
plateau, where improved open-pollinated varieties were introduced to farmers.



AIThiEX 3f
Page 3
This was matched only in the newly opened tropical lands in the south.
During the 1950's, only about 3% of all the maize land was planted with
officially produced hybrids, while distribution of open-pollinated varieties
was discontinued.   In the early 1960's the percentage increased to 13% and
fertilizer application was intensified in response to programs such as in
Jalisco and in the State of Mlexico, with the result that yields have increased
in these areas.
Data for 1960-68 indicate that the expansion of area under cultivation
of maize and beans, as well as their yields, rose at a significant rate.   Their
yield performance, however, was greatly overshadowed by that of wheat.   (On the
other hand, output of another crop of great importance to campesinos, in humid
and irrigated areas, sugar cane, showed a sizeable expansion only in area, reflect-
ing newly cultivated land in the tropical south.)
The Puebla Project.  There has been some misunderstanding of the nature
and implications of the Puebla Project both within and outside ihdexico.  Basically,
it is a combined field research-extension program.   Initially, it was focused
exclusively on determining economica'lly optimal plant density and fertilizer appli-
cations for locally grown maize varieties.   Its "secrets" consist of (a) the selec-
tion and motivation of the technical personnel, (b) the selection and training of
campesino communicators to serve as field assistants, (c) working through specially
organized groups of voluntary participants, and (d) the function of the technicians
as agents of change not only among the campesinos but vis-a-vis the banks, the
insurance agency and the fertilizer suppliers.
The program was started in 1967 involving 117,000 ha (80,000 in maize)
and about 43,000 small farmers (ejidotarios and smallholders).   Approximately
1O'O became direct participants in the program.   A total of 33 professionals partic-
ipated in the project from 19967 to 1973, and the annual input of professional time
varied from 2.2 man-rears in the first year to a maximum of 12.3 man-years (includ-
ing an evaluator) in 1971.   Through successive approximations based on both
controlled field trials and actual producer results, 16 different production
systems on different soil types (i.e., an average of 2,800 ha per system) were
identified over the years, and recommendations were developed for each with regard
to (a) optimum planting time, (b) plant density and (c) application rates for
nitrogen and phosphorus.
IVeasurement of the effect of the Project on income and employment
unfortunately has been limited so far to the first four years (through 1970),
i.e., before the new technology was completely developed.   Yet, real income
per family was found to have increased by 24% in general, and from crops alone
by nearly h45' (gross income per hectare from maize production increased by the
same percentage).   But since participants were mainly crop producers, while live-
stock is rather unevenly distributed among the farmers, average total family income
of farmers in the credit lists was still 6.65O less than the average for all farmers.
Average labor input per hectare of maize increased by 30', and it is estimatecd that
the average family spent 11% more time growirng maize, an admittedly small employment



MNNEX 3f
increase compared to existing level of underemployment.   The level of' aspira-
tion towards success in farming increased markedly.
Plan Puebla and PIDER Extension/Field Research
Under PIDER, the experiences of the Puebla Project are being adapted
and widened.   One example is the effort being made in the Zacapoaxtla micro-
region where a program called Plan Zacapoaxtla has been operating for nearly
two years with SAG sponsorship under the PIDEP. financial stimulus.
The purpose of Plan Zacapaoxtla is to transfer to the micro-region
the experience gained and the methodology elaborated in other parts of the state
under Plan Puebla.   The main thrust of this Plan is (i) to carry out field
research programs on the crops of the micro-region (corn, bean, potatoes) as
well as on fruit production, (ii) to implement technological innovations and
(iii) to promote the use of credit to finance the inputs required by farmers
willing to improve their technology.   This Plan, wh ch replaces SAG's tradi.tion-
al extension services in the area, will also act as a coordinator of the invest-
ments made by this agency in the field of soil conservation, supplementary
irrigation, fruit production and livestock improvement centers.   The ultimate
goal of Plan Zacapaoxtla, which will benefit heavily from the experience gained
under Plan Puebla, is to increase yields in the following manner:

In terms of extension, the goal of Plan Zacapaoxtla is to reach

Applied Research
On a wider scale, the principal and most immediate research thrust
needed for the semi-arid regions is the selection of local, high yielding
varieties of maize (and beans), accompanied by field trials of optimum seeding
density and fertilizer applications similar to the Plan Zacapaoxtla effort.
This may be followed, or accompanied by, selection of other crop species and
varieties that are shown, or believed to be, suitable for the area.   Field
trials would demonstrate whether such new crops are capable of raising small
farmer incomes sufficiently above those of the traditional crops.   This field
research extension approach is also being tested in the Sur de Nuevo Leon micro-
region with pastural results to date.
Research Capability
Starting from virtually no public infrastructure for agricultural
research and the dissemination of its results until about 30 years ago, Iexico
has made substantial progress, particularly in the past five years, in its
efforts to fill this void.   On the whole, technological achievements, particularly
the spectacular breakthroughs. in wheat (with considerable external inputs in all
respects) have been made in irrigated crops, and private enterprise has been
responsible for the bulk of developments in export crops.   Dissemina efforts until
very recently had also been virtually confined to irrigated agriculture, with a
heavy concentration on commercial-size farms rather than on the small producer.
Against this historical background, the current effort to reorient a greatly
reinforced service infrastructure toward the small, marginal producer is enormous,
at least in quantitative terms.   Qualitatively, it is seriously circumscribed by
the thin research base on wchich this effort is built.
Nevertheless, where irrigation is being developed for the benefit of
small producers in PIDER micro-regions, both applicable technology and delivery
systems (including credit and often also marketing and processing) are quite
adequate.   IThat is lacking, are low-risk technological packages applicable to
the semi-arid regions as well as methodologies and suitably prepared and motivated
personnel for their rapid introduction as and when they become available.
The history of agricultural research in Mexico goes back only about
30 years.   In 1960, Mexico integrated the existing organizations into the National
Institute of Agricultural Research (INIA).   A sister orgatization, the Institute
for Livestock Research (INIP), was also subsequentially created.   Significantly,
there was no published material available regarding research results until 1960.
As of 1960, the total Mexican budget for agricultural research amounted to only
14*27 million (US$2.2 million) of which more than M$8 million (US$1.6 million)
were contributed by the Rockefeller Foundation.   Up to 1970, the Foundation
gradually reduced its contributions but government's budget remained unvaried.
A staff of about 250 professionals and semi-professionals was maintained, distri-
buted among 28 experiment sub-stations in 1961, and 32 stations by 1964.



ATITEX 3f
Page 6
Capability for dealing with the problems of small farmers in the less well
endowed regions was limited by previous experience of staff and by the tradi-
tional attachment of local research centers to the large private land-owning
interests.   iloreover, most of the technicians were not trained to deal with
problems of animal husbandry, pastures, fruits and vegetables.
Until 1942 there were hardly any improved seeds available to Mexican
farmers.   During the early years of agricultural research in Miexico, highest
priority was assigned to research on maize and wheat.   The Government agency
which monopolized seed maize production by 1961 had produced seed for only
about 2% of the country's maize land, but output subsequently rose to a maximum
of 12'.   Mloreover, the 60 varieties of improved maize seed available were
suited to only about two-fif-ths of the land under maize.
Present research institutions.  Although available resources increased
since 1970, SAG's agricultural research infrastructure, as represented by INTIA
and TINTIP, appears still quantitatively adequate to respond to the challenge of
the PIDER program.   Clear policy directives are needed if the institutions are
to respond to the needs of PTDER.  INIA now has eight regional crop research
centers and 40 sub-centers.   These are still by-and-large located in the more
productive agricultural ateas, but recently many experimental fields have been
established in poorer areas.   INIA presently has 450 professional researchers.
LNIP has a total of 11 livestock research centers around the country.   Seven
of these are located in the tropical south, the area of greatest potential for
expansion of cattle production, and two are in highland areas.   There are only
two centers - in cooperation with cattlement's associations - in the vast areas
of semi-arid cattle raising.   Both INTA and INIP do pasture research, although
the bulk of whatever is being done in this field is INIP's responsibility.
Still, only one of the centers (Chihuahua) has data on dryland pasture regenera-
tion, and little attention seems to be paid to this aspect.
Other public institutions (in addition to many of the agricultural
colleges) are also engaged in research, *some of it in coordination with INTA
and INIP.   SRH reinforces SAG's research efforts in small-scale irrigated
farming, including management aspects.   And CNIZA (National Center for Research
in Arid Zones) has an active, though low-level program for research on arid zone
resource management and plant technology.
Extension
Agricultural extension work became institutionalized in Mexico in the
1950's about 10 years after the first initiatives in publicly sponsored research.
While the Ministry of Agriculture has had principal responsibility in this field,
other agencies have also played a role; some of them - such as SRH in irrigation -
for many years, others more recently.
SAG's extension service was created in 1953, but until 1963 its staff was
confined to a total of 230 agronomists.   However, in 1964, several institutions
were merged and staff rose to 435.   During the early years extension staff was



ANNEX 3f
Page 7
concentrated, as was research, in the eight agriculturally most important states.
TUntil five years ago, SAG's agricultural extension personnel comprised only
graduates of agricultural and veterinary colleges (Ingenieros Agronomos and
Mledicos Veterinarios Zooteenistas - or IIVZ's).   Middle-level agricultural
technicians (Peritos and Tecnicos Agricolas) began to be employed in 1969, and by
the end of 1972h one-fourth of the 1,070 agricultural extension technicians
were middle-level personnel working in about half the states.   From the start,
the extension service included home advisers (sub-professional female social
workers trained in special schools), and by 1967, there were 100 home advisers
working in eight states.   Doth types of middle-level technicians are typically
of campesino background, unlike the professionals who are more likely to come
from urban, middle-class families.
Extension and PTDER.  In mid-1974, a total of 741l SAC extension person-
nel was assigned to 33 micro-regions under PIDER financing.   hiddle-level agri-
cultural extensionists were present in only 2Lh of the micro-region teams.   This
reflects, on the one hand, the lack of a uniforn criterion at the 1.inistary level
and, on the other, continued prejudice among old-line SAG professionals in the
field.   The impact of SAG's agricultural extension workers on farm technology
in the micro-regions is likely to be relatively small, due to the absence of a
dry-areas technical package, inadequate staff preparation in experience and lack
of institutional coordination.   Numbers are more than sufficient and thev have,
by-and-large, adequate transportation and technical backstopping.
The real problem is a qualitative one.   This has to do, on the one
hand, with the large proportion of recently graduated technicians, their lack
of experience and the nature of their technical training and on the other hand
with their preparation and motivation for dealing effectively with small farners.
The training in agricultural colleges is generally said to be inadequate to equip
a new graduate for giving acceptable advice to small farmers.   There is a  lack
of emphasis on practice - especially as regards the particular problems of small
farming.   Moreover, technical training received in one ecological area - e.g.,
the dry, temperate North - is often not adaptable to the agriculture of a different
region - e.g., the humid, sub-tropical South.   SAG is attempting to overcome
these deficiencies through short courses, both in the field and at central locations.
Efforts are being made to create a close nexus between the extensionists
and the field researchers of INIA and INIP.   However, the administrative separation
of extension and research appear to make it difficult to establish real, continuous
two-way communication.   Another problem is the high rate of turnover of field
personnel particularly in 197h.   With most micro-regions not fully staffed until
late 1973, the aggregate number of field resignations in 1974 was 259 (1973 resig-
nations were 111) and amounted to 35% of the PIDER-authorized staffing as of mid-
year.   The main cause appears to be the rapid expansion in demand from many
pub lic agencies who offered more attractive salaries.
SAG is not the only institution providing technical assistance in micro-
regions.   Above all, other SAG departments - in addition to the two research
institutes - are heavily involved in their particular specialized activities:
Agricultural Engineering (Water); Soil and Water Conservation, Forestriy and

Page 3
Livestock each with its own substantial allocation of PIDE1 funds.   Even
though they do not assign personnel exclusively to micro-regions, their
state staff sometimes need to be strengthened to enable them to carry this
increased workload.   But coordination among these activities and with
extension agents is often lacking.   Extension-type activities of SRX and
the banks are perhaps more effective than those of the extension service
because they are almost always focused on specific projects, generally on
irrigated land.   Their personnel are not assigned specifically to micro-
regions but their overall capability for dealing with the country's small
farmers and poorer regions has been strengthened materially in recent years:
a.  The Department of Operation of Small Irrigation Units, of

Whenever the investments in the PIDER areas justify the full-time presence

Most PIDER regions though, will have smaller units of intensive

The Unidad de Servicios Ejidales (Ejido Service Units) USE
The unidad de servicios ejidales (USE) model has been implemented
by the Banco Agropecuario to give integral technical assistance to ejidos which
are engaged in capital intensive agricultural activities (e.g. fruit orchards,
dairy, intensive beef and hog raising) but on an economic scale which does
not justify the presence of a professional manager. The USE is a small company
organized to provide technical services to several ejidos located in the same
vicinity. This firm is usuaIly headed by an agronomist specialized in farm
management and includes agronomists, accountants-auditors, veterinaries,
economists, organization and communication specialists, according to the
requirements of the ejidos assisted 2 . The technical services are paid for by
the benefitted ejidos, out of production credit, in proportion to the number
of hectares and of livestock served 2/.
Experience shows that the ejidatarios pay willingly the cost of
technical services (which in the above branches amounts to roughly 5% of
total production costs) whenever they feel that they perceive direct benefits
from such services. Providing technical services through a government extension
service brings the following advantages:
a) Adequate technical assistance is guaranteed, since the number and
quality of professional personnel are not limited by a chronically
insufficient extension budget.
b) The technical staff are employees of the ejidos, who can remove
incompetent or excessive technical personnel. Thus the USE
system cuts down both beaurocratic inefficiency and paternalism.
/ The approximate requirements are: 1 agronomist per 2,000 ha., l sociologist-
organizer per 3,000 ha., 1 accountant-auditor per 5,000 ha., 1 economist per
20,000 ha., 1 communication specialist per 60,000 ha.
g The bank participates in the costs of maintaining the USE during an initial
period.



ANNEX 3f
Page 10
c) The USE staff live in the community, unlike extension or bank
functionaries who make periodic visits from headquarters, and
therefore have longer effective working hours and closer contact
with the beneficiaries.
The first USE has recently been installed to serve 8 ejido dairies
(with about 400 beneficiaries and 4,000 cows) in the state of Morelos. Ten
additional USE's are planned for establishment soon in cooperative dairies
of the Sur de Jalisco region. The system promises-to be of general applica-
bility in PIDER regions wherever significant multi-annual productive
investments, fruit -orchards, livestock) are contemplated.

MEXICO
INTEGRATED RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
RURAL EDUCATION
Education in Rural Mexico
1.          Mexico's education system has developed rapidly in the past
decade, but various critical problems remain. A most critical problem is
the severe inequality of education opportunities between urban and rural
areas. The limited efforts in the past to educate the rural population is
evident from the low literacy rate of 59 percent in rural areas as
against 80 percent in urban centers. Current enrollment ratios begin to
show some results of the Government's efforts to redress this imbalance,
while various nonformal education and training activities are being developed
to better meet the needs of the rural population.
2.          Rural Primary Education. In 1970, total enrollments in primary
education were a high 96 percent of the 7 - 12 age group. However, large
numbers of students are overaged as a result of the system's inefficiency,
and the net enrollment ratio was only 71 percent. Efficiency is particularly
low in rural areas where only 15 percent of the students complete the
primary cycle as against 60 percent in the cities. A main reason for this
disparity is the large number (h6 percent) of incomplete rural schools, that
do not offer the full six-year primary school program. As a result, the
lower grades in these schools are swollen by repeaters who cannot continue
into higher grades. A key constraint to the provision of primary education
in rural MIexico is the high dispersion of its population. Some 20 million
people, or 40 percent of the total population live in communities with less
than 2,500 inhabitants, and 3 million of them in communities with less than
200 inhabitants; the minimum under which the establishment of schools would
be uneconomical.
3.          The Government's policy in primary education is directed towards
the full participation of all primary school age youth. To this effect,
11,000 primary classrooms are being constructed per year. However, this con-
struction program is still heavily urban oriented. In 1973, only 20 percent
of the new school capacity under the regular program was constructed in rural
areas, where 40 percent of the population lives. The PIDER program reduced
this imbalance by adding some 4.0 percent to the construction program for
rural classrooms. Further, the M4inistry of Education is developing several
programs to reach the more scattered population, such as mobile classrooms
and part-time boarding facilities.
4.          In recent years, several measures have been taken to improve
the quality of primary education, but much remains to be done. A free textbook



AfNAEX 3 (g)
Page   2
program has made a significant impact on quality, especially in the rural
areas. Also, a massive teacher upgrading program has been instrumental in
improving the quality of particularly rural teachers. Much work has still to
be done, however, on curriculum development and teacher preparation, issues
which go beyond the scope of a rural development project.
5.           -Other Rural Education. A major effort is underway to expand agri-
cultural secondary education, From 1972 to 1974 the number of lower-secondary
agricultural schools (grades 7 - 9) increased from 158 to 466. Another
90 of these schools will be established under the regular school construction
program, many of which in the PIDER micro regions, but without PIDER financing.
A possible Bank-financed education project would extend these schools with
agricultural training facilities. These facilities would serve the dual pur-
pose of strengthening the production training of students and functioning as
focal points for nonformal education and training of the farmers and their
families in the area. Major nonformal education programs are the Cultural
Missions and Rural Development Brigades, while radio and television are
actively used to reach the rural population. The role of the agricultural ex-
tension service is analysed in Annex 3 (f).
6.           Training of Rural Development Staff. As a result of Mexico's in-
creased emphasis on rural development, the number of staff working in this
field has increased rapidly in the last few years, and many new staff have not
received adequate on-the-job training. Thus, there is a need for more syste-
matic training of both new and existing staff. The Agrarian Department (DA.AC)
and the agricultural extension service are both considering the establishment
of a network of centers for pre-service and in-service training of their per-
sonnel. Furthermore, the Center of Research in Rural Development (CIDER) will
have an important training and information function, The center will provide
a range of courses for all staff involved in executing the PIDER program, now
numbering 2,700 in 14 agencies. The courses would be mainly micro-region
based and aim at improving the "staff-campesino" interface at the local level.
Results from the evaluation programs will be introduced in this training
program and diffused through seminars, monthly newsletters and other communi-
cation means. The operation of CIDER would be supported by the Project.
The Project
7.           Content. In line with the Governmentys policy for primary edu-
cation, the Project aims at the establishment of sufficient school capacity
to enroll all primary school-age youth in the communities served by the
Project, i.e. al1 communities in the 30 micro regions with 200 - 3,000 inhabi-
tants. To this end the Project would provide for the construction and furni-
shing of 5h0 new classrooms in new and existing, incomplete primary schools,
thereby expanding school capacity by 27,000 student places, Further, 260 class-
rooms would be replaced and 350 classrooms renovated, thereby improving
school facilities for another 30,500 students. Two of the new schools and
25 of the renovated schools would have part-time boarding facilities to attend
the scattered school population in two micro regions. Teaching materials,
textbooks and staff for these schools would be provided by the Ministry of
Education and financed from its regular recurrent budget. To ensure that this



ANNEX 3 (g)
Page   3
provision be timely and adequate, the location and timing of school con-
struction would be closely coordinated with this Ministry. The resulting en-
rollment expansion in primary education would be 0,3 percent and the increase
in the recurrent budget for education, a marginal 0.1 percent by 1980,
which would be part of the normal increase in the education budget.
8.           Execution. The implementation of this project item would be the
responsibility of CAPFCE, a semi-autonomous federal agency responsible for the
planning and execution of all federal school construction in Mexico. During
the 30 yea-s since its foundation, CAPFCE has increased its capacity from
300 to 15,000 classrooms per year. It has 31 regional offices charged with
on-site supervision of school construction. CAPFCE has been successful in de-
veloping low-cost construction methods. Deliberate policies emphasizing
standardization of design, prefabrication of structural elements and compu-
terization of the construction process have led to an average cost of new
construction amounting to US $ 5,400 per classroom, or US $ 100 per m2 of con-
struction costs. These costs vary widely among the regions, but specially
adapted construction systems keep the cost of constructing in remote areas in
line with regional averages. For example, local materials are used to the
extent possible, and apart from the site, which should be donated by the local
community, other local contributions are actively promoted.
9.           Benefits.  The objective of the primary education component is
essentially to reinforce the long-term benefits of the Project as a whole.
First, by establishing lasting literacy and numeracy, primary education does
condition the access of farmers to modern agriculture and provide a basis
for a more efficient transmission of knowledge and skills by the agricultural
extension service. Second, primary education helps change the attitudes of the
rural population and reduce their resistance to change, thus reinforcing the
basis for sustained econmmic and social development in the long run. Third,
primary education is an important instrument for improving the quality of
rural life, and thus complementary to the pack-age of productive and social
investments provided under the Project.

MEXICO
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
HEALTH
Introduction
Rural health forms part of the Project's minimum package of social
services required to reinforce the basis for sustained development, laid by
the directly productive investments. Better health not only contributes
directly to social welfare as the final aim of development, but also in-
creases labor availability and productivity, improves learning ability,
avoids treatment costs and may eventually lead to a reduction in fertility.
Present Situation of Rural Health Services
As in most developing countries, gastro-intestinal and respiratory
diseases are the leading causes of mortality and morbidity.   They are
amenable to low-cost intervention. However, in rural areas availability,
accessibility and quality of health services are extremely poor. The major
causes are the virtual lack of the lowest tier of health care delivery, i.e.
the community health post, the ineffective operation of the health center
level, the lack of auxiliary and community level manpower, and the almost
exclusive reliance on staffing facilities with medical graduates doing their
"social service" (Pasantes). Related to these are the lack of community
participation in promoting health, the inadequacy of outreach preventive
services and environmental sanitation, and the insufficiency of input
supplies and of recurrent cost financing.
The Public Health Ministry (SSA) is the main responsible agency
for both the development and the operation of rural health care.]  Under
SSA, the lowest level at which the system operates is the health post (Casa
de S-{lud), established in small communities, staffed by an auxiliary nurse
and/or visited periodically by a physician. However, only some 2,000
health posts have been constructed and most of these are not working
efficiently. At the next tier, the standard three-bed health center is
located in small rural towns and is staffed by Pasantes and auxiliary
nurses. As state governments are reluctant to contribute to health center
operating costs, the existing network of about 1,500 health centers is not
being further expanded.  At present, only 8, of health centers operate on
the standard minimum budget required for effective service. Because of
SSk's difficulties in attracting and retaining Pasantes in remote rural
areas, a large proportion of health centers are not being adequately staffed.
As a result most health centers are underutilized. People depend on
traditional healers and midwives and on self-referral to available and
accessible sub-urban and urban hospitals.
1/ The Institute for Indian Affairs (INI) is operating health services in
areas with predominantly indigenous population and the Social Security
Institute (IMSS) is increasingly moving into rural areas.



ANNEX 3h
Page 2
Future Government Policies in Rural Health
The National Health Plan 1974-1983 presents major shifts in
health sector policies, particularly for the rural population, and
establishes ambitious goals for 1983. In rural health, emphasis is being
placed on (i) the promotion of community participation in health care, by
organizing local Health Committees and by training traditional midwives
and local volunteers; (ii) the development of the grass roots level of the
referral system, i.e., health posts operated by the Health Committees; and
(iii) the training and attracting of Community Health Physicians, Pasantes and
auxiliary personnel to staff rural health centers and to supervise and
support health posts. Salary levels are to be substantially increased.
In particular, the Plan envisages the construction of about 10,000 health
posts and the training of some 30,000 community health workers and 10,000
Community Health Physicians by 1983.
In its basic approach, the Plan constitutes a sensible program
of low cost health delivery in rural areas. However, the document lacks
estimates of investment and recurrent costs and assessment of financing
mechanisms and constraints. Moreover, SSA is recommending substantial
further elaboration of the design of the different components and strengthen-
ing of its somewhat limited capabilities for implementation. While there
is more experience on the predominantly medical issues (e.g., the activity-
mix of health workers),criteria for financing, operating and managing the
system have yet to be developed and tested (e.g., community contribution
to operating costs). Therefore, pilot or first phase projects have to pre-
cede large-scale implementation.
The Project
Under the Project, (i) the network of rural health facilities
would be expanded and improved in all micro-regions as part of the minimum
package of social services; and (ii) in three selected micro-regions, a
more comprehensive low cost rural health delivery system would be developed.
Project Micro-Regions (30). The Project would provide for renova-
tion, construction and equipment of about 220 rural health posts and 70
simple rural health centers in all 30 micro-regions.I/ This health component
is quantified in the following:
j   liot including additional health posts and rural health centers
which would be constructed in three selected micro-regions under
the pilot project (page 3).

50o  to 2,500 persons.  The building (70 m2) would include a waiting room,an examination
and treatment room, and a delivery room,and nave only the most elementary equipment.
SSA would promote local contribution of building materials and labor to the equivalent
of at least 20;o of the health post cost. Health posts would be staffed by community
health workers, trained to treat common diseases, conduct immunizations, and to pro-
mote environmental health, nutrition and health education. They would be supervised
by Pasantes. The annual operating cost is estimated to be about US$3.50 per capita
and would be paid for by the community. Assurances would be obtained that health
posts would only be constructed in communities agreeing (1) to contribute at least 20%
to investment and (2) to fully cover non-staff health post operating costs.
Standard, three-bed health centers (type C) would be established by SSA in
rural toiwns with a population of between 3,000 and 10,000 persons and with availability
of ancillary services (transportation, water supply, electricity). They would be
staffed by Pasantes and auxiliary nurses, supervise community health workers and
serve as referral points for health posts. Operating cost woud be about US$7,500 per
annum (not including the Pasante salary of US$4,000). During negotiations, assurances
would be obtained from government that (1) health posts and centers would be appro-
priately staffed and (2) health centers would be adequately budgeted.
Micro-Regions.       In order to develop and improve
criteria for implementation of the National Health Plan (which aims at increased
coverage and effectiveness of rural health services),                micro-regions would
be selected among PIDER micro-regions in the Northern, Central and Southern parts
of the country. Selection criteria would ensure maximum diversity in community
characteristics - particularly population density, social structure, income levels,
health status and disease patterns, and existing health services and resources. The
selection would allow for control areas within and/or outside the micro-regions.



ANNEX 3h
Page 4
Under the Bank's new health policy, the pilot project would explore
alternatives in delivering comprehensive health services in rural areas and
determine the cost-effective design of the different components, including
health-related activities (e.g., nutrition education, water supply and
sanitation). Past experience suggests that the following issues should be
emphasized: (i) ensuring reliance on preventive care; (ii) integrating
environmental sanitation, nutrition and health education activities; (iii)
achieving community involvement and participation; (iv) assuring adequate
and non-distortive financing of local scheme; (v) organizing and super-
vising each level of the system; (vi) designing the curricula for community
health workers, auxiliaries and community physicians; (vii) improving the
effectiveness of the Pasante system; (viii) increasing the attractiveness
of rural practice; and (ix) developing better supply logistics.
In the selected micro-regions, the project would provide for pro-
motion of community participation in health activities, the construction
of additional rural health posts and centers, the preparation of community
health workers and auxiliaries, the in-service training for other personnel,
the development of effective operation and management capacity, and a
strong research and evaluation component. In order to arrive at preliminary
cost estimates, some likely features of the pilot system have been quanti-
fied. Approximately 100 health posts would give coverage to all communities
with populations between 500 and 2,500 persons and would be staffed by
health workers, selected by the community and trained for about three
months on an intermittent basis at a cost of about US$500 each. Some 10
additional health centers would supervise and support 5 to 10 health posts
and would be staffed by Pasantes and/or community health physicians and
auxiliaries with 10 to 18 months training at a cost of between US$1,000 and
US$1,500. Using sample survey and other techniques, the effect of different
health care measures and health-related components of the rural development
package (e.g,, water supply, rural stores) on health status would be
monitored and results be evaluated to derive cost-effectiveness criteria
for rural health policy.
Different sub-units of SSA would be responsible for the design,
implementation, monitoring and evaluation of the pilot project. Other
government agencies (INI, IMSS) and Mexican universities would possibly
be involved. Due to the pilotpijectts resulting from only recent shifts
in health sector policies under the National Health Plan, details of
project organization and management still have to be deteFmined. Under the
Bank's new health policy, Bank staff would participate in the design, imple-
mentation and evaluation of the pilot project, in order to develop criteria
for the design of health components in future rural development projects.

l!EXICO
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
RURAL ELECTRIFICATION
Introduction
The Federal Electricity Commission (CFE) builds, operates and
maintains electricity generation and distribution facilities. It is a
decentralized state agency with a record of competence and effectiveness.
It has a large capacity for carrying out investment projects. In the
years 1970-74, it made investments in rural electrification at an annual
rate of more than US$40 million. PIDER will supply CFE with US$53.6 million
in the period 1973-76 for the electrification of villages in the 52 micro-
regions in operation. PIDER funds are projected to provide about 40% of all
rural electrification resources in the years 1973-76. Since PIDER is
limited to single investments in a. micro-region not exceeding US$5OO,O00,
larger investments, such as major additions to the national grids are not
made under PIDER auspices. There such large investments are needed to
implement the PIDER electrification projects, such outlays will be under-
taken through the ordinary or normal programs of CFE2.1
General Policies Affecting Rural Electrification
CFE considers part of rural electrification the conveyance of
electrical power to all population centers with less than 10,000 inhabitants
and whose main occupation is agriculture.  At the end of 1973, rural popula-
tion in Mexico was 29.3 million dispersed among 98,995 villages. Of these
totals, 15,510 rural towns and villages and their 17.1 million inhabitants
(58o of rural population) had access to electricity at that date. The goals
of CFE are to supply electricity to villages, to agricultural and grazing
activities and to rural industries.
The general criteria governing rural electrification are the
following:
a. Giving priority to projects that will stimulate use
of electric power for directly productive activities;
b. Supplying electricity to projects designed to bring
about a global improvement in the quality of rural
life; and
j For example, all available power in the Yucatan micro-region is being
used. Since PIDER investments call for the electrification of' 87 wells
and 16 villages, a 100 km 110 kv transmission line is necessary. That
line will be financed via CFE's ordinary budget.



ANNEX 3i
Page 2
c.  Attempting to make electricity available to the
largest number of people and enterprises at the lowest
possible cost. mnis implies the integration into a
national grid, if at all possible, as centrally gener-
ated power is very much more economical.
These policies are expected to contribute (even if indirectly) to
the promotion of local and state level participation in the planning,
financing and ex cution of the works and to the creation of additional jobs
in rural areas.P
Additional criteria for the selection of localities to electrify
are the following:
a. manifested interest of the local population;
b. availability of energy at a reasonable distance
without undue technical complications;
c.  availability of resources to the Government and
to the beneficiaries and the possibility of including
rural electrification in integrated rural development
projects.
The Project
The project would bring electricity to some 30,000 rural families
living in 350 villages located in some of the poorest areas of Mexico.  The
total to be invested is the equivalent of US$12.1 million, of which 10%
will be for the electrification of directly productive investments and the
rest for servicing the population.  The average cost of electrifying villages
in the PIDER project is US$70 per person. PIDER's goal is to bring
electricity to most of the villages in the range of 300-3,000 inhabitants
in the selected micro-regions.  CFE investment criteria under PIDER satisfy
the general guidelines governing rural electrification enumerated above and
are detailed as follows:
a. Eligible villages should have a population of at least
300 inhabitants and an access road.
b. Costs per person would not exceed US$150 in 1974 prices.
c.  Households would contribute 20<o of the investment costs.
d. In addition to the 20% contribution to the investment
costs, beneficiaries would also be charged the costs
of internal household wiring (equivalent to about US$25).
21  Financial participation by state goverlnments, 25 to 33,%, is conducted
through State Electrification Juntas.



AINNEX 3i
Page 3
CFE makes credit available for this purpose where
necessary.
e. Since the installation of meters adds US$50 to the
costs that have to be borne by the householders,
meters would, as a general rule, not be installed for
residential users in order to make electricity acces-
sible to even the poorest households. Meters would be
installed for agricultural, commercial, industrial and
public users.
f. Operation and maintenance costs would be fully covered
by user charges. Assurances that these criteria would
be satisfied for investments under the project were
obtained during negotiations.
CFE's capacity to carry on such a program has been amply demonstrated
during the first three years of the rural electrification plan and the first
year (1973) of the PIDER project.
Internal Organization of CFE
CFEts activities are conducted through a number of Gerencias
Generales (G.G.), such as rural electrification, operation and maintenance,
large scale construction, planning and control. The operating G.G.ts are
divided into a number of regional Divisions (generally 10). Bach Division
is composed of any number of Sub-gerencias (S. Gs), who actually carry out
the work.
The G.G. for rural electrification carries out the National Rural
Electrification Plan by designing and building necessary works and then
turning them over to the Operation and Maintenance G.G. This G.G. contains
four sub-gerencias that carry on specific functions such as generation,
transmission, distribution and supply to commercial users. A state-level
body, the Electrification Junta is involved in most steps during the planning
and implementation stages and supplies assistance to the local populations
on administrative matters (e.g., billing, collections, accounting, dealing
with CFE administration for repairs, etc.) and centralizes such activities
at the state level. A special department of CFE deals with State Electrifica-
tion Juntas.
Benefits
Foremost among Bank criteria for the appraisal of a rural
electrification project is whether it is part of a well-thought out rural
development program. All evidence suggests that, with PIDMR, this is
indeed the case. Since PIDER strongly emphasizes productive projects, the
precondition for successful electrification exists.



AlNN;EX 3i
Page 4
Since most benefits arising from electrification are related to
use of electricity, the expected rate of growTth of consumption is a useful
proxy for the benefits. Lndications from presently electrified villages
suggest that rapid growth can occur in consumption and in the nuamber of
customers../  CFEts insistence that electricity be brought preferentially
to commwnities where productive investments are taking place ensures that
consumption will grow.
In order that power consumption grows dynamically, a (minimum)
threshold income is necessary.  Since PIDR operates in relatively poor
rural areas it may be that such a threshold (however defined) is not
satisfied. This means that growth of consumption may be sluggish for a few
years until the productive projects of PIDER start increasing local incomes.
Thereafter, higher levels of consumption should be reached fast as house-
holds can afford more appliances and productive investments reach maturity.
j   The town of Ticul in Yucatan was electrified in 1967:  consumption grew
from 68 kwh per customer per month to 256 kwh in 1973. The number of
connections increased 150f over the same period.  Consumption by resi-
dential users can be exDected to go from 4o kwh/month to 130 kwh/month
in seven to eight years.

MEXICO
RURAL DEVELOP MET PROJECT
WATER SUPPLY AND SANITATION
Introduction
Adequate and safe water supply and sanitary waste disposal are
basic elements in environmental sanitation in both rural and urban areas
(see Annex 3h on Health). Fullest benefits from the water supply system
will result when water is provided directly to the house through a house
connection. Due to financial constraints, simpler systems based on public
hydrants may be necessary for smaller villages. To assure full benefits
from new water facilities, increased emphasis on health education would
accompany their installation.
Present SituatiOn of Rural Water Supply and Sanitation
In Mexico in 1973, 38% of the rural population had water supply
services through house connections or public hydrants and that adequate
excreta disposal facilities were minimal. In the micro-regions included
in the project the percentage with water supply services is much lower than
the country-wide average.
There are two agencies in Mexico which are carrying out the major
programs in providing water supply services for rural communities; the
Commission Constructore e Ingeniria Sanitaria de la Secretaria de Salubridad
y Asistencia (SSA) and La Direccion de Pequenas Obras de Agua Potable y
Alcantarillado de la Secretaria de Recursos Hidraulicos (SRH). SSA concentrates
on the smaller villages and SRH on the larger towns. Both agencies construct
systems under the PIDER project. While SRH operates and maintains the systems
it builds, the systems built by SSA are turned over to the towns which sign
agreements with the State Water Commissions (in the 19 States where they
exist) for operation and maintenance.  In both cases, supervision is sporadic
and as a result operation and maintenance of most rural water supply systems
are deficient. Corrective steps are being undertaken with the formation and
strengthening df state level Water Commissions specifica,lly charged with the
operation and maintenance of SSA constructed systems. Assistance through a
UNDP/WHO project is to be provided to SRH for strengthening its 0 and M
procedures.
The ProJect
The Project would provide for rural water supply *rstems to benefit
approximately 250,000 persons at an average cost of US$50 per person, a cost
in line with the Latin America averages ranging from US$40 to US$60 per



ANNEX 3.i
Page   2
beneficiary benefited and a 1974 average of US$48 in mexico.  Self-help
programs for the installation of sanitary privies will be encouraged under
the Project by the state Water Supply Juntas. The Project will provide
for the cost of cement and wire mesh reinforcing required for the construction
of privy slabs built by the villagers during the installation of the water
supply system, under the supervision of the local public health representatives.
The following criteria are being established for the rural water
supply component of the Project:
a.   Irrespective of the agency constructing the system (SRH or SSA)
uniform criteria,                        would be used for village
selection and project design.
b.  A local water committee would be organized with the assistance of
the agency constructing the system, to provide for the town's coopera-
tion before and during construction, as well as to earry out the
operation and maintenance of the system under the guidance of the
agency respaasible for  0 and M. Before system construction starts,
the town should enter into a contract with the constructing agency
(SSA or SRH) which would specify the contribution to be nade by the
town and the services and contributions to be provided by the
constructing agency both during the construction period and during
the all important operation and maintenance phase.
C.  The town would contribute no less than 15% of the construction cost
of the system, in cash, labor, land required for the system, and/or
local materials supplied to the site. A larger contribution would
give the town a higher priority for earlier construction. In special
cases where a 15% contribution requirement would work a hardship,
the situation would be reviewed by the Bank.
d. The actual house ccnmection (line and tap) would be installed at
the expense of the beneficiary. PIDER would finance the cost of
the system plus the cost of the line from the distribution system to
the beneficiary property line (see the attached chart which illustrates
the householder and the PIDER financed component).

Page   3
f.  Operation and maintnence costs would be covered by appropriate charges
set by the agency responsible for supervising 0 & M, in cooperation
with the beneficiaries. 0 & M charges would be collected by the
local committee which would retain that portion necessary for system
operation, passing on that portion required for system maintenance
to its account maintained by the agency responsible for supervision
of O & M of the system. The same agency would be responsible for
promotion of new systems and promotion of Project-supported self-help
projects for pit privies or septic tanks and related health matters
associated with the sanitary use of the newly supplied water.
Advantage will be taken of the mass approach in designing and con-
structing rural water supply systems. These are adapted to the financial .
and operational capabilities of the people being served, using multiple systems
where technically feasible. Construction of sewage systems would not be
financed under the Project. Promotion activities will be maintained on a
continuing basis from before construction through operation and maintenance.
In addition to the health education activities of all staff connected with the
water supply and exereta disposal activities, special health education programs
would be carried out highlighting the proper we and care of water supply and
exereta disposal facilities.

MEXICO
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
NUTRITION
PIDER and Nutrition
The PIDER Program does not contain a specific component dealing
with nutritional improvement in the micro-regions. PIDERts nutritional im-
pact is - by and large - indirect:(1) by reinforcing the Conasupo delivery
system operated through its rural store networks (fortified tortilla flour);
(2) by improving the capabilities of participating micro-region farmers to
broaden their cropping patterns beyond maize into beans and fruits, vege-
tables and small stock;-and (3) by introducing an integrated package of
supporting services including health care and home economics extension ser-
vices. The latter groups are expanding their operations in the PIDER micro
regions, directing their activities at improved home management, child care,
and especially better nutrition planning. The extension and health elements
of reinforced nutrition support are dealt with in Annexes 3 d and 3 h. The
problems of inadequate nutrition, especially in the Southern parts of Mexico,
and the thinking of Consupo in dealing with these problems in relation to the
PIDER Program are outlined below.
Background
The southeastern portion of the country (Chiapas, Yucatin, Oaxa-
ca) has the most serious nutritional deficiencies; the central region is
slightly better off; the northern and coastal regions are in the best situa-
tion. These regions also have a significant number of PIDER programmed micro-
regions, some 15 as of Dec 197h.
There is a calorie deficit in the southern rural areas of
8 - 10 percent relative to minimum requirements. The main nutritional defi-
ciency, however, is protein intake. The basic staple, maize, is low in protein
content and quality with triptophane being the limiting amino acid. In the
northern regions the greater consumption of frijoles relative to maize has
resulted in better quality protein intake with methionine being the limiting
amino acid. The consumption of animal protein (milk, eggs, and meat) is low
and accentuates the protein deficiency.
Deficiencies in calcium and iron were found among 80 percent of
the rural women and preschool-age children in these areas. Riboflavin and
niacin shortages also exist. Endemic pelagra exists in the Yucatgn peninsula,
perhaps due to the custom of washing the corn 7 times in the tortilla
making0 Vitamin A and ascorbic acid deficiencies also exist probably due to
the low consumption of fruits and vegetables. The nutritional deficiencies
are most serious among rural children.



ANNEX 3k
Page 2
Strategy Implications
There are several general implications of this overview of the
nutritional profile and dietary patterns of rural Mexicans for the efforts
of Conasupo within the PIDER program.
(1)   Nutritional deficiencies vary in severity and kind in different
regions and, therefore, nutrition supplements must be tailored to
the particular requirements of the specific region.
(2)   Critical target consumer groups from a nutritional standpoint are
the pregnant and lactating women and children under five; and pro-
ducts are being designed to meet these groups' needs.
(3)   Making new products available on the store shelf will not ensure that
it will reach the target groups; dietary patterns and food beliefs
must be dealt with in product design and via consumer education.
CONASUPO and Nutrition
In relation to these needs., CONASUPO has viewed malnutrition as
fundamentally a socioeconomic one: "We do not see the problem of malnutrition
as a problem in itself. For us, malnutrition is a reflection of serious
problems in our development. It is the consequence of the inequalities of the
Mexican society. It is the expression of distortions that have been intro-
duced in our form of evolution" SPerhaps stemming from this focus,
CONASUPO's nutrition efforts have been toward increasing the purchasing power
of marginal low income urban and rural families. As one CONASUPO official put
it, "we have to solve the quantity problem first before we can worry about
the quality".
Although CONASUPO has emphasized the quantity side, it has not
entirely neglected the quality dimension. It enriches the tortilla flour that
it produces and it is carrying out investigations on fortifying a hot sauce
and using Fish Protein Concentrate as a fortificant in a variety of products.
However, these efforts have been hindered by lack of technical expertise,
resource limitations, and interinstitutional relationships. Concerning the
latter, CONASUPO has been somewhat hesitant to move agressively forward in the
nutrition area because this is presumably the realm of the National Nutrition
Institute. The Institute undoubtedly has the requisite nutritional technical
expertise but CONASUPO has the operational delivery system skills. An
inter-institutional partnership seems most desirable.
The PIDER rural development program presents a meaningful oppor-
tunity for such a collaborative effort. One of the major problems that has
plagued efforts around the world to market successfully new high protein
foods such as INCAPARINA has been defective channels of distribution which
do not allow the product to reach the poor, rural, remote target groups
most in need of the product. CONASUPO's rural stores system provides a rela-
tively efficient delivery system with relatively broad coverage of the rural
sector. Probably the only other delivery system which is as successful in
penetrating and supplying regularly the remote Mexican ejidos is that of
2/ CONASUPO, "Hacia un Programa Nacional de Subsistencias" 1973, Mexico



ANNEX 3k
Page   3
Coca Cola. The channels are there, what is needed are the right products.
It is not clear by what criteria CONASUPO has developed the product line
it offers in the rural stores, but it is doubtful that a nutritionist
helped them select the product mix or explained to them the nutritional
significance of their product mix. A useful exercise would be to convert
the products offered to a cost per nutrient basis and to relate the pro-
ducts sold to the dietary patterns of the campesinos to determine possible
prodact-consumer misfits. The risk exists that the managerial personnel of
CONASUPO may be uninowingly imposing middle class food values on the cam-
pesinos. By offering products normally consumed by the middle class,
CONASUPO may be leading the campesino to "trade-up't from traditional to more
modern foods with a possible new loss in terma of a nutrients per peso basis.
This situation may not exist but the possibility is present. Because the
stores offer lower prices, purchasing power increases and food quantity in-
take can increase. But the food quantity and quality division is a false
dichotomy. Both variables should be considered in designing the rural stores
product line. The store's drawing power also puts it in a leverage position
to influence the campesino1s purchasing habits, and, therefore, it is in a
favorable position to shift purchases and consumption to new and more nutri-
tious foods.
CON1ASUPO's physical delivery system is supplemented by SAG home
economics staff who carry out a nutrition education function that deals with
the traditional food anid health beliefs. In addition, the health personnel
in the clinics may reinforce this educational effort as well as deal with
the sieknpss/disease problems. Ths , t1he village could simultaneously
aEttack the morbidity and malnutrition factorp. F     y  the clinics and the
CoRmunity Committees would facilitate the incorporation of a family planning
component to the services, thereby tying in with the government's new major
push in family planring. In effect, the coammnity would be receiving an
integrated he4th, nutritimn  aid fa4Ly f   pla;i  package. The potentials
for favorable yegism are great.
