MEXICO
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
THE NATIONAL INSTITUTE FOR INDIGENOUS AFFAIRS (INI)
Introduction
The National Institute for Indigenous Affairs (Instituto
Nacional Indigenista, INI) is a decentralized government agency. It was formed
in 19h8 to unify, coordinate and centralize the work of various government
agencies dealing with the protection and advancement of the indigenous popu-
lation of Mexico j  The indigenous population of Mexico in 1970 was estimated
at about 5 million people. Most of them live in rural areas, isolated back-
ward and poor. Since the rural poor are the target group of the PIDER program,
eventually most of the indigenous population will be among the beneficiaries
of PIDER. A large nuclei of indigenous people are present in Zacapoaxtla,
Puebla and Sur de Yucatan, two of the six micro regions appraised in June 197h.
Among other micro regions, the following also have large indigenous populations:
Helcechan, Campeche, Ometepec,.Guerreo Valles, San Luis Potosi and Valles de
Oaxaca.
Budgets and Growth
In the past, INI has lived under the ambivalent neglect of the
government: it was necessary to do something for the indigenous population but
no real cammitment had been made. This is evident in that the budget of I.N.I1
was kept, virtually constant at M$ 20 - 24 million (US$ 2 million) between
1962 and 1970.
Recently, INI's budget rose to M$ 31 million (US$ 2.5 million)
in 1971, M$h413 million (US$ 3.3 million) in 1972, M$ 87.1 million
(US$ 7.0 million) in 1973 and M$ 108.4 million (US$ 8.7 million) in 1974. The
field operations of INI expanded pari-passu as the number of "Coordinating
Centers" rose from 12 in 1970 to 52 in 197h. The basic operating unit of INI
is the Coordinating Center: it is located in an indigenous area, staffed by
5 - 6 professionals and a varying number of sub-professional technicians, the
bilingual promoters. The Center's role is to help the local inhabitants to
develop culturally and economically. Since the resources available to the
Centers are small (M$ 2 million) INI gets the cooperation of other Dependencias
in the building of works or in other activities. In fact, INI operates like
a mini-PIDER seeking the development of the areas under its responsibility
through an integrated program (which is often curtailed due to lack of re-
sources).
.3/ It should be noted that the term "indigenallis a cultural, not ethnic con-
cept. A person is "indigenous" if he speaks one or more of the pre-
hispanic local languages, lives in an area where many people have that
characteristic and has adopted neither the livestyle nor the values of
the modern (or modernizing) elements of Mexico.



ANNE 3 1
Page   2
PIDER and INI
Various activities of INI are supported by PIDER, such as
livestock improvement centers, beekeeping units, the raising of pigs, the
construction of schools and health clinics and materials used for the im-
provement of dwellings. Nurseries for fruit trees and centers for the pro-
duction of queen bees have also been financed. In Yucatan, where self-help
traditions are strong, community participation (labour and local materials)
in INI directed works are large. In late 1974 there are 7 micro regions in
which PIDER funds are channeUed through INI _/ Authorizations for 1974 in
these 7 micro regions totalled US$ 600,000. Since PIDER's goals are over-
whelmingly economic, virtually all PIDER investments through INI are directly
productive; socio-cultural activities are carried out through INIt's ordinary
or normal program.
g/ The micro regions are the following: Sur de Yucatan, Zacapoaxtla Pue,
Mixteca Alta, Chetuml-Carrillo Puerto, Amealco Qro., Ometepec Gro,
Tenango de Doria Hgo.

ANNEX 3m

MEXICO
Rural Development Project
CONAFRUT
(Commission for Fruit Development)
A. Background
1.        CONAFRUT (commission for fruit development) was established in
1961 as an advisory body to SAG and the Presidency. However, until 1970
fruit tree production did not receive high priority. Colleges and agricul-
tural schools did not have any special studies in fruit tree planting and,
production.
2.        On February 4, 1971 CONAFRUT was established as an autonomous
agency under SAG with a separate budget. CONAFRUT planned a 6-year invest-
ment of M$232 million (US$18.6m).  CONAFRUT since 1971 has established (1)
a policy for fruit tree planting, production and productivity and (2) six
programs for executing this policy.
B. CONAFRUT Policy
CONAFRUJT is currently concerned with research, direct extension
support to fruit tree development and support for marketing for both domestic
consumption, as well as exports. The overall policy has the following
objectives:
(a) To increase the rate of public and private investments
in the same proportion as the increase of domestic and
foreign demand;
(b) To stimulate exports by improving quality standards and
raising productivity levels;
(c) To promote fruit tree plantations in appropriate ecological
regions with comparative economic advantages;
(d) To supply the technical information and inputs needed to
develop production in each ecological region. Each ecological
region-should have a Regional Development Center to provide
the necessary support for developing new fruit tree varieties
to meet domestic and foreign demand;
(e) To decentralize fruit tree processing industries; and



ANNEX 3m
Page 2
(f) To study the possible repayment problems of Federal,
State and private investments in fruit production.
The cormission stated as a policy the "continuity" of these
proposals, since a major problem of agricultural plans in the past has been
to leave them incomplete or half-way finished with a large loss of public
resources. Though these policies have not been fully achieved in the past
three years considerable progress has been achieved.
C. Programs
Consistent with CONAFRUT policies, six programs were established
in order to implement the above major policies. The programs, each under
a department, are:
I.   Fruit Tree Development
The program goals are to provide technical education and
training to young professionals, technicians and peasants.
In this regard, the department has built libraries, labora-
tories, and schools, where training and technical assistance
is given to producers.  To complement such activity, a special
sub-program was launched to give priority to the most important
tropical fruit tree production areas of Mexico and to improve
production quality. Accordingly, 16 Regional Improvement
Centers were established to provide fruit tree saplings and
technical assistance. These centers cover most of the majority
of the ecological zones identified by COTNAFRUT.
The task of giving technical support to present fruit tree
plantations is large.  There are in Mexico 511,000 hectares of
plantations which represent only 3.3% of the total land in
agriculture. Not all of these plantations are market-oriented,
therefore the total area with fruit tree plantations is only the
equivalent of a rough estimate of all the present fruit trees.
There are 56,o00 hectares of fruit on irrigated land which represent
only 0.39% of the total irrigated surface in Mexico. The fruit
tree saplings produced by CONAFRUT in 1972 were enough to plant
4,500 ha, 8% of the total irrigated fruit tree plantations or 0.8%
of the total estimated area of the fruit tree plantations in the
country. This production of fruit tree saplings is not enough to
initiate new plantations and replace old ones. The rest of the
fruit tree saplings provided to plantations are supplied by private
nurseries. Only a few of these nurseries are under CONAFRUT
supervision.
The largest task of this program is to assess present inventories
of plantations and potentialities of each region. At present, there



ANNEX 3m
Page  3
is no complete national inventory of all the existing plantations
and there is only one study on fruit and vegetables marketing
done by CONASUPO.
II.   Fruit Processing Industries
The main goal of this program is to promote additional
fruit processing industries in rural areas, giving technical
assistance and quality specifications. Several research
programs are at present under way, in close collaboration with
local industries, to make good use of local production, by-products,
and improving the existing industrial process.
III.   Fruit Market Studies
The program's main concern is market surveys to inform
producers. The department in charge promotes producer organi-
zations for better market channels, storage, marketing costs
and margins. The program also carries out market forecasts to
supply information for fruit investments, both by CONAFRUT and
private producers. To date, this program has had only limited
success due to the size of the market, budget constraints and
lack of specialized professionals in the regions.
IV.   Communications
This department is in charge of CONAFRUT publications and
technical support to professionals working in the region centers
and producers. A considerable number of booklets with technical
information have been produced and made available free to pro-
ducers.
V.  Department of Economic Research
The department's main concern is the economic analysis of
fruit production, emphasizing production costs and productivity.
This department, though not considered as a program, has made a
complete survey of unit production costs, profits and investments
throughout Mexico.  The survey is based on case studies and suggests
the considerable economic advantages of fruit enterprises relative
to crops, though with large investments. (See attachments).
IV.   Community Development
This program was created exclusively for backward regions with
large populations, scarce natural resources and low production levels.
The program up to 1972 had not accomplished its purposes because of



ANNEX 3m
Page 4
several factors, among which are:
(a) Backward regions do not have the natural or capital
resources to start a high investment with an appropriate
technological level, and the commission did not have
enough resources to start the investments;
(b) The attitude developed by farmers after the failure of
previous fruit tree programs; and
(c) The lack of professionals to solve the technical
problems.
A complete coordinated plan to promote fruit tree plantations
was needed, including techinal assistance, physical input supplies,
and plantation investments. With the establishment of PIDER,
this program obtained resources to launch several integrated plans
and fruit tree plantations in the poorest rural regions of Mexico.
D. CONAFRUT and PIDER
The PIDER component of CONATRUT's program was placed under the
responsibility of the Community Development Department. This department
established a program for 11 micro-regions in 1973 and 3 additional in 1974.
Total expenditures were M$6,519,307 (US$0.5m) in 1973, virtually all the
authorized budget. As originally planned, the program in 1973 was to affect
about 266 ha. However, according to CONAFRUT the program had a direct benefit
on 813 ha (see attachment).
To May, 1974 the expenditures were about 80% of the authorized
budget. The development of the CONAFRUT program between these two years
is shown below. The 1973 investments' emphasis was in orchard preparation
and plantations (55% of the budget). The integrated program of fruit tree
development in a few micro-regions conmprising nurseries, orchard preparation
and plantations, technical assistance and pest control, was only 37%.

In 1974, 54% of the budget was planned for integrated programs
and only 38% for orchard preparation and plantations.
In 1974, PIDER allocations to CONAFRUT received a substantial
increase, virtually doubling the authorised expenditures and accoumting for
more than 19% of the total budget of CONAFRUT. However, the total employees
in charge of the PIDER program are only 9% of the total staff of CONAFRUT.
CONAFRUT and PIDER estimates are that the staff in charge of the PIDER
program should increase from 58 to 113 persons, while CONAFRUT permanent
employees may increase only 10%. If CONAFRUT trains and places new professionals
in the selected 14 micro-regions in 1974, the main technical constraints may
be overcome. Hence there would be an average of 8 professionals in each micro-
region if CONAFRUT is able to train and place the new professionals within the
14 micro-regions selected for joint development in 1974. The new permanent
professionals should be enough to meet the micro-region needs for planning fruit
development, giving technical assistance and building the necessary fruit
tree nurseries.
The Fruit Tree Regional Improvement Centers may provide a strong
technical support to the micro-region of the PIDER program. But since these
centers have been located mainly where there are large potentialities for
fruit production, not all the micro-regions selected by the PIDER program
will benefit with the Regional Improvement Center support. In 1973, only
the micro-regions in the states of Guanajuato, Nuevo Leon, Puebla, and Hidalgo
could rely on the technical support from a center located in a micro-region.
The new micro-regions selected by PIDER in 1974 could rely on the micro-
regicn centers of the states Chihuahua, Boja, California, Coahuila and Mexico.

p. 3
Livestock Production Areas
5.    Mexican livestock production occurs in two broad climate divisions:
(a)  60% in the arid and semi-arid north referred to as the northern
rangelands which are generally overstocked; and
(b)  40% in the wet and dry tronics including both Gulf and Pacifir
coastal areas, genera lJiv understocked.
Beef Production Areas - The Northern Rangelands production patterns are
oriented towiards the US fe-der cattle market (weaner calf 6-9 months of qge?
for which until late 1974 there was rising demand. Breeds such as Hereford,
Angus, Brehmen, Charolais and their crosses nredominate in the majority of
well managed herds, although the Criolla or native breed mainly oTwned by
smaller cattlement, ejido, communes and colonies have been able to gain
access to this market. Except when under drought stress, only male weaners
are exported since the export of females is prohibited. Some cattle in the
northern areas are fattened in feed lots and on irrigated pastures for
specialized higher priced markets. Weaning percentages range from 3%< under
low level management such as ejidos and small livestock owners in overstocked
areas to as high as 90% in well managed pastures under good management.  The
area is presently carrying about seven animal units/ha, with as high as 1 a.u./ba
in smallholder areas. This has led to a severe denletion of grassland resources
and erosion through overstocking. If managed efficiently under these conditions
with deferred grazing, the desirable carrying capacity is in the region of
20-30 ha/animal unit. Pasture deterioration has increased rapidly over the
past five years. Major steps will need to be taken in the near future to
alleviate the over-grazing and to preserve the grassland resources for
future generations.
Recent attempts to improve rainfed native pastures by introduction
of buffel grass and other adopted species have been successful, in Sonora
state. These techniques offer good opportunities on a well designed and
programmed basis to remedy the presently drastic situation.
6.   The tropics produce beef nrimarily for domestic consumption.  The nmost
important domestic production area is the Huasteca. Under the government
deforestation program presently underway, increasing areas of tropical and
coastal grazing areas are being cleared and planted with palatable improved
grasses, such as Panicum Maximum (Guinea Grass), nara, and pangola.  Although
a variety of native legumes nersist in these areas, tropical legumes. *s nart
of a oastoral mix with fertilizer applications, have not been significantly
developed. More research and demonstration are required before larger scale
imnact on optimum tropical pasture production can be achie-ed.
7.    Dairy Production Areas.  Mexico is a net importer of dairy products.
Approximately 2.5 million dair-y cows are concentrated in the irrigated distriets
of the central plateau and the northerr rangelands, with a nredominance of Holstein
and their crosses.  About 40% of Mexico City's millk requirement originates
within the city boundaries, generally derived from irrigated and greenfed
alfalfa under confined conditions.  In the tropical areas of Mexico, milk and
milk products are by-products of the beef cattle industry and orovide livestock
owners with a supplementary income.  For small scale nroducers, this is of great
importance as it tends to provide a daily cash flow whereas their beef off take
is seasonal.  Traditionallv, dairying has been concentrated principally in
the irrigated districts of the Central ?lateau of which the Bajio area has bsen



AN~NEX 3n
eo.4
the main producer for Mexico City.  This type of production has also spread
in recent years to the irrigated areas of the north and northwest, particularly
around Torreon. Milk from these areas is competing in price with the more
traditional areas and has captured about 20% of the Federal Capital market.
Good prospects exist for increased dairy production in most areas of the
country to service local urban and rural markets.  Early attention should
be given by government to developing dairy production to meet increasing
local demard based on low cost production techniques such as grazing
improved pasture under tropical and semi-arid irrigated and rainfed
conditions.
8.    Pig Production Areas.  Traditionally, commercial pig production has
been concentrated in the western edge of the Bajio. Tn recent years,
however, pig production has spread to the irrigated areas of the northwest
states as a result of increased availability of low cost feed grain.
Additionally, pig production on ejido farms is increasing in the Yucatan.
Recent efforts by Japan to import significant quantities of farm products
especially pork from Mexico has encouraged irnreased nig production.
However, government agencies are monitoring production increases carefully
in view of the danger of over supply, price reduction and unsure markets,
all of which historically are prevalent factors of the industry.
9.    Pig production methods are predominantly (90%) combined breeding and
fattening, with off takes of about 30% per annum. This can be improved con-
siderable mainly through better management practices, increased weaning rates
and nutrition. About 85% of pigs slaughtered in the Federal District are
produced in Guanajuato, Jalisco and Michoacan states, with northwestern
states production increasing yearly. The remainder of Mexico's pig production
(15%) is largely family and ejido type production of low management standards
and mainly consumed locally in these areas of oroduction.
10.   Sheen and Goat Production Areas.  Sheep production occurs mainly in the
northern rangeland areas, conducted by some large and small livestock owners
as complementary functions of beef cattle operations. Rambouillet types are
predominant with fleece yields of about 1-3 kgs per head ner annum. Carcasses
are consumed mostly locally and provide another source of regular cash flow
to smallholders. A few Corridales have been imported over the years, but
generally the sheep and wool industry has not developed sufficiently to provide
Mexico with self-sufficiency in sheep and wool products. Scope exists for
expansion on a limited and orderly basis, particularly with respect to:
(a) foreign exchange expenditure conservation on imports; and
(b) the browsing habits of sheep in semi-arid and arid areas.
Goat production is insignificant other than for sources of meat and milk for
localized domestic consumption.
l1.   Mexico presently imports about US$10 million, equivalent in wool and
wool products. Demand for mutton and goat meat is expected to double in
the next few years.

19.   Goat production has been traditionally a small-holder subsistence
animal resource young goat meat's ponularitv in rural con3uming areas as well
as milk for localized consumption is increasing and prices are rising as a
conseouence.
13.   Poulti7T Production Areas.  MrLost commercial production of broilers takes
nl.ace in the central plateau near Mex-co City, in the Federal District, as
near the western i.rrigated areas.  The princinal egg producing areas are
l.ocated in the northwest, north central and northeast irrigated zones and in
the central plateau, with increasing adoption in all production areas of large
scale modern technology. Daily egg production is estimated at about 18 million
of which about a third is consumed in the Federal District.  About 200,000
broilers are slaughtered daily of which the Federal. District absorbs 60o.

18.   Poultry, Bees and Rabbit Department is involved in oromoting production
and extension.  Due to market oversupply poultry production is not being
encouraged at present.
19.   Research (TNIP)  Two on-going programs are directly related to PIDFR
macro-regional development.  There are buffel seed production and pasture
research in Chihuaha state and the Sonora CIPES research unit.  Off the
two, the Sonora station (Cipes) is of most important, particularly in
the drier rangelands areas of the north.  It is realistic model -ith nroduction,
technologicl and sociological achievements and targets suitable for wide-
scale extension throughout the micro-regions of the northern rangelands.
20..  The Sonora Model.  This experiment station was created about four years
ago, Centro de Investigarciones Pecuarios del Estado de Sonora, A.C. (Cipes)
with the assistance of the Federal and State Governments through SAG and the
Regional Cattleman's Association of Sonora. The executive council is headed
by a senior technician of INIP (SAG) assisted by an economist from the State
Agricultural Department and senior officers of the Cattleman's Association.
The objectives were simply to devise a system of management suitable to
orovide the needs to stabilize efficiently and productively the livestock
and land resources of the semi-arid regions of Sonora and Durango.  To
achieve this an existing but progressive ejido was selected on which to
demonstrate this practical research program of which a lowl cost pasture
improvement and grazing control were key elements.
21.   Ten perennial grass species were introduced, viz:  Buffel (Centhrus
Ciliaris), Africano (Eragrostis lehmannia), Panizo Azul (Panicum antidotale),
Klein (panicum coloratum) and boer (Eragrostis curvola, var. contarts).  Of
these species, best results over a three year period have been obtained from
Buffel, Africano, and Panizo Azul in that order.  Buffel has been demonstrated
to be superior over all others from the Points of view of adaptation to the
climate conditions, production of good quality forage and fast economic re-
cuperation of the land. Accordingly this grass has been sown on both cleared
and uncleared land with good responses and economic results on both . However,
optimization in carrying capncity, unit rost of production of beef/ha and
management is better on cleared land.
22.   Co-related work has been carried out on low cost irrigated pasture as
an integrated function of day area grazing, directed at producing heavier
weaners and quicker maturity in retained replacement heifers for breeding.
By this integration it has been nossible to demonstrate:
(a)  bringing dry land with an existing carrying dapacibty of 35 ha/A.U.
up to 5 ha,A.U in a three year period.
(b)  on the 10O  ha of irrigated land sown with high quality pasture
grasses and legume, 1 ha/A.U.
(c)  a reduced herd for greater productivity and nrofitability by changing
the basic herd composition.
(d)  sirmDle low cost imnrovements such as fencing, corrals, etc. and
added income from grass seed sales.

MEXICO
RUrAI DEVELOPM1NT PROJECT
Organization, Planning and Management of the PIDER Program
1.        The Government has taken major steps in the past two years
to improve and coordinate institutions involved in meeting the objectives
of the PIDa3 program.  One of these objectives is to coordinate
development activities among participating agenciesl/ and at the same
time to focus them on targeted micro-regions. Another objective is
to encourage participation of target farmers and their communities
in development planning and project execution at the village level.
Recognizing that its existing organization was inadequate to administer
a multi-agency program like PIDER and that its administrative structure
and budgeting procedure were over-centralized, the Government revamped
its organization framework to plan and execute the PIDER Program.
2.        Principal responsibility for the PIDYR program is vested in
the Ministry of the Presidency (Presidencia). The Presidency has f-nal
authority on the investment budgets of all government agencies.  Public
agencies working in the rural sector had frequently been unable to
closely coordirate their activities. In 3)969, ho-wever, the Presidency
started coordinating the activities of these agencies on a pilot basis,
so that today their activities in the selected micro-regions are now
beginning to be synchronized within the PIDER framework. This task
was simplified with the earlier creation of special rural development
departments w thin many of the governmental investment agencies
(SOP, CFE, 4UNDO, SRH, CONASUPO and others).  In late 1974 some 2,700
agency professionals were engaged in carrying out the PIDER investment
program.
3.        The PIDER program is organized at three levels:
A ederal Level.  The highest policy-making body of PIDER
is a Coordinating Committee of senior officials from participating
agencies headed by the Presidency's Director of Public ITnvestment
(see Chart 2). The committee meets monthly to review policy, approve
micro-region programs and discuss inter-agency coordination prcblems
1/  Federal institutions participating at the federal and state levels
include: the Ministries of Agriculture (SAG), F`nance, Public Works
(SOP), Tater Resources (SRH), and Health and  Assistance (S3);
Department of Agrarian Affairs and Colonization (DAAC), Federal
Electric ComLmission (CE'E), Eational Corporation for Basic Marketing
(CONASUPO), Committee for Administration of the Federal Schcol
Construction Program, National Arid Zones Commission, National
Indigenous Institute, National Institute for Rural Community
Development and Low Cost Housing, the Ejido Bank, the Guarantee
Loan Fund and the Agricultural Bank.



ATRINC- 6
Page 2
that cannot be resolved either at the state level or by a Permanent
Working Group (FWG).  The PWG, made up of agency representatives at
the technical level, supports the committee by developing planning
methodology and concepts, screening micro-region investment plans
for consistency, and handling minor operational issues. the PWG in
turn is supported by a staff of 45 technicians located within the
offices of the Presidency.
B.  State Level.  A State Committee with the Governor as
chairman and made up of the state offices of federal agencies
coordinates program execution of participating agencies in the micro-
regions. The Executive Secretary (Vocal Ejecutivo) of the State
Committee or a senior state civil servant under him also serves as
the PIDER director for the state. He is assisted by a Technical
Secretariat (Grupo de Apoyo Tecnlco) of two to five professionals
seconded from PIDER, one of whom is appointed chief.  The Technical
Secretariat may live within the micro-region or visit it as often as
necessary--i.e., frequently each day-- in line with their primarily
responsibility to coordinate PIDER activities in a specific micro-
region.
C. Village/Ejido Level. Village and ejido participation
in both planning and program execution is being improved through a
variety of schemes adapted to the particular, political, social and
administrative characteristics of the micro-region. DAAC personnel
are charged with assisting communities to organize themselves in
order to ensure a fuller involvement in the PIDER program prior to
implementation of the investments program. The municipal chief and
ejido and village authorities also play an important role in local
participation. In a few micro-regions, for example in Sinaloa,
municipal chiefs, at least one day a week are required to monitor
local project execution and report problems to the state director
of the Technical Secretariat.
4.        This three-tiered organization provides the overall structure
under which the project is carried out. Selection of' micro-regions
and approval of investment plans remain centralized, although plan
preparation and coordination among the diffent agencies will be
increasingly delegated to the state level. The individual project work
would always be channeled through the public agency best-suited to
carry it out, as determined by the central coordinating committee
and agreed to by the state committee. Supervision and monitoring
would be ongoing activities of federal level FIDER staff. At the
state and micro-region level PIDER staff are continuously monitoring
project progress.



ANN-3X 5
Page 3
Th.ie project organ7zational framework breaks new grounrIs
in Mdexilco whi. -h has traditionally been characterized by strong
centralization.  Although many important functions have already
been delegated under PDEER, the Government intends to go further
and decentralize public agencies which do not depend on central
technical and organizational back-up.  Decentralization within
public agencies (except for SRH, SOP, DAAC, CFE and COA,iSUJP) has
been compieLed in three states.  Early results are encouraging.
6.        Ivicro-Region Plannig Proced.ures.  PIDER has established
a set of procedures for drawi-ng up mLcro-regioTI c'evelopment plans.
Initially, much of the wTork  as -andertaken 'oy federal level _     DT2.
svaff in conjunction with state level technicians o- federal agencies.
''ow that more micro-regions are being planned at the state level,
federal level PIDTE' staff mainly are responsible for review, revision
and final approval of' plans.  These procedures, suriarized schemati-
vally in Chart 3, show the points of partlcipation of ccmmunities
for determining their lriority needs and presenting them to state
and federal level agencies and PIDD11 represent"ati-ies. During the
initial half-day meetings with micro-region planners, most communities
are not yet sensitized to the possibilities for this kind of involvement.
Holwever, as development proceeds, member of the DAAC SORE teams are
'playing an important role in making them aware of development oppor-
tunit-ies and their obligations under the PIDME program.  When it revamps
and reprograms micro-region plans annually,  PID=i also revises and
incorporates the more detailed development preferences expressed by the
ommuni ties.
7.        Rural Development Departments in Federal Agencies.  Most of
the influential federal mnistries and agencies such as the SOTP, P5 SM,
CONASU-F0, CFE and the Bank of Mexico have substantiLally increased their
physical and organizational capability to deal with the rural pocr.
The l1nistry of FinaLce supplements the agencies' traditional program
budgets and directs them towiards new rural target groups.  Several
countrywide programs under these agernles have obtained T7TRD or 7DB
funding Lor -rural roads, irrigation and credit--some increasingly
benefiting low-income .armers.  The traditionally weaker agencies such
as ;-he SAG, SSA and SRA and the official rural credit banks are being
supported through a tehcnical, organ-izatlional and adminjstrative base.
A  better awareness of the needs of new clientele  s also being fostered.
The function of the PIDTY?. program is to use and build up this base,
decentralize the operational decision-making to the state level and
integrate agency activities among state and local levels. The micro-
region planning exercises are being used by the Presidency for this
objective, with the longer term goal  of eventually integrating and
coordinating all rural nublic investments.



ANTzx 6
Page 4
8.        Coordination at the  Field Level.  Coordination at the
municipal and village levels is necessarily flexible. CONASUFO
organizes its local committees to handle store management, handi-
crafts and small industries. Very often DAAC sets out to concentrate
on ejidos and later broadens its activities to embrace private
smallholders. In another instance, the lavor intensive feeder road
program of the Ministry of Public WJorks may act as catalyst--with
formal local village committees simultaneously functioning as
contractors for construction labor and as counterparts for road
maintenance.  The encouragement these broadening activities have
given to other areas of village organization have been impressive.
SRH operates programs for small irrigation works on a similar
oraanization base. Prior to project installation a local consumers'
association is formed.  SRH and DAAC then draw up an agreement wfith
the association, specifying: (a) works and services the SRH will
provide;  (b) operation and maintenance conditions; (c) amount of
water to be used; and (d) charges covering operation and maintenance
costs and capital payment.

MEXICO
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
EVALUATION AND MONITORING
Introduction
Due to the many agencies involved in the PIDER program, Presidencia
is devising a system to receive a continuous flow of information on diverse
actions taken at both the agency and at the ejido or community level. The
type of information needed by PIDER to adjust micro-region programs requires
both the monitoring of agency interventions and a more substantive evaluation
of effectiveness,
A, Monitoring
At present, the newness of the rural development program and the
involvement of a great number of agencies have led the PIDER group to focus
on developing an adequate monitoring system. Monitoring will continue to
be performed within Presidencia itself, rather than by the new Research Center
for Rural Development (CIDER), described below*  Currently, monitoring is
limited to monthly reports on the physical progress of construction and the
disbursement of appropriated funds. There is no systematic procedure for
comparing the latter information with the investment program to determine the
causes of discrepancies between actual and planned investments. There is
also no attempt to compare actual versus planned unit costs, Comparative
analysis along these lines is required, and Presidencia has expressed a
willingness to expand the monitoring function accordingly. Steps are pre-
sently being taken to experiment in one micro-region with a new format for
monthly reports, in the likelihood of its adoption throughout the PIDER
program.
B. Ongoing Evaluation
The substantive reprogramming of micro-region investments and actions
requires information on the results of agency interventions in relation to
intermediate objectives. An adequate monitoring system, which includes an
identification of the reasons for differences between actual and planned
investments or actions, will produce some information on results, unanticipated
problems and perhaps misconceptions in the initial diagnosis of the micro-
region. But a monitoring system is inevitably incomplete in providing data on
these aspects because of a focus on the physical progress of planned works.
The focus of an ongoing evaluation is on effectiveness rather than physical
progress, and is much more difficult to design and implement.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
Ongoing evaluation will be one of the responsibilities given to
CIDER, established in December 1974. This Center is to be budgetarily
independent from the PIDER program. However, CIDER will be conducting its
action research in close cooperation with agency personnel in the micro-
region. If CIDER is conceived as personally or institutionally threatening
to agencies in the field, ongoing evaluation cannot be successfully carried
out and would consequently have to be developed within an alternative
organizational framework.
In the methodologies being developed within CIDER or individual micro-
regions, the focus is being put on acquiring and digesting the information
needed for decision-making. Although some of the necessary information must be
identified by individuals with an intimate knowledge of the programs, organiza-
tions and areas included in various micro-regions, many information needs are
universal. Illustrative areas for "action research" by CIDER are provided
below.
10 Comparison of Planned and Implemented Actions
In any given year of a micro-region plan, particular investments and
activities are programmed for specified communities or areas, If physical tar-
gets are not met or some action is added, there should be an analysis of the
causes of the discrepancies. Reasons for the discrepancies encountered-agency
inefficiency, community unresponsiveness-- may fall into the categories
described below. Or the initial diagnosis of the problem may have been incorrect,
e.g. the area is more appropriate for pasture than for frait trees. No matter
what the cause of the difference, corrective action can then be taken in the
following year.
2. Inter-Agency Coordination and Intra-Agency Efficiency
Many of the investments and actions carried out in micro-region com-
munities are complementary. SRA efforts at organization and settling land
tenure problems, for example, should be carried out in conjunction with action
by credit institutions. SAG extensionists working in the forage silo program
should be in close contact with technicians dealing with other aspects of crop
and livestock production. There should be records to indicate the extent to
which agencies with complementary responsibilities are working in the same com-
munities, the frequency of organized meetings, etc. Some idea of the effective-
ness of their interactions can be derived through interviews with both techni-
cians and campesinos.
Some very good work has already been done in Mexico to assess the
efficiency with which agencies carry out their tasks and the degree to which per-
sonnel understand the nature of their responsibilities. For agencies working
directly with faruer organizations ( SRA, SAG, credit institutions, etc.),
an excellent methodology to assess agency effectiveness has been developed and
tested by the Banco Agropecuario de Occidente. Similar work has been under-



ANNEX 7
Page 3
taken in evaluations of the Plan Puebla experience, and certainly in other
programs and agencies as we1.
3. The Effectiveness of Campesino Organizations
Campesino organizations vary considerably in their purposes and
comprehensiveness. The evaluation of their effectiveness, therefore, requires
a typology of farmer organizations. The typology would range from loosely
organized groups for credit only to enterprises involving the joint ownership
of land and assets. BANAGRO has also created such a typology.
There should be a strong correlation between the comprehensiveness
of camResino organization and the communities' stage of development, as
measured by the indicators identified previously. Unless there is a tradi-
tion of group action, eiidatarios used to operating individually in a sub-
sistence or below subsistence economy are unlikely to form more than credit
groups. The first few years of PIDER action can only be expected to raise on-
farm production to cover family consumption needs and to produce a small sur-
plus for financing limited new technology and the costs of maintaining and
operating PIDER investments. Later, as the marketable surplus grows and cash
income increases, raising effective demand, there is a motivation for more
comprehensive types of organization. The motivation for more sophisticated
forms of organization will be conditioned by the early simpler group experience.
Indicators of group efficiency and the degree to which decision-
making is shared include: (1) frequency of assemblies, (2) attendance at
meetings, (3) key components in group charters or regulations, (4) credit
repayment records, (5) reasons for non-payment, (6) sanctions for non-payment
and many other, depending upon the principal purposes of the organization.
In organizations involTing joint ownership of assets, there should also be an
analysis of the internal distribution of profits and work, as well as the
distribution criteria. Interviews with group members are particularly impor-
tant to determine how individuals perceive both the benefits from organization
and their role in decision-making.
4. Production. Cost and Marketing Data
Micro-region targets, and the ex-ante evaluation, call for immediate,
significant increases in the yields per hectare of annual crops, primarily
through the application of fertilizer and changes in farming techniques. All
livestock programs similarly anticipate high levels of productivity, measured
in terms of effective calving rates and animal units per hectare. Records
should be kept regularly of yields, calving rates and other basic indicators
of productivityO In the case of fruit trees, there are also two or three
measures of progress, such as annual tree growth and records of diseases.
Production costs, transport costs and marketing information, in-
cluding product prices, selling points and final market outlets are important
for ongoing evaluation0 Product prices, input costs and physical output data
are also used in the financial analysis.



ANNEX 7
Pa-ge 4
5. Labor Intensity of Feeder Roads and Water Conservation
The intensity of labor in feeder road and water conservation works
varies from 60% to 80% of cost, excluding overheads. During the evaluation
of the project, various economic tests of varying the intensity of capital
to labor ratios in these projects would be undertaken0
6. Financial Analysis
An annual review of the financial situation of participants in
directly productive activities is essential to prevent excessive indebtedness
in relation to present and expected net cash inflow. The repayment of PIDER
productive investments is supposed to be determined on the basis of a cash
flow analysis, so that amortization periods can be adjusted to the ejido's
repayment capacity. Yet official banks and other entities will also be ex-
tending both short and long term credit. The ejido and individual borrowers
could easily find themselves suddenly in an untenable financial position with-
out comprehensive monitoring of cash flow.
When long term investments are undertaken, there is also a need for
cash flow protections. If, for example, effective calving rates are not
reaching ex-ante targets, it is critical to estimate the effects upon future
income when interest and/or amortization payments must be made. An early
response, in the form of action to increase potential income or to reschedule
repayments, could mean the difference between eventual success or failure
of the farm enterprise. This type of analysis will also modify initial targets
and objectives, and lead to improved ex-ante evaluations.
7.  Commuity Attitudes and Responsiveness
The follow-up campesino organizations, described above, will indicate
a great deal about attitudes and responsiveness0 Other indicators are: (1) the
proportion of e.iidatarios contributing free labor to community projects,
(2) the regularity of the labor contribution, (3) visual evidence of the care
taken for PIDER investments, (4) the sense of either group or individual owner-
ship of PIDER investments, etc. (Additions by Sergio).
Data Collection. The quantitative data required for ongoing evalua-
tion should be collected and recorded either by agency technical staff or
ejidatarios themselves. Evaluation personnel should participate to the ex-
tent that they are assured the data are recorded accurately and completely.
Much of the data, particularly for (3), (4) and (5) above, can be collected
by ejidatarios, who would probably be more thorough and reliable than tech-
nicians. The work load of technicians is such that they are often pressed for
time in field visits, and other responsibilities are considered more imme-
diate than filling out fairly detailed forms. The e.jidatario, on the other
hand, has much easier access to the information. Furthermore, a community role
in data collection transfers additional responsibility to the ejido. There
could be significant secondary benefits through learning to use skills
acquired through education for improved farm management.
Although ever poor, subsistence farmers are acutely aware of costs,
yields, etc. without keeping written records, a permanent and systematic
recording of farm and financial data should have very positive results.



ANNEX 7
Page o
C. Ex-nost Evaluation
E:c-post evaluation is not as important for PIDEP as action research,
but it is essential for the design of future rural development programs in
Mexico and elsewhere.  Ex-post evaluations will also be conducted through
CIDER, although much of this work will be contracted out to universities and
other research institutions.  CIDER's planned autonomy is particularly
important for this level of evaluation, which must be carried out with
complete objectivity. CIDER is expected to initiate this activitv bv con-
tracting for the evaluation of earlier, Mexican rural development projects
and programs. The study of experiences in other programs should provide
some immediate benefits for the design or reprogramming of PIDER micro-region
activities. PIDER. has already benefitted, for example, from the ex-post
analyses of the Plan Pueblo program.
Despite the several year waiting period before the PIDER program
is sufficiently mature to warrant ex-post evaluation, CIDPR must develop a
tentative methodology for these studies in 1975.  A methodology is needed
to identify and collect the base-line data. Even some of the more obvious
base-line data are presently not available at the community or eJido level.
There is inadequate or no information collected systematically cn family
income, sources of income, consumption, land distribution, employment and
migration.  Yet these data are fundamental for assessing the long-term impact
of PIDER actions in micro-regions.
Organization of CIDmER
CIDER will be created with budgetary resources provided directly
from the Secretaria de IIacienda v Credito Publicos.  The projected 10-member
Board ol Directors includes the major agencies involved in the PIDER program
(SAG, SOP, SRH and SRA ), PIDER, DiDECO, CONASUPO, the Fjido Bank and two
representatives from the I-linistry of Finance.  The Coordinating Comnission
for the Agricultural Sector (COCOSA), which has provided the most substantial
ideas for an approach to evaluation witiin CIDER, is not currently linked to
the Center through the Board, but is through an Advisory Panel.
Training
As previously indicated, CIDER will be structured to accommodate
both evaluation and training functions. The Center's training activities are
intended to complement the instruction received 'bv rural development
nrofessionals in universities and in their respective agencies. >raining
material and case studies will be generated through the research carried
out or contracted by CIDER.  The current emphasis withJin Preside-ncia is on
the need for cross-fertilization bet-ween research and training througn the
movement of staff from one function to another.  Present clans for CIDER,
therefore, anticipate a loose structure under a Director and Deputy Diretor,
who would be free to alter the internal organization as recuired for the
Center's work.



ANNEX 7
Page 6
Initially, the internal organization of CIDER is expected to involve
the division of a staff of about 30 into a number of project groups, corres-
ponding to particular activities. The largest group would contain action
research teams, distributed in three or four micro-regions for ongoing
evaluation.  The teams would conduct local conferences and seminars as part
of their research and training activities. T'he size of each team would be
four, although persons would be added temporarily for certain phases of work.
ZYfo other groups would be established for background research and
sub-contracted research. The background research group jould scan, evaluate
and comnmunicate selectively the findings of research projects and the reports
of practitioners in rural development both in Mexico and abroad.  The studies
reviewed would not be limited to evaluations of rural Drojects, but would
include community and anthropological studies contributing to a better under-
standing of PIDER's client groups. This group would also conduct its own
research work to produce a typology of communities, indicators of rural devel-
opment, etc. and would become involved in ex-post evaluation.  The background
research group would work closely with the group for sub-contracted research,
which would cornmission similar studies with outside institutions.
- fourth group, which would probably be one person rather than a
team, would coordinate and develop training activities associated with CIDER.
The training group would provide liaison between the Center and agencies
dith their own training programs, particularly SRA.
The final group would have responsibility for the dissemination
of research. There would be a rotation of staff from the field to perform
this function, with the possible exception of a nermanent publicist.
In documents prepared for Presidencia, great importance is being
given to the careful selection of CIDEPR staff.  There is acceptance of the
need for a multi-disciplinary staff in the fields most relevant for analyzing
the development process at the community level. S'election of personnel will
also be based upon both academic background and field experience. CIDER> is
exoected to borrow a portion of its staff from participating PIDETR agencies.
the latter may be especially desirable for ongoing evaluation,, which depends
upon the responsiveness and involvement of action agencies.

MEXICO
RURAL DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
ASSUMPTIONS ON ECONOMIC AND FINANCIAL ANALYSES
Introduction
1.        In assessing a countrywide program involving some 14 agencies,
various distinct investment categories and considerable beneficiaries, a
number of assumptions were made in analyzing the economic and financial
returns. First, the micro-regions were categorized into six ecological
zones considered typical for the major zones to be covered by the overall
program. Micro-regions were appraised in detail from each of these
"fecological" zones. Second, "representative" ejido or village development
plans were developed for each of these "typical" micro-regions. These
budgets included credit, directly productive infrastructure, productive
support infrastructure, and social investment and supporting applied
research and extension services. Financial rates of return were calculated
only on the directly productive activities including credit. Economic rates
of return measured returns to directly productive and credit investment, as
well as to supporting productive investment, such as feeder roads, electri-
fication, extension, research and markets necessary to achieve the incremental
benefits. Investment in social infrastructure such as water, health and
education facilities were not charged as costs in the economic analyses.
Financial AnaL2ses
20        Family Sabsistence Allowances. Current familiest expenditures are
taken as the present level of the imputed value of on-farm consumed produce
plus consumer goods purchased. On-farm consumption is high and most of the in-
cremental income may be expended in non-durable goods. There is no data on
consumption patterns and any projection is arbitrary. 2/
3.        Income Levels, Savings and Consumption. The goal of the PIDER pro-
ject is that participating beneficiaries should receive an income similar to the
minimum legal wage. The minimum legal daily wage multiplied by 270 working
days per year equals the annual income. The inccme level is assumed to include
all consumed goods plus the expected net cash balance left after expenses for
j A case study of the indigenous community of San Pedro Lagunillas, Nayarit
micro-region, shows that 63.1% of the total family income goes for food
and clothing. Health accounts for 13% and house maintenance for 5%. The
average family income level is Mex$9,129 (US$730/year). This average income
comprises 4607% of imputed value of on-farm consumption and 53.3% of
cash-income from off-farm employment.



ANNEX 8
Page 2
maintenance and operation of all directly productive and support invest-
ments are paid. All additional income above the target income based on
the minimum legal wage is assumed as savings, available to repay the PIDER
investment amortization and interests of development loans of the following
year. However, if the target income is not achieved, all incremental
income is considered as family subsistence allowances. Family allowances
are considered in the cash outflow as family expenditures.
4.        The beneficiaries of the PIDER project are mainly subsistence
farmers with very small marketable surpluses, if any, available to repay
long-term productive investments in the short run. Therefore, it is essen-
tial that the loan repayment include a grace period until production reaches
a minimum marketable surplus. In addition, the repayment period must be
long enough to permit small payments so that the ejido will be left with
sufficient cash to provide the participating farmers with a minimum income
for family expenditures.
5.       The PIDER project does not envisage interest charges on long-term
loans made for basic infrastructure investment from PIDER funds. The
justification for not charging interest is that the selected "backward"
micro-regions for the most part are at a comparative economic disadvantage
with the rest of the country. However, interest rates are charged in all
"development loans", which comprise mainly cattle, hogs, beekeeping and
tree-fruit production. These types of investments are financed by the
Banks. Operating expenses for development activities as well as annual
crops are to be financed either by official or private Banks.
Financial Rates of Return
6.        Financial rates of return were measured for all of the investment,
including credit in directly productive activities being carried out at the
ejido or village levels. These returns were measured from "representative"
ejido or village-level development budgets. Returns were aggregated, e.g.
irrigation, livestock, fruit production and rainfed grain production, into
"packages" for purposes of the rate of return calculations. Separate or
"partial" budget analyses for specific development activities such as dairy
production or rainfed corn production were not undertaken. Such analyses
were done under Loan 910-ME for beef cattle, irrigated dairying, fruit
production and some small-scale agro-industries.
7.        Physical contingencies of 10% of investment costs were included
in the cost streams as these would represent, if required, a utilization of
real resources by participating beneficiaries.
8.        Sensitivity analyses were undertaken primarily on the financial
flows, though critical technical assumptions, such as yield increases on
rainfed corn production 1 were also varied. The resulting financial rates
of return for the six representative micro-regions are given in the Table
following.
%/ Corn yields increased 20% raised the rate of return to 22%; a 20%
decrease in expected yields dropped return to 19%.

Economic Analysis
9.         The economic and financial analysis takes as the direct benefits of
the project only the incremental output, net of farming expenses.  This
incremental output is due to higher yields of the ongoing agriculture and new
agricultural activities implemented through the directly productive invest-
ments. However, there are a number of direct benefits implicit in the increm-
ental output, aside from higher levels of employment and income. The new
agricultural activities are feasible only through higher skill levels achieved
by farmers which are direct benefits since they come from on-farm training
provided through the PIDER project. Higher skill levels may lead to better
farm management and more efficient use of available resources, which in turn
has direct benefits through soil and water conservation badly needed in these
backward micro-regions. These direct benefits from training and soil and
water conservation can be assessed only by comparing output levels with and
without PIDER project. Output levels have not been constant, rather they
have been declining over the past decade due to a deterioration of natural
resources and the low levels of physical and technological inputs. There
are no available data to quantify the increasingly lower levels of yields and
production, which are apparent today from the depleted resources of once
productive croplands, grazing lands and forests. Hence without the PIDER
project both output and standard of living could very well deteriorate in the
near future; in any case, they can in no way be assumed as constants.
10.-       The second package of direct benefits are measured through improve-
ments in the standard of living in the ejidos and indigenous communities. It
is apparent that most new production will be absorbed by on-farm consumption pro-
viding higher standard of living in the short-run and better family diets in
the long-run. New health and education facilities will contribute to improved
standards of living, although these effects are not taken into account in the
incremental income and employment levels. The present poor health levels are
characterized by respiratory diseases and stomach disorders due to unbalanced
diets, polluted drinking water sources, and contaminated foods. Improved
education facilities may lay the basis for a better trained population some of
which eventually may have to outmigrate.
11.        The indirect benefits are difficult to assess since they refer more
to derived demand of services and physical inputs required by the new developed
productive units and the incremental aggregate output. The indirect benefits
rely more on the efficiency attained by development agencies in reaching the
entire target population and production areas and achieving their short-term
objectives and long-term goals.
12.        In undertaking the economic analyses, the basic module used to
generate the cash flows was the ejido or village-level development budget.
The net cash flow to the beneficiary was adjusted under the following assumptions:



ANNEX 8
Page 7
a. The costs of productive support (feeder roads, extension, research
and markets) needed to achieve the estimated increment in production
were added to the cash flow. These costs are borne by the Govern-
ment and not the beneficiary.
b. The cost of unskilled labor used in the construction of indirectly
productive and productive support assets was redaced from the
rate actually paid by Government to the opportunity wage. This op-
portunity wage was estimated in each of the "representative" micro-
regions as the casual labor daily wage rate in the non-crop season,
e.g. what a landless laborer could earn doing odd jobs on a daily
basis in that area. This rate varied from 70% of the government mi-
nimum wage in Sinoloa to 40% of this wage in the densely-populated,
very poor micro-region of Zacapoaxtla in the state of Puebla. The pre-
vailing local labor wage or opportunity wage in each micro-region in
the non-crop season was taken as an approximation to shadow wages and
used to calculate both the value of community contributions in terms
of labor (as already was said above) and to adjust the minimum legal
wage used in financial calculations for the purpose of the economic
rate of return analysis. The relationship between the legal minimum
wage and the opportunity cost of unskilled labor on a daily basis
are shown below:

The opportunity wage levels of off-farm employment in raral areas in
these micro-regions were assumed relatively constant through the
entire development period. The wage levels are not expected to increase
since unemployment and disguised unemployment are high in these micro-
regions, and income is expected to increase for on-farm employment.
Co Physical input pricesO The prices of all variable inputs were assumed
equal to the local market prices or those domestic prices controlled
by government agencies. The local market prices in each micro-region
were taken,with special attention to draft animals, seeds and trans-
portation. Controlled domestic prices were taken especially for fun-
gicides, pesticides and fertilizers. Cattle production costs were taken
on the basis of information provided by SAG for each year.



ANNEX 8
Page 5
d. Output prices. The incremental output was assumed not
to affect the overall price relationships. The PIDER
project though affecting a large number of micro-regions
will benefit only a fraction of the total population of each
micro-region; moreover, the target population will consume
a large proportion of the incremental output in the short-
and medium-term of development.
e.  The foreign exchange costs and earnings of the Project were
not adjusted.
f. The results of these adjustments are reflected in the economic
rates of return listed below:

13.      Sensitivity analysis was carried out indicating that economic returns
on productive investment in a representative ejido in, for example, Sinaloa
state would fall from 16% to 12% with a 20% increase in investment costs and
to 13% with a 20% increase in operating costs. A 20% shortfall in incremental
maize yields would drop this economic rate of return to 14% while a 20%
increase in yields would raise the rate to 17%.
14.      Employment levels.   Projected employment levels reflect the temporary
labor requirements in the four year investment period of the PIDER project, as
well as those of the on-going agricultural activities. Following the four year
program, further increases in employment levels are generated either from the
higher labor requirements of improved farming systems or from the new
activities implemented through the directly productive investments.
15.      The overall employment generated by PIDER, temporal and permanent,
will solve in different degrees the present levels of unemployment, but in few,
if any, cases exist where PIDER investments would lead to full employment.
Thus, outmigration is likely to continue, especially in over populated micro-
regions.



ANNEX 8
Page 6
16.        The PIDER project states that community labor contributions may
account for up to 20% of the total investment cost. However, if community
contributions in terms of labor are valued at the on-going local wages
(roughly 60 to 80% of the minimum legal wage) in some cases almost all the
labor requirements would be undertaken by the communities. This situation
would go against one of the short-term objectives of the PIDER project, which
is to stimulate participating farmers through temporal employment generation
and income. Thus, the assumption used instead is that communities' contri-
butions account up or it is equal to 50% of the man-day requirements of the
investment program at the on-going local wages. The remaining labor require-
ments are assumed to be paid at the minimum legal wage by PIDER.
17.        In addition to these analyses, some quantitative sector analyses
(feeder roads and rural stores) were undertaken to ascertain benefits.
Feeder Roads
18.        Except in rare cases there is no evidence that feeder road invest-
ments in the micro-region can generate much local economic development of
their own. While accessibility is a serious constraint in every micro-region,
road network improvement and expansion efforts obviously cannot remove other
constraints such as lack of water, demographic pressure, poor agricultural
techniques and so forth. The case of roads opening up areas with a good
agricultural potential that had not been previously exploited because of poor
accessibility is the exception.
19.        Flows are, in general, too low to justify feeder roads economically
in terms of transport cost savings on normal and diverted traffic. Some
tentative evaluations were made in the Nuevo Leon and Zacapoaxtla micro-
regions by adopting a cost of Mex$2.13 (US$0.17) per ton km vs. a mule cost of
Mex$30 (US$2.40) per day (the trucking cost was determined by analyzing SOP's
truck expenditures in the Zacapoaxtla micro-region; the mule cost was set
equal to the rental fee; a mule carries about 130 kg). Agricultural surpluses
were determined by estimating productivity and consumption per family. Traffic
flows induced by such surpluses were then calculated, with the users' savings
associated with a shift from animal to truck transport after the road is opened.
Other benefits resulting from mobility increases due to the introduction of
bus and taxi services and to shorter road distances were not taken into account,
being generally of little significance. It was then found that most feeder
roads could not break even and that noe had an economic rate of return higher
than 10%.
20.        The results of the above economic analysis does not, however, mean
that the roads generally are unjustified investments. These roads have to be
looked upon in a wider context. The most important contribution that road



ANNEX 8
Page 7
investments in the micro-regions are making to the development of their
area of influence is the infrastructure support they provide to the
PIDER project. Such a contribution cannot be evaluated by looking upon
the roads in isolation from the rest of the PIDER package. Inversely,
this package would not be feasible technically and economically without
the feeder roads (in particular, accessibility improvements were implicitly
taken into account in projecting agricultural production).
21-        The purpose of the proposed procedures is to select feeder road
projects and the related directly productive investments they support
whenever their joint economic rate of return is above 10%. The major directly
productive PIDER components in every micro-region are set on some uniform
scale (in this report, the scale is 100 ha for crops and 100 initial A.U.
for livestock investments). They are then analyzed independently and the
present worth of their respective net benefit stream (with 100 ha or 100
initial A.U.) evaluated at10%rate of interest, using the village and ejido
models. Dividing present worths by the net present cost per km of feeder
road at 10%rate of interest (with a typical construction cost per kn;
maintenance costs are set at US$240 per km on 20 years) yields corresponding
critical distances -- beyond such distances, it does not pay to build a
feeder road for the sole purpose of supporting some productive investment of
the scale specified above.
22.        Checking the economic feasibility of any project is done by
combining such critical distances. As an example, consider a 15 km road
project in the Zacapoaxtla micro-region, supporting the development of 360 ha
of rainfed corn and beans and 15 ha of avocados; 125 ha of potatoes are grown
as a second crop. The critical distance for such investments is the sum of
the following:
i. the critical distance for corn and beans development
= 3.60 x 1.7 or about 6.1 km;
ii.  the critical distance for potato growing development
= 1.25 x 10.2 or about 12.7 km;
iii. the critical distance for avocado development
= .15 x 40 = 6 km.
i.e. the total critical distance is 24.8 km and the project is acceptable.
Providing that a road is necessary to develop a block of land, directly
productive components should be identified and the critical number of km that
can be built determined. If the number of km actually programmed is larger
than this critical value, the road would be rejected along with the directly
productive investments it supports.



ANNEX 8
Page 8
23.        The method has been designed to check the effectiveness of joint
road and related directly productive investments, none of which could be
profitably undertaken independently. If the procedure is applied to cases
where the accessibility constraint is not pronounced, conservative results
should be expected.
Rural Stores
2h.        Because the rural sector is generally very poor, with limited
buying power, it is often assumed away as part of the country's commercial
market. As a consequence, marketing of consumer goods to the rural sector
has generally been a forgotten dimension of rural development. Such over-
sight ignores reality. Most campesinos do enter into the goods economy to
some extent as indicated in the introductory statistics on subsistence
farmers. Because of this, and because food expenditures probably absorb
80% of their family budget, food prices have a dramatic impact on the economic
situation of the rural family. The fixed investment ir- a rural CONASUPO
store amounts to about US$2,400 and another US$2,500 is needed for working
capital. The rural CONASUFO store in Scolasticas had sales of US$5,400 in
1973. Assuming that CONASUPO prices were 20% lower than in other stores,
campesino purchasing power in Scolasticas increased US$1,090, plus the
store manager received commissions amounting to about 4% of sales or US$200.
If one takes off depreciation at 15 years and interest on the working capital
at 10%, the return on the US$4,900 fixed investment is 19.9%. This represents
a significant direct economic benefit to the campesinos. In addition, the
store may help untie the campesino from having to sell his crop at lower
prices to store owners in exchange for merchandise; he might thereby be able
to sell to CONASUPO buying centers at higher prices, thus increasing further
his income.
