MEXICO
RURAL DEVELOPi$ENT PROJECT
COST RECOVERY AND FISCAL BURDEN
General
Government's policy on cost recovery is not only based on budgetary
criteria but takes into account the extent to which private benefits are
identifiable and beneficiaries have the capacity to repay. Thus, Government
would aim at full recovery of most directly productive investments, but repay-
ment rates would depend on the income levels of beneficiaries. Since the
benefits of this project would go to the rural poor, who will need part of
the income increase to meet their subsistence needs, 4O% of directly productive
productive  infrastructure  recovered,  but all costs of maintaining these
investments would be borne by the beneficiaries. Further, only a small part
of productive support services and about half the cost of social infrastructure
would be recovered (Tables 1 and 2). The procedures for cost recovery are set
forth below.
Investment Costs (See Table 1)
Irrigation. The Federal Water Law requires beneficiaries of irriga-
tion to repay capital investments at rates determined by SRH and SAG after
detailed study of their repayment capacity.  The contract with SRH and SAG
would commit beneficiaries to repay the original investment, and indicate the
amounts to be repaid in the third and fourth year after completion of the
infrastructure. These amounts would be revised for the fifth and subsequent
years on the basis of actual income and estimated subsistence needs, for which
the minimum wage is an indicator. The composition of beneficiaries would be
about the same as for livestock infrastructure, allowing a recovery rate of
50% SRH collects repayments from its water charges. Recovery is over 25
years without interest.
Fruit Production. CONAFRUT also enters into agreements with groups
of beneficiaries committing them to repay the original investment. However,
with about the same composition of beneficiaries as above, the recovery rate
would be about 50%.
Livestock Infrastructure. Groups of beneficiaries would be committed
in principle in their contract with SAG to repay original investments in the
on-farm commercial-scale demonstration units. However, some 30% of the expected
beneficiaries have before-project family incomes under US$375 p.a. and would
not be able to repay the investment. Another 40% of the beneficiaries with
incomes between US$375 and US$475 could repay an estimated 50%. Thus, Govern-
ment would on the average recover about 50% of this component.



ANNEX 9
Page 2
Soil and Water Conservation. The Mexican Government considers
conservation of soil and water of national interest and its policy is not
to charge beneficiaries for the initial investment. However, subsequent
maintenance of conservation works by beneficiaries is closely supervised by
SAG.
Beekeeping. INI enters into contracts with groups of beneficiaries
obliging them to repay the costs of the initial investment. Since beekeeping
provides additional cash income and has a high rate of return, a 100% recovery
is feasible.
Credit. Current terms and conditions are 3 to 12 years at 7.6%
for development credit and up to one year at 12% for seasonal production credit.
Credit would be provided through public and private banks under technical and
financial supervision by FONDO.
Productive Support. Investments in feeder roads, extension and field
demonstration. and farmer organization would not be recovered from beneficiaries
For marketing, however, OONASUPO is expected to enter amortization over 15 years
and interest at the opportunity cost of capital into its cost calculations for
purposes of setting its margins. Moreover, the community would provide the
site and a building for remodelling into a rural store.
Other Infrastructure. Government does not recover its capital invest-
ments in health, water supply, primary education and self-help projects.
However, the benefitting communities participate in these investments by
providing sites and - to the extent possible - labor and local materials. In
health and primary education, this contribution amounts on the average to 10%
of the financial cost. For self-help projects, all local labor and materials
are provided by the community; the project would only provide supervisory
power and materials that are not available locally. For electrification and
drinking water, cost recovery is arranged as follows.
ELectrification. Upon installation, beneficiaries would provide a
20% cash contribution to the investment cost of the system. Further, individual
families would pay all costs of internal household wiring, which are not included
in the project. The remaining 80% of the system's cost would be recovered
through electricity charges, which CFE sets sufficiently high to recover the
investment over 25 years without interest.
Drinking Water. Both SRH{ and SSA would require beneficiaries to
contribute at least 15% of the construction cost of the system, either in labor
and local materials or in cash. Beneficiaries' cash payments, which have been
included in the project cost, are estimated at one-fifth of their contribution,
or about 5% of the amount included in the project for water supply. Contri-
butions in kind and the costs of house connections, which are installed at the
expense of individual families, are not included in the project.
Project Administration. The costs of technical services would be
borne by Government without recovery.



ANNEX 9
Page 3
Conclusion. On the basis of the above estimates, Government would
recover US$150 million, or 50% of project cost. Apart from interest, the
fiscal burden for Government would be US$144 million.
Operation and Maintenance Costsi/(See Table 2)
Livestock Infrastructure. Under their contract with SAG, benefi-
ciaries are obligated to maintain the infrastructure according to SAG's
guidelines and under supervision of the regional SAG conservation staff.
Irrigation. Both SRH and SAG would ensure a 100% contribution
to maintenance by beneficiaries. SRH takes full responsibility for both
operation and maintenance of its systems, and recovers all costs from the
users associations through water charges. SAG leaves this to the local
water committees, which maintain their system at their own expense, under
SAG supervision.
Soil and Water Conservation. Maintenance of the conservation works
constructed under the project would be carried out by the ejido/village con-
servation association under SAG supervision. The obligation of beneficiaries
to maintain those works would be spelled out in their contract with SAG.
Beekeeping, Forestry:i Fishery. For all three components, the
groups of beneficiaries are obligated under their contract with SAG to main-
tain these investments. Maintenance would be carried out by beneficiaries
under SAG supervision.
Feeder Roads. Road maintenance is the responsibility of SOP, which
finances this from its regular budget without recovery from beneficiaries.
Extension and Field Demonstration. After the initial intensive
effort to introduce improved technical practices and to ensure proper use of
the productive investments, SAG's extension and field demonstration staff
would be reduced. Their salaries and other operating expenses would be
financed from SAG's regular budget without recovery from beneficiaries.
Fanmer Organization. Once the basic village/ejido organizations
are set up and=operating, SRAts community development brigades wrould be
reduced to an average size of two agents per micro-region. Their salaries
and other operating expenses would be financed from SRA's regular budget
without recovery.
M4arketing. I4aintenance and operating expenses, amounting to
US$3,000 per rural store and US$20,000 per rural warehouse, would be carried
out by CONASUPO. These costs would be recuperated through the prices that
CONASUPO charges beneficiaries. A start-up working capital would equally by
recovered.
/ For directly productive investments, only maintenance costs have been
estimated, since beneficiaries are fully responsible for their use.



ANNEX 9
Page 4
Health. The health posts and centers would be operated and
maintained by SSA, and their costs of US$3,000 per post and US$15,000
per center, financed from its regular budget. Medicines and dressings,
however, estimated at US40.75 per visit, would be paid by beneficiaries.
Primary Education.  The primary schools would be operated by
the Minist                 nd maintained by CAPFCE.  Their costs, amounting
to US$3,000 per 50-student classroom, would be financed from the regular
budget. However, costs of materials, such as paper and pencils, and extra-
curricular activities would be paid by the parents.
Electrification. Operation and maintenance of the electricity
network would be carried out by CFE, with full cost recover-y from benefi-
ciaries.
Drinking Water. The local water committees would operate and
maintain the water systems under SRH or SSA supervision. Their costs would
be fully covered by user charges, set in consultation between these committees
and SRH or SSA.
Self-Help Projects. The benefitting communities would operate
and maintain the facilities constructed with self-help at their own expense
and with advisory assistance by SOP.
Project Administration. The ongoing evaluation effort w-ould be
financed by Government through the regular CIDER budget.
Conclusion. The above estimates indicate that beneficiaries would
contribute US$6.5 million to operation and maintenance costs. They would
bear 100% of the cost of maintaining the directly productive investments and
contribute 30% to the operation and maintenance costs of productive support
and social infrastructure investments. The fiscal burden for Government
would be US$6.7 million p.a., or 0.6% of estimated total public recurrent
expenditures in 1980. These expenditures would provide the beneficiaries
with a level of Government services which is about average nationwide, but
still far below urban standards. For direct beneficiaries average expendi-
tures per capita would be US$10, which compares with total public recurrent
expenditures, estimated at US$85 per capita in 1980.
